Received 
      15th July 2013
    First published on 13th September 2013AbstractThis critical review is devoted to semiconducting and high spin polymers which are of great scientific interest in view of further development of the organic electronics and the emerging organic spintronic fields. Diversified synthetic strategies are discussed in detail leading to high molecular mass compounds showing appropriate redox (ionization potential (IP), electron affinity (EA)), electronic (charge carrier mobility, conductivity), optoelectronic (electroluminescence, photoconductivity) and magnetic (magnetization, ferromagnetic spin interactions) properties and used as active components of devices such as n- and p-channel field effect transistors, ambipolar light emitting transistors, light emitting diodes, photovoltaic cells, photodiodes, magnetic photoswitches, etc. Solution processing procedures developed with the goal of depositing highly ordered and oriented films of these polymers are also described. This is completed by the description of principal methods that are used for characterizing these macromolecular compounds both in solution and in the solid state. These involve various spectroscopic methods (UV-vis-NIR, UPS, pulse EPR), electrochemistry and spectroelectrochemistry, magnetic measurements (SQUID), and structural and morphological investigations (X-ray diffraction, STM, AFM). Finally, four classes of polymers are discussed in detail with special emphasis on the results obtained in the past three years: (i) high IP, (ii) high |EA|, (iii) low band gap and (iv) high spin ones.
Piotr Bujak
Piotr Bujak got his MSc in chemistry from Silesian University of Technology in 2004. In 2008, at the University of Silesia he defended his PhD thesis devoted to the NMR investigation of copolymers. Then he started working at the Department of Inorganic and Coordination Chemistry (University of Silesia), where he developed an effective method for the synthesis of new isoxazolines. In 2012 he accepted the position of a research associate at Warsaw University of Technology, joining the group of Professor Adam Pron, where he presently works on the preparation of electroactive hybrids consisting of inorganic semiconductor nanocrystals functionalized with organic semiconductors.
Irena Kulszewicz-Bajer
Irena Kulszewicz-Bajer obtained her PhD in chemistry from Warsaw University of Technology in 1984. She spent one year in the Laboratory of Inorganic Chemistry at the University of Nancy I in France at the position of “maitre de conference” in 1985. Then she returned to Warsaw University of Technology, at which she has been working till now. In 1991 she worked in Uniax Corporation in Santa Barbara (USA), collaborating with Alan J. Heeger and Paul Smith. She became full professor in 2009. Her research interests involve the synthesis and spectroscopic characterization of π-conjugated materials and high-spin organic compounds.
Malgorzata Zagorska
Malgorzata Zagorska, a University of Warsaw graduate, obtained her PhD in chemistry from Warsaw University of Technology in 1987. Then she moved to Royal Institute of Technology in Stockholm (Sweden) where she worked as postdoctoral fellow under the supervision of Goran Ahlgren and Bernd Krische. In 1992–1994 she worked in UNIAX Corporation in Santa Barbara (USA), closely collaborating with Alan J. Heeger. In 1994 she returned to Warsaw University of Technology where she became full professor in 2005. Her research interests involve the synthesis, electrochemistry and spectroelectrochemistry of organic semiconductors as well as their application in organic electronics.
Vincent Maurel
Vincent Maurel obtained his PhD in 2004 from Joseph Fourier University in Grenoble (France) working under the supervision of Serge Gambarelli and Jacques Gaillard. Then he moved to Canada working as a postdoctoral fellow in the team of Professor Tito Scaiano at the University of Ottawa. In 2006 he joined the University of Strasbourg where worked under the supervision of Philippe Turek. Since 2007, he has been working as a researcher at Atomic Energy Commission (CEA) in Grenoble. His research interests involve advanced EPR spectroscopy, molecular magnetism and photocatalysis.
Ireneusz Wielgus
Ireneusz Wielgus was born in 1969 in Warsaw (Poland). In 1993 he obtained his MSc degree in chemistry and chemical engineering from Warsaw University of Technology. In 2000 he defended his PhD thesis at the same university, devoted to conducting polymers and carried out under the supervision of Professor Malgorzata Zagorska. In 2003 he obtained the position of an assistant professor. His research interests involve the synthesis and characterization of specialty polymers.
Adam Pron
Adam Pron was born (1951) and educated in Poland. After obtaining his MSc in chemistry he moved to the USA where in 1980 he completed his PhD at the University of Pennsylvania (under the supervision of Alan G. MacDiarmid). Then he joined the Warsaw University of Technology where he became full professor in 1993. In 1998 he moved to Atomic Energy Commission (CEA) in Grenoble (France). In 2012 he retired from CEA and accepted full-time professorship at Warsaw University of Technology. His research interests involve preparation and characterization of organic electroactive materials and hybrid organic–inorganic nanomaterials.
1 Introduction
Semiconducting and conducting properties of low and high molecular mass organic compounds have been attracting some interest from various scientific communities for more than six decades, although up to the early 70s of the past century a large majority of papers devoted to this domain of research passed essentially unnoticed. Intensive interest in the chemistry, physics and materials science of organic semiconductors and metals started in 1973 after the publication of an important report on high and metallic-type conductivity of tetrathiafulvalene–tetracyanoquinodimethane (TTF–TCNQ).1 A few years later, the discovery of the highly conductive form of polyacetylene – the simplest polyene – stimulated the research directed towards the synthesis of conjugated polymers and elucidation of their electrical properties.2 Up to the early 90s of the past century the majority of published studies dealt with doped polymers, exhibiting a very high conductivity approaching that of metals. Among the plethora of these compounds, two must be mentioned: poly(3,4-ethylenedioxythiophene) abbreviated as PEDOT and polyaniline abbreviated as PANI. They are used in their oxidized (polycationic) state and by appropriate choice of the anions compensating the positive charge they can be rendered solution and thermally processable. This facilitates their blending with conventional polymers. Thus, doped PEDOT or PANI can be used as components of transparent electrodes, replacing indium tin oxide (ITO) in organic electronic devices such as light emitting diodes or photovoltaic cells,3,4 as hole transporting materials and in other applications where high conductivity and good compatibility with conventional polymers are needed.5
In this review we will not discuss doped (polycationic) conjugated polymers, frequently showing metallic behaviour,6,7 but rather focus our attention on neutral (undoped) ones which are semiconductors. The interest in polymeric semiconductors aroused in the early 90s after the publication of reports announcing successful fabrication of field effect transistors from polythiophenes,8,9 and oligothiophenes,10 light emitting diodes from poly(p-phenylene vinylene)11 and its dialkoxy-substituted derivative3 and after the introduction of the bulk heterojunction (BHJ) concept to the design of organic photovoltaic cells.12 Since that time several thousands of papers have been published on various aspects of chemistry, physics and engineering of polymers used in organic electronics.
In parallel interest in high spin (magnetic) polymers started to rise.13 They are somehow similar to electronic polymers since they contain ferromagnetic coupling (FC) units of conjugated nature which connect the spin containing (SC) units.14 Moreover magnetic polymers in which spins are present in the form of radical cations very closely resemble doped conjugated polymers, although the macromolecular topology may be different.15,16
As a consequence of this increasing interest, in the past decade several reviews have been published on electronic and magnetic π-conjugated polymers including special issues of Advanced Materials,17Chemical Society Reviews,18 and Chemical Reviews19 entirely devoted to this subject. One should be, however, aware of the fact that in the recent three years the number of reported polymeric semiconductors nearly doubled. Although some papers discussing this progress have been published20,21 a large majority of the recent review publications focus on the description of devices,22,23 their architecture,24 their functioning,25 their stability26 and their application in sensing,27etc. It therefore seems that a review paper discussing in detail the strategies for the synthesis of semiconducting and magnetic polymers, the methods of their characterization as well as the relationship between the applied preparation method and their macromolecular structure is strongly needed in view of the fast growing library of these compounds.
The organization of this review is as follows. In the first part we discuss typical synthetic pathways leading to semiconducting polymers with tunable band gap (Eg), controlled ionization potential (IP) and electron affinity (EA). The effect of the applied synthesis method on the chain microstructure and macromolecular parameters (molecular mass and its distribution) of the resulting polymers is discussed in the next part of the paper. This is followed by the description of the redox and spectroelectrochemical properties of high molecular mass semiconductors which frequently play a diagnostic role in predicting the suitability of a given polymer for electronic or spintronic applications. A short description of the processing techniques and their effect on the supramolecular organization of the polymers in thin layers is then discussed in the subsequent section. This general part is completed by the description of typical electrical transport features of electroactive polymers. In the three subsequent sections different classes of semiconducting polymers are discussed. In the last part of the paper a detailed description of the high spin polymers showing different macromolecular (molecular) topologies and different spin configurations (free radicals or radical ions) is given.
2 Preparation strategies for conjugated polymers and their basic characterization
2.1 Synthetic pathways leading to semiconducting polymers
In the early days of the research focused on electroactive polymers classical polymerization methods were used for their preparation. This involved polymerization of acetylene using either Ziegler–Natta or Luttinger catalysts to yield semiconducting polyacetylene.28 The morphology of this unprocessable polymer could be controlled by the use of liquid crystalline solvent providing a chiral nematic reaction field.29 Polyacetylene was also prepared via ring opening metathesis polymerization (ROMP) of 1,3,5,7-cyclooctatraene (COT)30 or its derivatives.31 This method was also used for the synthesis of poly(p-phenylene vinylene) (PPV) from a bicyclooctadiene derivative.32
Simple unsubstituted or β-substituted heterocyclic monomers of the thiophene, pyrrole or furan family as well as macromonomers containing terminal rings of this type can be oxidatively polymerized (chemically or electrochemically) to yield the corresponding conjugated polymers.33 Electrochemical polymerization of these monomers is limited by the surface area of the working electrode, and for this reason difficult to apply in large scale preparation. In contrast, this obstacle does not exist in the polymerization of pyrroles or thiophenes with iron(III) chloride. Oxidative polymerization of thiophene derivatives with this reagent yields polymers of a higher molecular mass, as compared to electrochemically prepared ones. In the case of asymmetric monomers like 3-alkylthiophenes, for example, the oxidative polymerization route is not regiospecific and yields irregular polymers.34 Their regioregularity degree can be improved by lowering the polymerization temperatures and the concentration of the monomer as demonstrated by Amou et al.35
Much better results were obtained in the case of alkylphenyl-substituted thiophene. Andersson et al.36 reported excellent regioselective synthesis of poly[3-(4-alkylphenyl)thiophene] through oxidative coupling polymerization of 3-(4-octylphenyl)thiophene by slow addition of FeCl3, although the regiocontrol mechanism was not clear. Haba et al.37 also reported that such regioselective oxidative coupling polymerization could be achieved by adding a catalytic amount of vanadium(IV)-oxy acetylacetonate (VO(acac)2) to the reaction mixture. These polymerization reactions, which succeeded in the case of 3-alkylphenylthiophene, failed however for 3-alkylthiophene, as mentioned above. Evidently, the bulky alkylphenyl substituent better prevents head-to-head (HH) coupling, which perturbs the regioregularity of the polymer chain.
The nickel-catalyzed Kumada catalyst transfer polycondensation (KCTP), also known as Grignard metathesis (GRIM) polymerization, turned out to be one of the most convenient ways for the preparation of almost purely head-to-tail (HT) coupled poly(3-alkylthiophenes).
Treatment of 2,5-dibromo-3-hexylthiophene with an alkyl Grignard reagent results in two metalated regioisomers via a magnesium exchange reaction. Introduction of a catalytic amount of Ni(dppp)Cl2 (dppp = 1,3-bis(diphenylphosphino)propane) into this isomeric mixture affords P3HT with regioregularity >99% (Scheme 1). The chain-growth mechanism of the NidpppCl2-initiated polymerization of 3-hexylthiophene (3HT) to yield regioregular poly(3-hexylthiophene) (P3HT) shows essential features of a quasi-living process as demonstrated by the following findings.38–42 First, the molecular mass of the resulting polymers turned out to be dependent on the monomer to catalyst ratio. In particular their number average molecular mass (Mn) increased linearly with increasing monomer/catalyst feed ratio up to the degrees of polymerization (DP) of ∼120. Second, the measured polydispersity indices (PDIs) were low, usually from 1.2 to 1.4.39,41 Third, this quasi-living polymerization could also be used for the preparation of block copolymers by sequential addition of different 3-alkylthiophene monomers.39 A detailed end-group analysis via1H NMR and MALDI-TOF (Matrix Assisted Laser Desorption and Ionization-Time of Flight mass spectrometry) showed that all P3HT chains uniformly had one hydrogen atom and one bromine atom at the end.41 The GRIM polymerization has been developing very fast and a plethora of conjugated polymers of different chain architectures have recently been prepared by this method.43–46
 Scheme 1  Synthesis of poly(3-hexylthiophene) via the Grignard metathesis (GRIM) method. 
Mechanistic studies are becoming increasingly important since the elucidation of the reaction mechanism can enrich possible applications of KCTP. The extent of the chain regularity control is certainly very high for thiophene-based monomers. Further improvements in this respect are still needed if more complex initiators or monomers are to be used. This involves problems of chain termination, transfer, and reinitiation. The most significant impact could be generated by applying KCTP to the synthesis of n-type, ambipolar, and low band-gap conjugated polymers.45
The nickel-mediated Yamamoto dehalogenation coupling reactions provide an alternative pathway for the polymerization of single monomers or macromonomers47 but they are essentially ineffective in the preparation of regioregular alternating copolymers. The Heck and Sonogashira coupling methods, while effective in this context, are limited in the selection of monomers (macromonomers).48–52
Alternating copolymers consisting of segments of different electron donating/accepting properties have become increasingly important in view of the search for new low band gap semiconductors for ambipolar transistors, photovoltaic cells and near infra-red (NIR) active photodiodes.20,33
The design of these types of polymers is based on the efficient carbon–carbon single bond formation between unsaturated carbons in two different aromatic units. In general, this process involves a transition-metal-catalyzed oxidative addition reaction across the C–X bond of an electrophile and then transmetalation with a main group organometallic nucleophile, followed by a reductive elimination step leading to the desired intermolecular carbon–carbon bond formation. The most commonly employed transition-metal catalysts are nickel- or palladium-based complexes, although other metals have been used. The organometallic nucleophiles can be, already discussed, Grignard reagents (Kumada–Corriu),53 stannyl reagents,54 boron reagents (Suzuki–Miyaura),55 or copper reagents (Sonogashira).56 Thus, the growing conjugated chain can be extended through consecutive transformations in the catalytic cycle. Another advantage is that the applied reaction conditions are generally mild and many functional groups are tolerated.
Stille and Suzuki coupling reactions, using two distinct monomers, are the most efficient and the most widely used methods for preparing alternating copolymers of donor (D)–acceptor (A) type: –(DA)–, –(DAD)– and –(ADA)–. It is noteworthy that stannyl groups-functionalized benzene exhibits low reactivity towards aryl halides under Stille coupling conditions.57 Therefore, Stille coupling is more suitable for thiophene-capped macromonomers substituted with stannyl groups, whereas Suzuki coupling is more widely used for polycondensation of benzene-capped macromonomers functionalized with boronic groups.58 In the past two decades impressive progress has been observed in Stille-type polycondensation.59 There exist however some disadvantages of the Stille-type processes. The synthesis of distannane monomers requires the use of reactive organometallic compounds, such as organolithium or Grignard reagents, which impose problems in the monomer functional group compatibility. Moreover, these difunctionalized monomers are usually difficult to purify when they are not crystallizable. Palladium catalysts are relatively expensive and palladium black formed during the polymerization, which is sometimes difficult to remove, can worsen the semiconducting properties of the resulting polymers. The heteropolycondensation may also be perturbed by homocoupling of the distannane compounds, which changes the stoichiometric balance of the monomers.59 Finally, both Stille and Suzuki couplings yield polymers of relatively low molecular mass, frequently only oligomeric fractions are obtained.
Classical reactions such as the Wittig–Horner reaction or Knoevenagel condensation are also of use in the synthesis of conjugated polymers via the carbon–carbon double bond formation between the respective monomers.60,61
In the very recent years the direct arylation polymerization (DARP) has become increasingly popular. This synthetic procedure represents an economically attractive and ecologically acceptable alternative to the traditional cross-coupling methodologies.62–65 In this method nonfunctionalized (hetero)arenes are employed directly and coupled through C–H bond cleavage. However, since different C–H bonds exhibit comparable dissociation energies, the major challenge is enabling highly regioselective direct arylations for synthetically useful transformations. The regioselectivity of direct arylation can be controlled by the electronic structure of a given arene substrate, which determines its reactivity. Several (hetero)arenes exhibit preferential reactive positions, and this feature is exploited in the highly regioselective metal-catalyzed direct functionalization of both electron-rich and electron-poor arenes. However, reactions of electronically unbiased arenes are often nonselective, yielding undesired mixtures of regioisomers, which are often difficult to separate. This is particularly critical for polycondensations where regioselectivity is of primary importance. Incorporation of an undesired segment into the conjugated backbone may significantly worsen the electronic and optoelectronic properties of the resulting product.
Wang et al.66 successfully polymerized 2-bromo-3-hexylthiophene to P3HT in quantitative yields (99%), using Herrmann's Pd-catalyst and tris(2-dimethylaminophenyl)phosphine as catalyst precursors. The obtained polymer showed a relatively high Mn value (30600 Da using polystyrene (PS) standards) and high regioregularity (98%). The same group67 reported the synthesis of aryl group-capped regioregular poly(3-hexylthiophene)s by palladium-catalyzed DARP of 2-bromo-3-hexylthiophene. A variety of aryl groups were installed at the initiated end using aryl bromides and iodides as capping agents. The polymerization proceeds via a two-stage process. In the first stage 2-bromo-3-hexylthiophene is consumed, the competitive formation of end capped Ar-P3HT and non-capped P3HT occurs. At this stage Mn increases linearly with the monomer is totally consumed. In the second stage the monomer is consumed, the resulting Ar-P3HT and P3HT are coupled with each other to afford Ar-P3HT with a higher molecular mass.
DARP is also a convenient way of preparing alternating conjugated copolymers. Lu et al.68 prepared a high molecular mass poly(tetrafluorophenylene-alt-dioctylfluorene) by this method.
Leclerc and his coworkers focused on the application of the direct heteroarylation polycondensation method for the synthesis of thieno[3,4-c]pyrrole-4,6-dione-based polymers.69 The imide group may act as an orienting and activating group for the hydrogen atoms at 2- and 2′-positions,70 and on this basis, this monomer could be a good candidate for DARP. To prove the concept, the authors69 synthesized an alternating copolymer of this monomer with 4,4′-dialkyl-2,2′-bithiophene by both Stille coupling and DARP (see Scheme 2). The copolymer obtained by Stille coupling showed inferior macromolecular parameters as compared to that synthesized by DARP, Mn being 9000 g mol−1, PDI (polydispersity index) = 1.8 and 56000 g mol−1, PDI = 2.6, respectively.69 This relatively low molecular mass might be related to the loss of some functional groups during the Stille polymerization. Lower molecular mass of the Stille polymer was also manifested by a hypsochromic shift of its π–π*transition band by 10 nm with respect to the position of this band in the spectrum of the DARP polymer (from 474 nm to 464 nm). Finally, the DARP polymer showed a higher melting point, probably also associated with its significantly higher molecular mass. This instructive example clearly demonstrates the potential of the DARP method.
 Scheme 2  Comparison of the Stille versus the direct heteroarylation polymerization (DARP) approach.69 
To recapitulate, the progress in heterocyclic chemistry enabled the synthesis of a large variety of homo- and copolymers with tunable redox, electronic and spectroscopic properties. The most popular preparation methods are collected in Scheme 3.
 Scheme 3  The most popular synthetic routes to prepare semiconducting polymers. 
2.2 Macromolecular parameters
Marcomolecular chain microstructure is of crucial importance for the electrical transport properties of semiconducting polymers. In general regioregular and, where it applies, stereoregular polymers show better electrical parameters than their counterparts with less regular chain structure. HT-coupled P3HT is here an instructive example. Lowering its regioregularity from 96% to 70% results in a decrease of its charge carrier mobility by over three orders of magnitude.71 This is caused not only by a more difficult flow of charge carriers along the polymer chain but also by less ordered supramolecular organization which makes the transport in three dimensions more difficult. Among a large number of methods used for the synthesis of semiconducting polymers only few are regiospecific, therefore nominally the same polymers may differ in their regioregularity and may show different properties.
Other parameters that should be strictly controlled for semiconducting polymers are their molecular mass and its distribution. For polymers showing the same regioregularity degree, spectroscopic, redox and electrical transport properties are strongly dependent on the molecular mass.72 Moreover, this dependence persists at surprisingly high molecular masses.73
Molecular mass of conjugated polymers and its distribution are usually measured by size exclusion chromatography (SEC), using sharp fractions of PS as standards. One should however be aware of the fact that these standards tend to overestimate the molecular mass and this overestimation strongly increases with increasing Mn. This was elegantly demonstrated by comparing Mn determined by SEC and by MALDI-TOF for fractionated regioregular P3HT.74
The majority of synthetic methods used for the preparation of macromolecular semiconductors yield polymers of high PDI. In fact crude polymers are frequently mixtures of oligomers and true high molecular fractions.75 The presence of oligomeric fractions in a given polymeric sample can have a negative impact on the quality of the solution processed thin semiconducting layers. Such studies have been carried out for oligo(dialkylterthiophenes). It has been demonstrated by scanning tunneling microscopy (STM) that strictly monodispersed oligomers, deposited on highly oriented pyrolytic graphite (HOPG) or gold monocrystals, form highly ordered monolayers via self-assembly involving π-stacking and interdigitation of alkyl chains. However, with increasing oligomer length this supramolecular organization undergoes profound changes, being transformed from two-dimensional to one-dimensional.76 Moreover, admixtures of monodispersed oligomers to true high molecular mass fractions result in a quick phase separation. It must therefore be stated that the supramolecular organization of short oligomers is not compatible with that of true polymeric fractions. As a result, for crude non-fractionated polymers, which contain oligomeric fractions, poorly ordered, amorphous layers are obtained upon casting.76 Lack of the PDI control is the main reason for large discrepancies in charge carrier mobilities measured for regioregular polymers of nominally the same Mn.73 Thus, polymers showing large values of PDI should be fractionated before any type of their use in organic electronics or optoelectronics.
2.3 Redox properties
Suitability of the use of semiconducting polymers in a given application is determined by their redox properties and more precisely by their electron affinity (EA) and ionization potential (IP). For example, polymers used as active layers in air operating n-channel field effect transistors (FETs) should be characterized by |EA| > 3.9 eV, whereas for those applied in air operating p-channel FETs IP should be higher than 5.1 eV. For ambipolar transistors both mentioned conditions must be fulfilled. Semiconductors for organic photovoltaic cells must show a narrow band gap so the IP − |EA| term should be low. Therefore polymers that are suitable for ambipolar transistors may under some circumstances be applied in bulk heterojunction solar cells. This, however does not only depend on the IP − |EA| difference but also on the absolute values of IP and EA.33
IP and EA can be determined by photoelectron spectroscopy (PES) and inverse photoelectron spectroscopy (IPES), respectively.77 However, in everyday practice electrochemical methods are frequently used for this purpose.
The determination of IP from cyclic voltammetry investigations is based on the assumption that the formal potential E0ox [E0ox = (Eox(peak) + Ered(peak))/2] of the redox couple corresponding to the oxidation of the neutral macromolecule to a radical polycation and its consecutive reduction to the neutral state is related to the molecule ionization potential. By symmetry, EA can be determined from the formal potential of the redox couple corresponding to the transformation of the neutral macromolecule into a polyradical anion and its subsequent oxidation to the neutral state: E0red [E0red = (Ered(peak) + Eox(peak))/2].
Cyclic voltammogram of low molecular mass semiconductors frequently yields reversible or quasi-reversible redox couples, for which the determination of E0 is unequivocal.78 However for thin layers of polymeric semiconductors, deposited on a platinum electrode, the cyclic voltammograms may be more complex, sometimes yielding peaks of irreversible oxidation (reduction) with no reduction (oxidation) counterpart. Fig. 1 shows a typical cyclic voltammogram of an ambipolar DA copolymer.79 Although it yields a rather clear E0red value, the determination of E0ox is more ambiguous.
 Fig. 1  Cyclic voltammogram of a thin film of poly[(5,5′′′-dioctyl-[2,2′;5′,2′′;5′′,2′′′]quaterthiophene)-alt-(2,7-fluoren-9-one)] deposited on an ITO electrode. Electrolyte 0.1 M Bu4NPF6 solution in acetonitrile; Ag/Ag+ reference electrode; scan rate 20 mV s−1 [reproduced by permission from R. Demadrille, N. Delbosc, Y. Kervella, M. Firon, R. De Bettignies, M. Billon, P. Rannou and A. Pron, J. Mater. Chem., 2007, 17, 4661]. 
Very frequently peaks of the oxidation to radical cations and the reduction to radical anions become irreversible and the E0 values cannot be determined. In this case for the calculation of IP the potential of the first oxidation peak onset is taken whereas for the EA calculation – the onset of the first reduction peak.
When determining the EA and IP values one must express these potentials in the absolute potential scale i.e. with respect to the vacuum level. Assuming a value of −5.1 V for the half-wave potential of the Fc/Fc+ couple in non-aqueous electrolytes, IP and EA can be calculated using eqn (1) and (2): IP(eV) = |e|(Eox(onset) + 5.1)(1) EA(eV) = − |e|(Ered(onset) + 5.1)(2)
The value of −5.1 V is taken from the paper of Trasatti.80 One should be however aware of the fact that in the majority of publications devoted to low and high molecular mass organic semiconductors a value of −4.8 V is attributed to the Fc/Fc+ couple. This value is not recommended. Detailed justification for the preferable use of −5.1 V can be found in ref. 81. EA and IP determined electrochemically are frequently termed HOMO and LUMO levels, respectively. This is not rigorously correct since the molecular orbital energies are not observables but originate from quantum chemical approximations. The difference IP – |EA| is, in turn, called “the electrochemical band gap”. In the case of polymers with pronounced electron accepting character it is sometimes difficult to determine electrochemically their IP. Similarly, for strongly electron donating polymers electrochemical determination of EA may constitute a real problem. In these cases EA is sometimes calculated by adding the optical gap energy to IP whereas IP – by subtracting the optical gap energy from EA and changing the sign. Such approach is allowed only under the condition that the HOMO–LUMO transition has a sufficient oscillator strength to be optically detected. Sometimes this is not the case, especially for molecules (macromolecules) for which the HOMO and LUMO orbitals are separated in space.82,83
Differences between the experimental conditions of the UPS and CV measurements of IP and EA should be pointed out. In the former, a thin solid layer of the semiconducting polymer deposited on a metallic substrate like gold in ultra high vacuum is measured in situ. In electrochemical measurements the studied polymeric layer, covering the working electrode is in contact with the electrolyte solution. Low molecular mass semiconductors are frequently studied in the dissolved state, being an additional component of the electrolytic medium.
The final verification of the adequacy of the electrochemical determination of IP and EA is furnished by a very good correlation between the “electrochemically” determined values and those obtained from the photoelectron spectroscopy.84,85
Some polymers give a rather unclear response in a cyclic voltammetry study. In this case spectroelectrochemistry can be of help in determining EA and IP. The spectroelectrochemical method is based on the experimental finding that the onset of the oxidation to a radical polycation or the reduction to a radical polyanion induces profound changes in the UV-vis-NIR and Raman spectra, the latter due to changing resonant conditions. By raising (lowering) the working electrode potential in very small increments with simultaneous registration of the spectrum, it is possible to precisely determine the potential of the oxidation (reduction) onset.86,87
2.4 Structural organization and processing techniques
Appropriate redox properties of a given organic semiconductor constitute only a “conditio sine qua non” of its potential applications in organic electronic devices. In the majority of semiconducting polymers, their electrical transport properties are strongly dependent not only on their chain regularity and molecular mass, as discussed in Sections 2.1 and 2.2, but also on the type of the supramolecular organization which they adopt upon processing. This organization may frequently be considered as the decisive factor in determining the charge carrier mobility. Although, for some high molecular mass semiconductors like poly(triarylamine)s, reasonable charge carrier mobilities are measured for thin amorphous layers,88 in the case of macromolecules consisting of a stiff conjugated backbone and flexible, solubilizing side groups of alkyl- or alkoxy-type, the electrical transport properties depend on the crystallinity index and the degree of macromolecules orientation.33 These polymers have a tendency to form ordered supramolecular aggregates through π-stacking and interdigitation of the flexible side substituents, as schematically depicted in Fig. 2. Such a structure gives rise to three characteristic Bragg reflections in the diffractograms of thin layers: an intensive reflection at low angles which corresponds to a distance between the π-stacks of conjugated polymer chains separated by lateral groups. This distance is determined by the length of the solubilizing groups and the degree of their interdigitation. For the polymers described in this review it varies between 1.5 nm and 2.8 nm. In the case of lateral groups of different lengths, coexisting in the same repeating unit, it is imposed by the longest substituent.87 The second less intensive reflection is attributed to the distance between π-stacked parallel conjugated chains which, for the polymers described here, varies from 0.36 nm to 0.42 nm. Usually, the charge carrier mobilities are higher for structured polymers in which the π-stacking distance is inferior to 0.38 nm. The third feature is a broader halo whose maximum corresponds to 0.44 nm – the alkyl interchain correlation distance. The described structural arrangement is remarkably robust. It can be found not only in homopolymers like P3HT but also in the majority of conjugated polymers containing a long lateral solubilizing group which are isomorphic with this structure.33
 Fig. 2  Possible structural arrangement of a semiconducting polymer, consisting of a stiff conjugated backbone and flexible lateral solubilizing groups, with respect to the substrate. 
Based on the available crystallographic data no precise information can be gained concerning the exact ordering along the polymer chain as well as the exact chain stacking pattern. It can, however, be postulated that these two factors may influence the charge carrier mobility.
In real devices semiconducting polymers are used in the form of thin layers, usually deposited from solution. Taking into account strong anisotropy of these compounds, the orientation of the macromolecules in the layer becomes of crucial importance. This problem can be illustrated using a typical top gate-bottom contact field effect transistor or a bottom gate-bottom contact one, schematically shown in Fig. 3.
 Fig. 3  Thin film field effect transistors (FETs) of different configurations. 
In the first case (top gate-bottom contact), the source and drain gold electrodes are usually patterned on a polyester substrate and then the semiconductor layer is deposited on them by one of the usually used solution processing technique. The orientation of the macromolecules on the substrate/semiconductor interface determines the direction of π-stacking with respect to the substrate surface. If macromolecules lie flat on the surface of the substrate, the π-stacking direction is perpendicular to the substrate (see Fig. 2a). If they adopt the edge-on position (Fig. 2b), the π-stacking direction is parallel to the substrate/semiconductor interface. Evidently, different electrical transport properties are expected for these two orientations.
Frequently, in FETs an inorganic dielectric is used, for example a SiO2 layer grown on the surface of the gate in the bottom gate-bottom contact configuration. In this case, again, the flat (face-on) and edge-on positions can be adopted. Macromolecules of semiconducting polymers usually adopt the latter position on SiO2 and polyester substrates being either perpendicular or slightly tilted with respect to the plane of the interface. The flat position (Fig. 2a) is rare and is usually found on layers deposited on the HOPG surface, where π interactions between the macromolecule and the substrate are very strong.
In devices where oriented crystalline aggregation is needed like in bulk heterojunction-type solar cells, the formation of highly crystalline polymer nanofibers, through special processing, greatly improves the measured device parameters.89
All these examples discussed above underline the importance of the supramolecular organization control through the selection of an appropriate processing method. There are several processing techniques used for the deposition of thin polymeric layers from solution: dip coating, spin coating, dip casting, zone casting, directional solidification, etc. The last two are especially suitable for the fabrication of oriented layers.
The zone casting method was developed more than 30 years ago for the preparation of thin layers of oriented polymers on large surfaces.90 Upon development of organic electronics it has been used for depositing both low and high molecular mass semiconductors.91–93 In the zone casting procedure a solution of a given polymer is dispensed on a moving substrate through a nozzle. By controlling the substrate speed and its temperature as well as the solution concentration, temperature and dispensing rate, it is possible to induce the orientation of the macromolecules near the drying zone.
Oriented films of semiconducting polymers can also be obtained by directional solidification.94 In this case a crystallizable solvent is used, for example 1,3,5-trichlorobenzene (TCB) for P3HT. Cooling of a solution of this polymer in TBC on a special hot bar induces first directional crystallization of the solvent along the temperature gradient with the formation of large TBC crystals of crystallographically well defined surfaces. These crystals coexist with the remaining P3HT solution whose concentration increases with ongoing crystallization of TBC crystals. Above a given concentration of the polymer, its deposition on the surface of TBC crystals occurs with the formation of an oriented polymer layer, showing improved crystallinity. Crystalline TBC is then removed by sublimation.
A modification of this method involves the deposition of P3HT on the crystallographically well defined surface of potassium 4-bromobenzoate (K-BrBz), followed by the polymer melting and its subsequent cooling to the temperature of isothermal, epitaxial crystallization of P3HT on the K-BrBz surface. This is followed by rapid cooling of the system to RT. The K-BrBz substrate is then removed by dissolution.95
Another widely used epitaxial processing technique, leading to oriented polymer films, is the epitaxy on the friction transferred poly(tetrafluoroethylene) (PTFE) substrate. This method consists of fabrication of an oriented PTFE substrate by sliding a PTFE rod against a glass slide. A thin polymer film is then deposited on this substrate, usually by doctor-blading. It subsequently undergoes thermal cycling involving quick melting and slow constant rate cooling. The described procedure was proven to be extremely efficient in preparing oriented films of semiconducting polymers.96,97
Epitaxial solidification methods are interesting processing techniques, however difficult to apply on large surfaces. The orientation of macromolecules in thin films of semiconducting polymers deposited by classical casting or spin coating can greatly be improved by mechanical rubbing.98
Uniaxial alignment of macromolecules in the solid state is greatly facilitated for polymers showing lyotropic liquid crystallinity. In these cases the alignment can be induced by an applied flow field. By optimizing this method thin polymeric layers, showing high orientation in grazing-incidence X-ray diffraction investigations as well as a high dichroic ratio, were obtained.99
2.5 Electrical transport properties
Metals, semiconductors and insulators are characterized by different values of conductivity. The conductivity, σ, is defined as the ratio of the current density J to the magnitude of the electric field and usually expressed in [S cm−1] or [S m−1] (eqn (3)): J = σE(3)
Room temperature conductivity varies from 106 S cm−1 for the best conductors like silver or copper to 10−21 S cm−1 for the most insulating materials such as PTFE. Doped conjugated polymers such as polycationic form of PEDOT or PANI reach conductivities of the order of 102–103 S cm−1. Conductivity of these highly conductive materials has to be measured by the 4-probe method which eliminates the contact resistance.
When discussing neutral conjugated polymers which are semiconductors, in the context of their application in organic electronic devices, it is better to use mobility as a parameter describing their electrical properties. The relationship between the conductivity and the charge carrier mobility is given by eqn (4): σ = neeμe + nheμh [cm2 V−1 s−1](4)where μe and μh are the mobilities of electrons and holes, ne and nh – their number densities and e is the elementary charge.
In conjugated polymers usually one type of charge carriers is present or the two different charge carriers significantly differ in their mobilities. In such cases one of the two terms of eqn (4) can be neglected. Conjugated polymers reach the mobilities of a few cm2 V−1 s−1, much less than inorganic semiconductors. Mobility is one of the key parameters that determine the efficacy of electronic and optoelectronic devices and its highest values are desirable which can be reached only by the synthesis of highly regular polymers with good macromolecular parameters and optimized supramolecular organization.
There exist several techniques of measuring charge carriers transport in organic semiconductors such as space-charge-limited current (SCLC), time of flight (TOF), carrier extraction by linearly increasing voltage (CELIV), photogenerated charges in CELIV (photo-CELIV), double injection (DoI) and impedance spectroscopy (IS). We will not discuss them here since they are excellently reviewed in recent review papers.100,101 Here, we will limit ourselves to a brief description of the determination of charge carrier mobility from the characteristics of a field effect transistor (FET). This practical approach has recently become very popular.
The two popular FET configurations are schematically depicted in Fig. 3. In this three-electrode device the source and the drain electrodes form an ohmic contact with the conducting channel. The source electrode is grounded and all other voltages are given in relation to this electrode. Charge carriers are injected from the source electrode and collected at the drain one. The semiconducting polymer layer is separated from the gate electrode by a thin dielectric layer (either polymeric or inorganic). The gate electrode controls the current in the active layer of the organic semiconductor by capacitive coupling of the dielectric layer.
There are two principal characteristics describing functioning of a given transistor: the output and the transfer characteristics (Fig. 4).
 Fig. 4  Typical output (a) and transfer (b) characteristics of a polymeric field effect transistor. 
The output characteristics are a set of plots of the drain–source current (IDS) versus the drain voltage (VDS) for varying gate voltages (VGS). For output characteristics two regions can be distinguished: the linear one and the saturated one (see Fig. 4a). For the transfer characteristics IDS is plotted as a function of VGS for a constant value of VDS. In the saturation region (VDS > VGS − VT, where VT is the threshold voltage defined in Fig. 4b) of the output curve the IDS can be expressed as follows (eqn (5)): (5)where W and L are the width and length of the conduction channel, respectively; μ is the charge carrier mobility, CS is the capacitance per unit area of the dielectric layer and VT is the threshold voltage. For the calculation of μ in the saturation regime, the slope of the transfer characteristics IDS1/2vs. VGS (VDS = constant) can be used.
One should be careful in comparing the mobility values obtained by different methods (for example from TOF and FET measurements) since the charge generation methods are different (field effect and photogeneration, respectively) as well as the configurations of the measurement. However, they play an important predictive role in selecting new semiconductors suitable for fabrication of devices. In the description of the electrical properties of semiconducting polymers, discussed in the subsequent sections, we always indicate by which method the charge carrier mobility was obtained.
3 Polymeric semiconductors with n-type conductivity
From the point of view of their electrical transport properties, semiconducting polymers can be classified as electron (n-type semiconductors) or hole transporting (p-type semiconductors) or ambipolar materials according to the type of orderly transferring charge carriers, under a given set of conditions.102
Charge transport in conjugated polymers is a series of successive charge transfers between their neutral and charged repeat units. In n-type semiconductors this process involves the transfer of an electron from the half occupied LUMO level of the radical anion segment to the empty LUMO level of the adjacent neutral segment. As clearly stated in Section 2.3, these polymers should exhibit a low lying LUMO level and as a consequence a high value of |EA|. These are rather harsh requirements for synthetic and polymer chemists and for this reason n-type polymeric semiconductors are scarcer than the p-channel ones.33 However, over the past two decades, and especially in the last few years, some progress has been made in developing solution processable n-type polymers, largely due to the improvement in the synthetic techniques yielding high molecular mass semiconductors, in which a delicate balance between their redox properties, adequate solubility and appropriate supramolecular organization in the solid state has been established.103–106
Two general methods of solubilizing stiff backbone polymers are usually applied. The non-covalent route involves complexation of the macromolecules with an appropriate Lewis acid or protonation with a functionalized Brønsted acid, provided that appropriate complexation or protonation sites are present in their repeat units. In the covalent route flexible, alkyl- or alkoxy-type type side groups are grafted to the polymer backbone. The introduced lateral substituents should not, however, perturb the π-stacking of the stiff polymer backbones in the solid state. This π-stacking is of crucial importance for the transport properties since strong electronic coupling between the π-systems on adjacent macromolecules promotes high electron mobility. Air stability under operating conditions is another key parameter for the application of n-type organic semiconductors. They are usually stable in air in the neutral state but upon injection of electrons from the electrode some of their parts are temporarily transformed into radical polyanions, which are prone to irreversibly react with H2O or O2 in air. It is experimentally established that the semiconductor showing |EA| > 3.9 eV does not degrade under air operating conditions.107
A large majority of n-type polymeric semiconductors have been prepared through functionalization of rylene constituents such as naphthalene-1,8:4,5-tetracarboxylic bisimide and perylene-3,4:11,12-tetracarboxylic bisimide.108 Interest in rylene bisimides and other aromatic bisimides has its origin in long dated observations that for this family of compounds redox, electrical transport and processing properties can be precisely tuned by the size of the conjugated core and by variation of the substituents on the imide nitrogen atoms or on the rylene skeleton. Rylene bisimides can exhibit relatively high electron affinities, high electron mobilities, and excellent chemical, thermal, and photochemical stabilities.108
Poly(benzobisimidazobenzophenanthroline) (n3.1) (Fig. 5) was first synthesized via condensation of naphthalene tetracarboxylic acid and tetraminobenzene.109 This ladder-type polymer can be solubilized by complexation or protonation. It was used for the fabrication of active layers in the first n-channel polymer FET showing high electron mobility.110 Due to the very strong intermolecular π–π stacking of the rigid ladder polymer main chain, n3.1 exhibits high glass transition temperature Tg (>500 °C). It undergoes a reversible reduction to a radical polyanion state (n-type doping), and its |EA| = 4.0 eV. Organic field effect transistors (OFETs) based on thin layers of n3.1, spin-coated from methanesulfonic acid, showed an electron mobility (μe) of 0.03–0.1 cm2 V−1 s−1 and the Ion/Ioff ratio of 5 × 105, after annealing at 105 °C (Table 1). Electron diffraction data indicated that thin films of n3.1 cast from methanesulfonic acid were semicrystalline. Briseno et al.111 obtained highly oriented nanobelts of n3.1 by injecting a solution of this polymer in methanesulfonic acid into a mixture of methanol and water which resulted in nanophase separation. In these nanobelts, the polymer chains are oriented parallel to the nanobelt axis (i.e., face-to-face, perpendicular to the nanobelt axis) with an unusually short stacking distance of 0.336 nm. This unique packing differs from that observed in other conjugated polymers, for example in P3HT nanowires, where the polymer backbone packs face-to-face along the nanowire direction.73 The unique packing in n3.1 nanobelts arises from the extraordinary strength of intermolecular contacts, which is a result of the rigid and planar molecular backbone of this polymer. N-channel FETs fabricated from the nanobelts showed electron mobilities of (3–7) × 10−3 cm2 V−1 s−1 and current on/off ratios of ∼1 × 104 (Table 1).111 Ambipolar transistors were also fabricated from blends of n3.1 and p-type semiconductor polymers, namely poly[(thiophene-2,5-diyl)-alt-(2,3-diheptylquinoxaline-5,8-diyl)] and poly[(10-hexylphenoxazine-3,7-diyl)-alt-(3-hexyl-2,5-thiophene)]112 or from mixtures of n3.1 with copper phthalocyanine.113
 Fig. 5  Polymers used in n-channel transistors.

More recently, n3.1 was functionalized by grafting poly(ethylene oxide) chains to render it water dispersible. Cyclic voltammograms recorded for a drop cast layer of this functionalized polymer showed that it retained its reversible reduction (n-doping) properties.114
Poly(1,10-phenanthroline-3,8-diyl) and poly(3-nonyl-1,10-phenanthroline-3,8-diyl) show no semiconducting properties when uncomplexed. Upon complexation (1:1) with RuII(bpy)2 (n3.2A and n3.2B) (Fig. 5) they can be applied as semiconductors in n-channel FETs115 showing electron mobilities of 1.9 × 10−3 cm2 V−1 s−1 and 5.5 × 10−3 cm2 V−1 s−1 for n3.2A and n3.2B, respectively (Table 1).115
Alternating copolymers of arylene bisimide and bithiophene i.e. poly{[N,N′-bis(2-octyldodecyl)-1,4,5,8-naphthalenedicarboximide-2,6-diyl]-alt-5,5′-(2,2′-bithiophene)}, n3.3 and poly{[N,N′-bis(2-octyldodecyl)-3,4,9,10-perylenedicarboximide-1,7-diyl]-alt-5,5′-(2,2′-bithiophene)}, n3.4 (Fig. 5), were synthesized via a Pd-catalyzed Stille polymerization.116,117 For n3.3 Chen et al.118 reported an electron mobility of 0.06 cm2 V−1 s−1 (Table 1) and reasonable operating stability. Four weeks after fabrication μe dropped to 0.01 cm2 V−1 s−1. Surprisingly, this value was 30 times higher than the carrier mobility measured for its perylene bisimide analogue (n3.4), processed and measured under identical conditions. This difference can be rationalized by better regioregularity of n3.3. During the synthesis of perylenebisimide dibromide, which is one of the reagents for the preparation of n3.4, two regioisomers are formed, namely 1,6- and 1,7-dibromo, which are difficult to separate. Even a small amount of the undesired regioisomer in the reaction mixture lowers the regioregularity of the resulting polymer.
An improvement in μe (values of 0.1–0.85 cm2 V−1 s−1 with on/off ratios > 106) measured for n3.3 in the FET configuration was later reported by Yan et al.119 for transistors operating under ambient conditions. n3.3 seems to be much more promising for technological applications than its perylene analogue n3.4.118 It is available as a commercial product of Polyera (ActiveInk™ N2200). The characteristics of OFETs with active layers of n3.3 are strongly dependent on the processing conditions. In particular strictly orthogonal dielectric solvents must be used at each deposition step, with special emphasis on the deposition of the dielectric layer. Both spin-coated and inkjet-printed n3.3-based FETs exhibited reasonably high values of field-effect mobility of 0.1–0.3 cm2 V−1 s−1 (Table 1) with poly(methyl methacrylate) as the gate dielectric when carefully selected orthogonal solvents were used for processing.120 The value of μe decreased by one order of magnitude if a solvent showing worse orthogonality (e.g. 1,2-dichloroethane) was used. The value of μe decreased dramatically, to 0.005 cm2 V−1 s−1, with poly(4-vinyl phenol) (PVP) as the dielectric material, despite the fact that the processing was carried out with a perfectly orthogonal solvent (1-butanol). This drop was rationalized by electron trapping by the hydroxyl groups in PVP. X-ray scattering experiments reveal that n3.3 exhibits an exceptional degree of in-plane ordering and adopts a largely face-on packing.121 These excellent in-plane transport properties can be obtained with a face-on packing texture, which is not a common crystallographic feature for n-type high field effect mobility semiconducting polymers. However, it is similar to the case of p-type semiconductors, where the best transport properties are attained for a high degree of in-plane π-stacking of the conjugated chains of soluble polythiophene derivatives, facilitating two-dimensional (2D) transport in the plane of the substrate.122–124 The implications of the high performance face-on packing in the layers of n3.3 are important for further development of related n-type and ambipolar materials. In plane π-stacking is important for FET applications in configurations depicted in Fig. 2 where the electron transport is limited to a very thin layer at the substrate/semiconductor or semiconductor/dielectric interface and the in-plane stacking plays a dominant role. For other applications like in light emitting diodes (LEDs) or photovoltaic cells (PC) bulk electron mobility is equally important. Steyrleuthner et al.125 used the TOF technique to determine this parameter, demonstrating that layers of n3.3 exhibit also one of the highest TOF mobility values reported for polymeric semiconductors.
Rivnay and coworkers126 demonstrated that the supramolecular organization in thin films of n3.3 can be modified by post-deposition processing. When spin-coated films are heated to melting and then controllably cooled down, the orientation of lamellae changes from predominantly face-on orientation to almost 100% edge-on one. At the same time a 2 fold increase in the crystallinity index is observed. Evidently, this post-deposition processing moves the polymer phase closer to the thermodynamic equilibrium. The observed drastic change of the texture does not influence the FET configuration mobility. This means that the structural modifications either do not occur at the semiconductor/dielectric interface or they are not the limiting factor for the in-plane electrical transport. In contrast in the electron-only diode a six-fold decrease in the current density was observed, showing the importance of the texture effects in the bulk electrical transport.
Brinkmann et al.97 used two different processing techniques to fabricate highly oriented films, namely on a crystallizable solvent (1,3,5-trichlorobenzene in this case) and epitaxy on friction transferred PTFE substrates (see Section 2.4). Both methods lead to highly ordered, oriented supramolecular organization, however with different π-stacking patterns. Directional epitaxial crystallization results in the formation of a polymorphic structure in which segregated stacking of naphthalene bisimide and bithiophene is observed. Both segments form separate stacking columns. Epitaxial growth on friction transferred PTFE yields a different type polymorph, in which a shift between successive π-stacked chains occurs leading to mixed stacking in which bithiothene segments π-overlap with bisimide ones.
Solutions of n3.3 in different solvents were studied by Steyrleuthner et al.127 in view of optimizing the process of thin films casting. Six different solvents were tested: chloronaphthalene, toluene, chloroform and three different chloroderivatives of benzene. It turned out that only the first one, whose solubility parameter is closest to that of the polymer, yields the true solutions. In the remaining solvents aggregates are formed even at low concentrations. The aggregation process occurs via an intermediate state in which initial aggregates (type 1) are replaced by thermodynamically more stable ones (type 2). Since both types of aggregates exhibit distinctly different spectroscopic features (both in absorption and in emission spectra), it is possible to quantitatively differentiate them. In solid films type 2 aggregates dominate. It is possible to obtain films containing type 1 aggregates by using a mixed solvent consisting of chloronaphthalene (good solvent with high boiling point) and chlorobenzene (poor solvent with low boiling point). However, upon annealing of the film type 1 agglomerates are gradually transformed into more stable type 2 ones.
Durban et al. synthesized a series of three alternating copolymers of naphthalene bisimide and either thiophene or bithiophene or terthiophene (n3.5A, n3.3 and n3.5B) (Fig. 5).128 The effect of the length of the thiophene segment on the redox, structural and electronic properties is clearly observed. The IP and |EA| values decrease with the number of thiophene units, which means that both HOMO and LUMO levels are being raised. Taking into account only the redox properties, n3.5A should be the best candidate for the application in n-channel FETs. In reality, the opposite is observed, since in identically fabricated transistors, the lowest μe and the highest threshold voltage are measured for the n3.5A-based one (Table 1). This can be rationalized by differences in the structural organization of the polymer thin layers. The lamellar ordering improves with increasing length of the thiophene segment, facilitating in this manner the transport of charge carriers. One should also comment on a lower μe reported for n3.3 in this paper and the values given in ref. 119 and 120. This is caused by the fact that Durban et al. chose the bottom gate-top contact configuration for their transistors whereas in the above quoted papers top gate-bottom contact transistors are described. The value of μe reported by Durban128 is only slightly lower than that given in ref. 118 whose authors tested transistors of the same configuration (BGTC).
Recently Hwang and coworkers129 synthesized the biselenophene analogue of n3.3i.e. poly{[N,N′-bis(2-decyltetradecyl)-naphthalene-1,4,5,8-bis(dicarboximide)-2,6-diyl]-alt-5,5′-(2,2′-biselenophene)}, n3.6, (Fig. 5) by Stille coupling polymerization of N,N-bis(2-decyltetradecyl)-2,6-dibromo naphthalene-1,4,5,8-bis(dicarboximide) and 5,5′-bis(trimethylstannyl)-2,2′-biselenophene in the presence of Pd2(dba)3 and P(o-tolyl)3 in chlorobenzene. This new n-type semiconductor showed a highly ordered structural organization of lamellar type in thin layers. As a consequence its μe measured for transistors in the BGTC configuration (0.07 cm2 V−1 s−1) (Table 1) was comparable to that reported for n3.3 for the same configuration.118
Stille coupling was also used for the synthesis of an alternating copolymer containing perylene bisimide and a fused thiophene unit, namely dithienothiophene n3.7 (Fig. 5). In this synthesis N-dialkyl-1,7-dibromo-3,4,9,10-perylene bisimide was reacted with distannylated dithienothiophene.130,131 The resulting, solution processable polymer n3.7 showed μe as high as 1.3 × 10−2 cm2 V−1 s−1 a low threshold voltage of ca. 4.4 V in the bottom-gate OFET configuration (Table 1).130 Air operating, top-gate OFETs, with a polyacrylonitrile (PAN)/PS dielectric layer, exhibited a μe value of 6 × 10−2 cm2 V−1 s−1, Ion/Ioff 104 and a threshold voltage of 7 V in air (Table 1).132n3.7 differs from n3.4 in its thiophene unit (Fig. 5). Replacing bithiophene by fused dithienothiophene stiffens the polymer chain and promotes π-stacking and as a consequence better supramolecular organization can be achieved. Not surprisingly, in a similar device geometry, n3.7 shows better electrical transport properties than n3.4.118

n3.8 (Fig. 5) i.e. an alternating copolymer of perylenebisimide and N-n-dodecyldithienopyrrole differs from n3.7 by the presence of an N-alkylpyrrole ring in the fused heterocyclic unit. It was synthesized by palladium-catalyzed coupling of dibromo-derivatives of perylene bisimide with N-n-dodecyldithienopyrrole.131 Incorporation of the pyrrole moiety into the polymer backbone raises the polymer HOMO level which is manifested by a decrease of its IP by 0.4 eV as compared to n3.7 (Table 1). This can be considered as a clear manifestation of stronger electron-donating properties of the pyrrole ring as compared to the thiophene one. The EA value remains unaffected by this change. Significantly lower electron mobilities, μe, were measured for n3.8 than for n3.7 in FETs of the same configuration. This can be tentatively attributed to lower Mn of n3.7 (Table 1). The strong effect of molecular mass on FET mobility was reported for several semiconducting polymers.72a–c,73,127
Letizia et al.133 reported the synthesis of an interesting polymer in which the electron withdrawing imide group was integrated directly into the conjugated poly(bithiophene) main chain. This poly(N-alkyl-2,2′-bithiophene-3,3′-dicarboximide) n3.9 depicted in Fig. 5 was obtained by Yamamoto polycondensation of the corresponding dibromo-functionalized monomer.134 Films of n3.9 deposited on a SiO2 surface, which was rendered hydrophobic through the treatment with hexamethyldisilazane (HMDS), after annealing are highly oriented and show a high degree of structural ordering. In their diffractograms a set of Bragg reflections, originating from the same family (up to 5th order) and corresponding to a d-spacing of 2.51 nm, can be observed. If no interdigitation of alkyl groups is assumed, this spacing imposes a tilt angle of the macromolecule with respect to the substrate of 70°. Partial interdigitation would increase the value of this angle. In earlier studies, mobilities of ca. 0.01 cm2 V−1 s−1 were measured for n3.9 in the FET configuration which was remarkably independent of the film-deposition conditions (Table 1). More recent studies concentrated on the effect of Mn on the morphology and electrical transport properties of n3.9.135 It was found that both the crystallinity of the polymer thin layers and μe increased with the molecular mass. Through device optimization using top-gate/bottom-contact OFET architecture, it was possible to fabricate n3.9-based FETs exhibiting a high electron mobility of ∼0.2 cm2 V−1 s−1.135
Alternating copolymers of n3.9 and monothiophene, bithiophene and quaterthiophene were also reported.135 Their electrochemically determined |EA| and IP decrease with increasing number of the thiophene rings. This means that both HOMO and LUMO are being raised as a consequence of increasing electron donating effect of the thiophene unit. As a consequence their ambipolar electrical properties become increasingly pronounced and they exhibit field effect in both n-channel and p-channel configurations. Copolymers of N-alkyl-2,2′-bithiophene-3,3′-dicarboximide and oligodialkylbithiophenes in thin layers showed ordered supramolecular organization similar to that of poly(alkylthiophene) where π-stacked conjugated chains are separated by interdigitating alkyl substituents.
Diaryl derivatives of diketopyrrolopyrrole (DPP) constitute convenient building blocks for the preparation of semiconducting alternating copolymers with tunable n-type and p-type conducting properties. Over 70 semiconducting polymers of this family have been synthesized to date. We will not discuss them in detail since an excellent review on this subject has recently appeared.136 We will rather describe the strategy used in the design of such polymers using a few instructive examples.
The repeat unit of an alternating conjugated copolymer containing the dithienyl DPP unit can be depicted as shown in Scheme 4.
 Scheme 4  General formula of alternating conjugated polymers containing dithienyl diketopyrrolopyrrole (DT DPP) building blocks. 
The redox properties of the resulting copolymer and as a consequence its capability of conducting holes or electrons in a given configuration are determined by a delicate balance of electron donating/accepting properties of DT DPP and the π building blocks. If in the macromolecule DT DPP alternates with a strongly accepting unit like for example bis-benzothiadiazole (BisBT) (n3.10 in Fig. 5), the resulting polymer has predominantly n-type character with μe significantly higher than μh.137 Replacing BisBT with dialkylbithiophene i.e. lowering of the electron accepting ability of the π-segment leads to an almost purely ambipolar transistor with well balanced μe and μh of 0.1 cm2 V−1 s−1.138 If the π-segment is strongly electron donating, like N-alkyl-2,7-carbazole, the semiconducting polymer is purely p-channel in behaviour showing a μh of 0.02 cm2 V−1 s−1.139
Ambipolarity is a technologically interesting feature and ambipolar organic semiconductors have gained much attention because of their potential use in complementary circuits and light-emitting field-effect transistors (LFETs),140,141 including those emitting in the infrared.138 In particular, there is significant interest in the application of ambipolar polymers in FETs, because of their capability of hole and electron transport within the same material, which can simplify the circuit design and offers processing simplicity and flexibility to fabricate low cost, flexible, and portable devices.142–144 Moreover, some ambipolar semiconductors can also be used as components of bulk heterojunction solar cells because of their low band gap. This embraces, among others, polymers such as poly(diketopyrrolopyrrole-dithieno[3,2-b]thiophene) or its copolymer with thiophene,145 poly(diketopyrrolopyrrole-alt-terthiophene)146 or a terpolymer consisting of diketopyrrolopyrrole, 1,4-phenylene and bithiophene units.147
Polymers containing oligothiophene and indenofluorenebis(dicyanovinylene) segments also exhibit ambipolar behavior with comparable hole and electron mobilities. This proves that electron withdrawing properties of the cyano units are sufficiently strong to assure n-type semiconductor behavior, despite the fact that they are separated by quaterthiophene segments. Usta et al.148 discussed the synthetic strategy used for the preparation of such polymers and reported several derivatives of indenofluorenebis(dicyanovinylene) prepared either by Stille or by Yamamoto coupling whose electrical and structural properties could be tuned. For semiconductors with low-lying LUMO level (high |EA|) (n3.11, Fig. 5), these authors148 observed air-stable n-type behavior with electron mobilities of up to 0.16 cm2 V−1 s−1 and exceptionally high on/off ratios 107–108 (Table 1).148
Stecker et al. reported a new soluble, spray-processable, donor–acceptor polymer n3.12 (Fig. 5), which was synthesized by C–C coupling polymerization of the strong donor, N-(3,4,5-tris(dodecyloxy)phenyl)dithieno[3,2-b:2′,3′-d]pyrrole, with a strong acceptor 4,8-di(2-thienyl)benzo[1,2-c;4,5-c′]bis[1,2,5]thiadiazole via Stille methodology.57,149n3.12 shows a very low optical band gap of 0.5–0.6 eV. These values suggest the possibility of facile injection of both holes and electrons from commonly used electrodes, and therefore of ambipolar charge transport. Ambipolar OFETs based on n3.12 exhibit hole and electron mobilities of 1.2 × 10−3 and 5.8 × 10−4 cm2 V−1 s−1, respectively.149
An interesting, and distinctly different than those presented above, approach to the preparation of n-type semiconductors was proposed by Hüttner et al.150 who obtained a saturated main chain polymer with perylene bisimide pendant groups, namely poly(perylene bisimide acrylate) (n3.13, Mn = 30900 g mol−1, PDI = 1.86) (Fig. 5). Since the pendant groups were connected together by a flexible main chain, their strong π-interactions were not impeded. As a result side groups crystallization occurred,151 which was manifested by a clear polymer melting point at 190 °C. As spun films yielded rather poor electron mobilities in the bottom gate FET configuration. They could be, however, greatly improved by over 2 orders of magnitude by annealing at 210 °C, i.e. above the melting temperature of the side groups (Table 1). These results suggest that after annealing the perylene bisimide side groups become highly structured at the dielectric/active layer interface forming an efficient n-channel.
Interestingly, very similar results were obtained for a diblock copolymer of poly(perylene bisimide acrylate) and PS (70:30%; Mn = 37900 g mol−1; PDI = 1.52). The main reason for the improvement in the charge carrier transport after annealing lies in the microphase separation, observed only in the annealed layers. Cylindrical domains of poly(perylene bisimide acrylate) blocks are formed within the polystyrene phase and they are preferentially located at the interface with the dielectric. This phenomenon is widely known and originates from the strong surface interaction with one of the blocks.152
These two types of semiconducting polymers, described here, are more similar to low molecular mass arylene bisimides rather than to their high molecular mass analogues. They differ from the low molecular compounds only by restricted mobility due to their grafting to a saturated polymer chain.
Only a fraction of polymers which are capable of transporting electrons show field effect in the n-channel FET configuration. Other applications of these polymeric materials involve organic light emitting diodes (OLEDs), where they can serve as electron transporting layers.
The role of electron transporting polymers in OLEDs can be briefly outlined as follows. In these devices electrical energy is transformed into light in the so called “electroluminescence effect” involving injection of electrons and holes from the respective electrodes, formation of excitons and their annihilation with simultaneous emission of light. Balanced charge injection and transport is one of the key factors determining appropriate functioning of these optoelectronic devices. The majority of electroluminescent materials, used for the fabrication of active layers in OLEDs, are hole conductors (p-type conductors). This facilitates the injection of holes and their transport within the layer. However, the injection barrier for electrons is in this case very high and the electron mobility within the layer is low. As a consequence of this charge injection and charge transport imbalance, the processes of exciton formation and annihilation occur in close vicinity of the cathode and are very inefficient because of the excitons quenching on the surface of the metallic electrode. To avoid these problems it is advisable to separate the emissive layer from the electrode by an additional layer which facilitates the injection and transport of electrons and at the same time blocks the transport of holes to the cathode, shifting the formation and annihilation of excitons far from the cathode. Some n-type semiconductors are very well suited for this purpose. In this review we will discuss them only briefly, since an excellent detailed paper on this subject has recently appeared.105
Polymers containing electron-deficient 1,3,4-oxadiazole groups either in the main conjugated chain or in side chains being in conjugation with the main chain are promising candidates for application in electronic devices.153–156 However, they do not show field effect or their electron mobilities measured in the FET configuration are very low.157 Moreover, initially prepared polymers of this type were not soluble in typical organic solvents and their solution processing required the use of strong acids (sulfuric acid or methanesulfuric acid), which is technologically inconvenient.158 To induce the solubility of polyoxadiazole derivatives in organic solvents, flexible tetraphenylsilane or hexafluoroisopropylidene units were introduced into the main chain.159 Another strategy for the improvement of their processability followed the classical approach of attaching solubilizing side chains such as long alkyl or alkoxy groups to the polymer main chain.160
Electron transporting polymers containing oxadiazole units are usually amorphous and show high Tg. These good thermal parameters prompt their use as additional layers which do not only improve electron injection and transport and, as a consequence the external quantum efficiency (EQE), but also extend operating lifetime of the fabricated OLEDs.156,159–163
Other thermally stable organic semiconductors which show high |EA| and for these reasons are suitable for use as electron transporting layers in OLEDs (and sometimes emissive layers) embrace polymers containing such electron deficient units like pyridine,164 quinoline,165 1,3,5-triazine,166 benzobisthiazole,167 benzobisoxazole167a,c and benzothiadiazole.168
To summarize, conjugated polymers of high |EA| find application in organic electronic devices as active layers in n-channel FETs, electron transporting layers or emissive layers in LEDs, the latter in combination with an appropriate hole transporting layer. Finally, in bulk heterojunction-type organic photovoltaic cells (OPC) some of these electron transporting polymers can be used as acceptors which facilitate exciton dissociation at the interface with an appropriate donor polymer.
4 Polymeric semiconductors with p-type conductivity
Polymers which can be used as semiconductors transporting holes find a variety of applications in organic electronics: as active layers in p-channel FETs,33 components of bulk heterojunctions in PCs,20 as emissive layers in LEDs,169 and as components of photodiodes (PDs),170 to name the most important. Given the plethora of such polymers we have selected the most representative examples. Their chemical structures are presented in Fig. 6–8 and 10–23.
 Fig. 6  Chemical structures of copolymers containing thiophene units. 
 Fig. 7  Chemical structures of copolymers containing thienothiophene units.

 Fig. 10  Chemical structures of copolymers containing bithiazole units.

 Fig. 12  Chemical structures of copolymers containing dithienopyrrole or silylene-2,2′-bithiophene units. 
 Fig. 13  Chemical structures of the copolymers p4.50 and p4.51. 
 Fig. 14  Chemical structures of copolymers containing fluorenone units. 
 Fig. 15  Chemical structures of copolymers containing thieno[3,4-c]pyrrole-4,6-dione units. 
 Fig. 16  Chemical structures of copolymers containing 2,5-dihydropyrrolo[3,4-c]pyrrole-1,4-dione.
