Hole carrier mobility measured for thin layers of p4.73 in the FET configuration reached 0.20 cm2 V−1 s−1 (Table 3) with no signs of deterioration after the device storage in air (56% relative humidity) for 5 months. p4.73 was also tested as a component of BHJ PC with PC71BM, yielding devices with a power conversion efficiency of 6.26%.302
 Scheme 24  Synthetic route to prepare p4.73. 
Naphtho[1,2-c:5,6-c′]bis[1,2,5]thiadiazole consisting of two fused benzothiadiazole units was first reported by Mataka et al.303 Recently, Osaka et al.304 have published a detailed paper in which they compared p4.74 and p4.75 – the former being a copolymer of benzothiadiazole with dialkylquaterthiophene whereas the latter naphthobisthiadiazole with dialkylquaterthiophene. The synthesis of p4.75 is depicted in Scheme 25. In their principal hypothesis the authors assumed that the replacement of a smaller rigid electron accepting unit by its double extended analogue would not only increase its IP value but also would improve the π-stacking, facilitating in this manner the formation of ordered supramolecular organization in thin layers. Indeed, extension of the electron accepting unit led to a slight increase of the IP value by 0.05 eV (to 5.20 eV) in p4.75. An excellent agreement between the “photoelectron” and “electrochemical” IPs, reported in this paper should be pointed out. As expected the optical and electrochemical band gaps of p4.75 were also lower. In line with the predictions of the authors, thin layers of p4.75 exhibited much better structural order of lamellar type than p4.74 with a short π-stacking distance of 0.35 nm. In the difractograms of p4.74 the reflection ascribed to π-stacking was hardly detectable. Superior redox and structural properties of p4.75 were clearly manifested in its performance in FETs and BHT PCs. Hole carrier mobilities measured for thin layers of p4.75 in FET configuration reached 0.56 cm2 V−1 s−1 whereas for p4.74 they were 8 times lower. Similarly, maximum PCE values obtained for BHJ PCs made of p4.75 reached 6.3%, in p4.74 they never exceeded 2.6%.304
 Scheme 25  Synthetic route to prepare p4.75. 
Polymers containing carbazole units have been studied for decades as photoconductors or organic semiconductors.305 There are several advantages of using carbazole derivatives as building blocks in semiconducting polymers: (i) 9H-carbazole is a fully aromatic unit providing good chemical stability; (ii) bridging its phenyl group prevents it from rotation, therefore carbazole containing polymers show better conjugation and lower band gaps than their p-phenylene analogues; (iii) N-functionalization of carbazoles, which is relatively easy, allows us to tune the solution processability as well as electronic and optoelectronic properties of the resulting polymers.305,306
In the polymer chain the carbazole unit can be either 3,6-coupled or 2,7-coupled (see Fig. 20).
Polymers and copolymers of 3,6-coupled carbazole derivatives have been tested for many years, however much more promising polymers containing 2,7-coupled carbazole units started to be synthesized much later.305,307 More difficult synthesis of polymers with 2,7-carbazole units is caused by the fact that in the carbazole moiety 3,6- and 1,8-positions are highly activated because the electron-rich nature of carbazole inhibits direct and specific aromatic substitution at the 2,7-position. Indirect approaches have to be used in this case. They usually use a 4,4′-functionalized biphenyl unit bearing an activated group at the 2(2′) positions which undergoes ring-closing reaction, yielding 2,7-functionalized carbazoles, but other approaches are also possible (see Scheme 26).
 Scheme 26  Possible synthesis of 2,7-substituted carbazole. 
Quick progress in the preparation of semiconducting polymers with 2,7-carbazole units was made possible by exploiting advances in metal-catalyzed polycondensation.308 The synthesis of various 2,7-carbazole containing building blocks was optimized: (2,7-dichloro,308 2,7-ditriflate,309 and 2,7-dibromo).310 Yamamoto,47,311 Stille,57,312 or Suzuki313 coupling polymerization was used in the preparation of alternating copolymers consisting of 2,7-carbazoles and other conjugated units.314 Restriction imply to Yamamoto copolymerization which can be applied to the preparation of random copolymers only. In Stille copolymerization significant difficulties in the synthesis of pure organotin derivatives can be encountered. Suzuki polycondensation seems to be so far the most versatile method for the preparation of polymers of this family.313 Based on methodologies reported for the preparation of poly(2,7-fluorene)s,315 efficient syntheses of 2,7-diboronic ester derivatives of carbazole were developed from halogen–lithium exchange and palladium catalyzed borylation.309,316
In the early stages of research on poly(2,7-carbazole)s photo- and electroluminescence properties were of primary interest, especially in view of the fact that many of these compounds were blue emitters.308,317 Several solution processable poly(2,7-carbazoles) with tunable optoelectronic properties were prepared by N-substitution with alkyl307a,308,317,318 or aryl307,319 groups. Copolymers with specifically designed comonomers were also prepared to be tested as components of light emitting diodes and other optoelectronic devices.307b,319c,320

p4.76 and p4.77 are N-substituted poly(2,7-carbazole) homopolymers307a whereas p4.78 and p4.79 are their alternating copolymers with bithiophene. They were prepared either via Yamamoto coupling of the appropriate dibromoderivative (p4.76 and p4.77) or by Stille coupling of the same derivative with distannylated bithiophene (p4.78 and p4.79) (Scheme 27).
 Scheme 27  Syntheses of p4.76–p4.79. 
These solution processable polymers showed IP values in the range of 5.2 to 5.3 eV, indicating their resistance against oxidation under air operating conditions.321 They differed, however, in their electrochemical properties: p4.78 and p4.79 could be quasi-reversibly oxidized and reduced whereas for p4.76 and p4.77 a non-reversible oxidation was observed, as in other homopolymers of poly(2,7-carbazole).309p4.76 and p4.77 exhibited rather poor performance as components of active layers in p-channel FETs (Table 3), probably as a result of limited conjugation and not sufficiently ordered supramolecular organization. Hole carrier mobility in thin layers of p4.78, measured in the FET configuration after annealing reached a moderate value of 2.0 × 10−3 cm2 V−1 s−1 (Table 3). Consistent with the increased IP values, hole mobilities of p4.78 and p4.79 remained essentially unchanged after one month of their exposure to an ambient atmosphere.

p4.80 – a hyperbranched homopolymer of carbazole, obtained in one pot Suzuki coupling – was reported by Li et al.322 Its FET hole mobility was rather low 1 × 10−5 cm2 V−1 s−1 (Table 3), however its optoelectronic properties were more interesting. p4.80-based LEDs exhibited better efficiency (3.05 cd A−1) than those fabricated from standard poly(N-vinylcarbazole) (2.19 cd A−1). This improvement was attributed to lower energy barrier for charge carrier injection and higher hole-transporting ability of p4.80 layers.322
Low molecular mass indolo[3,2-b]carbazoles are known semiconductors exhibiting good hole carrier mobilities in the FET configuration.323 In the corresponding polymers the indolo[3,2-b]carbazole units can be coupled either at C3/C9 or C2/C8 positions to give poly(indolo[3,2-b]carbazole-3,9-diyl) (p4.81) or poly(indolo[3,2-b]carbazole-2,8-diyl) (p4.82), respectively. p4.81 has an extended π-conjugation along its chain, resembling that of poly(p-arylene)s, while in p4.82 the conjugation is restricted as in polyarylamines.

p4.81A–B and p82A–B were synthesized via a dehalogenative coupling of dihaloindolo[3,2-b]carbazole monomers (Scheme 28).224 The monomers i.e. 3,9-dichloroindole[3,2-b]carbazole and 2,8-dichloroindolo[3,2-b]carbazole were prepared respectively by condensation of 3- and 4-chlorophenylhydrazine with 1,4-cyclohexanedione, followed by double Fischer indolization (Scheme 28). p82C–E were synthesized using an alternative method, namely by regioselective oxidative (FeCl3) coupling of indolo[3,2-b]carbazole. Regioselectivity of this reaction is enhanced because of a strong activation of C2/C8 positions which are para located with respect to the amino functions (Scheme 29).224 Both preparation methods led to rather low molecular masses.
 Scheme 28  Synthesis of monomers and their polymerization to p4.81 and p4.82via dehalogenative coupling. 
 Scheme 29  Synthesis of p4.82 through oxidative coupling. 
Exhibiting sufficiently high IP values in the range of 5.1 to 5.2 eV p4.81A and p4.82 seem to be good candidates for the fabrication of active layers in p-channel FETs. Unfortunately, p4.81A and p4.81B showed rather poor film forming properties and the hole mobilities in the resulting FETs were low (Table 3). Better results were obtained for the p4.82, especially for those polymers which were obtained by oxidative coupling. For p4.82E hole carrier mobilities of 0.02 cm2 V−1 s−1 were measured (Table 3). It was postulated that better performance of p4.82 as compared to p4.81 is not only caused by morphological differences but also by better resonance stabilization of the ammonium radical cations formed in the charge carrier transport process.323b
Further modification of the properties of semiconducting polymers containing the carbazole moiety can be achieved by designing appropriate heteroacene units. In designing such polymers it should be remembered that unsubstituted poly(heteroacene)s are insoluble or very hard to solubilize. Long alkyl chains have to be attached to the conjugated chain so as to overbalance strong intermolecular interactions. The second role of the alkyl substituents is to promote appropriately ordered supramolecular organization, usually by the interdigitation. Thus, both the chemical constitution of the polymer and the geometry of its chain have to be carefully designed so as to achieve the desired goal.242,304,324
Chen et al.325 synthesized dithieno[2,3-b;7,6-b]carbazole, with a central N-substituted carbazole unit and two thiophene rings fused at the ends. This unit was then used in the copolymerization with dialkylbithiophenes via Stille coupling, to yield p4.83. The synthetic pathway is depicted in Scheme 30. Polymers of the p4.83 series are on the borderline of air operating stability since their IP values (IP < 5.0 eV) are lower than those of poly(carbazoles), poly(indolocarbazoles) and their copolymers with oligothiophenes (vide supra). As cast polymers show poor supramolecular structure which can be improved by annealing to give a lamellar-type organization. Hole mobility in thin layers of the p4.83 series increases with an increase of the alkyl substituent length reaching 0.035 cm2 V−1 s−1 for p4.83C (Table 3) owing it to slightly improved supramolecular structure caused by more efficient interdigitation.325
 Scheme 30  Synthetic pathway leading to p4.83. 
Very similar polymers, namely poly(dithieno[3,2-b;6,7-b]carbazole-alt-dialkylbithiophene)s (p4.84), have more recently been reported.326 The synthesis of the heteroacene monomers as well as their Stille coupling with the dibromoderivative of bithiophene to yield p4.84 are presented in Scheme 31.326 After annealing, both p4.84A and p4.84B showed improved lamellar-type supramolecular organization with the π-stacking distance of 0.37 nm. Annealed layers showed hole mobilities of 0.2 cm2 V−1 s−1 and 0.39 cm2 V−1 s−1, for p4.84A and p4.84B, respectively.326
 Scheme 31  Synthetic pathway leading to p4.84. 
Low molecular mass arylamines and the corresponding polymers and copolymers have been extensively studied as promising materials for active layers in FETs, hole transporting layers in LEDs, photoconducting layers, etc.327 They are characterised by a low ionisation potential (close or inferior to 5.0 eV) and can be reversibly oxidised to generate stable radical cations.328 The latter property is exploited not only in organic electronics but also in the design of polymeric high spin magnetic materials (see Section 6 of this review). With few exceptions poly(arylamine) derivatives do not crystallize, forming so called “molecular glasses”.33 This is associated with nonplanarity of the macromolecules which makes the crystallization difficult. Due to their amorphous structure, less problems are encountered in the reproducibility of films morphologies, so simple solution processing techniques can be used for the film deposition. Moreover, these materials combine amorphous structure with reasonably high hole carrier mobility of the order 10−2 cm2 V−1 s−1.329
In the past decade a great improvement has been observed in the preparation of arylamine building blocks and the resulting polymers. Classically, triarylamines are obtained by amination of aryl halides.330 Progress in the design of the amination catalysts, based on palladium, copper or nickel complexes, led to significant lowering of the reaction temperatures and improvement in the yield of the desired product.331 Still, the applied procedures remain very demanding. The preparation of well-defined, high molecular mass polytriarylamines by the condensation of aniline with a dihalobenzene is an instructive example here. Since this is a step-growth reaction it requires exact stoichiometry and very high conversion of the reacting functional groups to obtain a high molecular mass product with good yields. Conversions >0.95 are necessary to prepare polymers suitable for use in organic electronics.329a
Microwave-assisted methods were successfully applied to accelerate the rate of transition metal catalysed C–C and C–N coupling reactions, in some cases also improving the conversion and the selectivity of the reaction.332 Microwave irradiation was exploited not only in the preparation of the arylamine building blocks but also in the condensation polymerizations, where its action significantly accelerated the reaction rate and minimized the occurrence of side reactions.333 Synthesis of polytriarylamine from 2,4-dimethylaniline and 4,4′-dibromobiphenyl using microwave-assisted, palladium-catalysed amination is an instructive example of this effect. The reaction times could be reduced to five minutes at 100 °C. The depolymerization of the resulting polymer could be avoided in this manner which occurred at longer reaction times.334
Alternating copolymers of aryl amine and 2,9-fluorene (p4.85), 3,6-carbazole (p4.86) and 4,4′-biphenyl (p4.87) were prepared by a microwave-assisted palladium-catalyzed cross-coupling reaction between a primary arylamine and an aryldibromide (Scheme 32).335 The IP values of all three polymers were 4.5 eV for p4.85 and p4.86 and 4.8 eV for p4.87i.e. much lower than those reported for polyflourene, polycarbazole and poly(p-phenylene) homopolymers336 and carbazole (IP = 5.3 eV) units.337 This is a clear consequence of the presence of electron donating, easily oxidizable amine group in the polymer main chain. All three polymers were tested as active layers in FETs. The best performance was observed in the case of p4.85A, for which the FET hole mobility of 2.4 × 10−3 cm2 V−1 s−1 was measured under ambient conditions (Table 3).335
 Scheme 32  Synthetic route to prepare p4.85, p4.86 and p4.87. 
Electrical transport properties of the poly(triarylamine) homopolymer (p4.88) and alternating copolymers of triarylamine with fluorene (p4.89) as well as indenofluorene (p4.90) were compared by Zhang et al.338 All three polymers were synthesized by Suzuki coupling polycondensation. p4.88 was prepared by homocondensation of N-(4-bromophenyl)-4-(4,4,5,5-tetramethyl-1,3,2-dioxaborolan-2-yl)-N-(2,4-dimethylphenyl)benzenamine, whereas both copolymers (p4.89 and p4.90) were obtained by heterocondensation of dibromotriarylamine and pinacol ester boromates of fluorene or indenofluorene. It was found that the FET mobility of hole carriers increases in the following order p4.88 < p4.89 < p4.90, reaching 0.04 cm2 V−1 s−1 for p4.90 (Table 3).338 It seems that the introduction of bridged aromatic units improves the planarity and the persistence length in the copolymers, resulting in better intramolecular π-orbital overlap and improving the local structural organization.
An interesting paper correlating the chemical structure of a given arylamine copolymer with its hole transport properties has recently been published.339 The investigation was carried out for copolymers depicted in Scheme 33 which were synthesized using Suzuki-coupling340 and showed high molecular masses in the range 200–600 kDa with PDI in the range 2.8–4.0. IP values of the polymers of the investigated series were strongly dependent on the number of amine nitrogens in the repeating unit (see p4.91, p4.92, p4.93 and p4.94 in Scheme 33) and could be varied from 4.86 eV to 5.40 eV.
 Scheme 33  Chemical structures of copolymers used in a comparative study of electrical transport properties. 
Time of flight (TOF) mobilities were measured under identical conditions for each polymer. Surprisingly, an inverse exponential relationship was found between μh and IP. Since the mobility value depends not only on the redox properties of a given conjugated polymer but also on such factors as structural and energetic disorder, polaronic effects, and wave function localization, the authors determined the contribution of all these factors to the measured mobility using complementary methods such as absorption and emission spectroscopy and DFT calculations. In conclusion it was postulated that disorder effects and hole localization, which are more pronounced in polymers with lower IP, were the principal factors that determined the mobility values.
To summarize this part of the review, the number of solution processable polymers which show good hole mobilities and for this reason can be used as active layers in FETs, hole transporting layers in LEDs or donor components of BHJ PCs by far exceeds the number of polymers exhibiting high electron mobilities. Polymer chain engineering, which involves appropriate geometrical arrangement of main chain conjugated segments as well as the positions of the solubilizing substituents, is the most important factor determining the redox and electrical properties of the resulting polymers. These properties can be tuned in a relatively large range by the synthesis of copolymers containing units with balanced donor–acceptor interactions. Finally, in these polymers an efficient p-type transport can be achieved not only in highly structured thin films of lamellar-type organization but also, in some cases, in amorphous layers.
5 Low band gap semiconductors
Low band gap semiconducting polymers of D–A type have been recently very intensively studied as components of bulk heterojunction photovoltaic cells, photodiodes or field effect transistors, including ambipolar ones.341 The active layer in a typical BHJ PC consists of a donor i.e. a conjugated polymer capable of absorbing light in the visible and NIR parts of the spectrum and an appropriate acceptor which can be fullerene derivatives, inorganic nanocrystals or a strongly electron deficient conjugated polymer.276,342 All components should be solution processable and form two co-continuous networks of large interface so as to facilitate the formation of charge carriers through the dissociation of excitons at this interface.171b,341a,e,342a,343
Conjugated homopolymers such as regioregular P3HT can be used as donor components of BHJ providing power efficiencies of 3–5%,344 under the condition, however, that the morphology of the polymer network in the BHJ favors charge transport.89 Further progress in PCs containing P3HT is intrinsically limited by poor matching of its absorption spectrum and the solar spectrum. In addition its IP is too low. The same applies to solution processable poly(p-phenylene vinylene) (PPV) derivatives, namely poly[3-methoxy-5-(2′-ethyl-hexyloxy)-1,4-phenylene vinylene] (MEH-PPV) and poly[2-methoxy-5-(3′,7′-dimethyloctyloxy)-1,4-phenylene vinylene] (MDMO-PPV) where even lower energy conversion efficiencies were obtained.344,345
Even quick literature search clearly shows several advantages of donor–acceptor alternating copolymers in this respect. First, due to narrower gaps, as compared to the corresponding homopolymers, their absorption spectrum is shifted towards NIR, which is favorable for the solar radiation harvesting. Moreover, in the spectra of DA polymers an additional intramolecular charge transfer absorption band frequently appears at longer wavelengths, which additionally broadens the spectrum and makes the conversion of photons to charge carriers more efficient. Second, the positions of the HOMO and LUMO levels in these polymers and as a consequence their IP and |EA| can be precisely tuned in a wide range and optimally adapted to a given acceptor so as to achieve satisfactory open circuit voltage Voc.
Although the design and synthesis of DA polymers is presently the most popular direction of constructing new high molecular mass semiconductors for photovoltaics,346 other approaches are also worth mentioning: (i) extension of the π system,171h,223,347 polythiophene containing conjugated bi(thienylenevinylene) side groups shows a narrower band gap than P3HT and better power conversion efficiency in devices processed under the same conditions; (ii) stabilization of the quinoidal structures, this approach was initiated by Wudl et al.348 nearly 30 years ago who prepared poly(isothianaphthene) – the first low band gap conjugated polymer. Many other low band polymers were prepared using this approach. A mixed approach was also applied, in which the stabilization of the quinoid structure was combined with DA copolymerization to yield poly(thieno[3,4-b]thiophene-benzodithiophene) exhibiting a narrow band gap (1.6 eV).349 Photovoltaic cells with this polymer as a donor component of the BHJ and PC71BM as an acceptor exhibited a power conversion efficiency of up to 7.4%.349b
Two methods discussed above are efficient in tuning the band gaps but they inevitably lead to raising the HOMO level (decreasing the IP value). This may have a negative effect on the open circuit voltage (Voc). In the approach involving DA copolymerization this problem is easier to avoid.
In DA-type conjugated polymers the electron rich, donor units are most frequently constructed from bridged dibenzene or dithiophene moieties. Another class of donors involves heteroacenes, for example fused benzene and thiophene units. The effect of bridging is very pronounced in terms of conjugation since the presence of the bridge impedes the torsion of the adjacent rings and fixes in this manner the planarity of these building blocks. Fluorene, dibenzosilole and carbazole are representative examples of dibenzene-based donor units. Because of the electron deficient nature of benzene which weakens the electron donating effect of the bridging group these building blocks are classified as weak donors (Scheme 34). Their presence in the corresponding DA copolymers leads to moderate gap narrowing, which limits their performance in photovoltaic cells, although it can have a beneficial effect on Voc.
 Scheme 34  Typical electron donating units used in the design of donor–acceptor conjugated polymers. 
In the bridged dithiophene series the following donors can be distinguished: cyclopentadithiophene (CPDT), dithienosilole (DTS), dithieno[3,2-b:2′,3′-d]germole (DTG) and dithieno[3,2-b:2′,3′-d]pyrrole (DTP). Compared to the bridged dibenzene donors, in the corresponding DA copolymers orbitals of this type of donors better mix with those of the acceptor which results in a better planarity of the polymer chain. Taking into account their pronounced effect on the optical band lowering and diminishing of the oxidation potential in the DA polymers they can be classified as strong donors (Scheme 34).
Fused dithienobenzene units, frequently containing two solubilizing alkyl groups can be considered as medium donors where strong electron donating properties of alkylthiophene rings are weakened by the presence of the electron deficient benzene group (Scheme 34).
In general, the band widths of DA copolymers, containing the same A unit, can be correlated with the strength of the donor. This is illustrated in Scheme 35 where – IP and EA values are compared for a series of dithienopyrrolopyrrole copolymers with different donors. It is clear that the change in the donor has a more pronounced effect on IP (or HOMO) than on EA (or LUMO).
 Scheme 35  Effect of the electron donating strength on IP and EA of diketopyrrolopyrrole copolymers containing different donors. Data taken from ref. 283d and 283f. 
Electron-deficient acceptor units used for designing DA conjugated polymers usually contain imine nitrogen (benzothiadiazole (BT), quinoxaline (QA), thienopyrazine (TP), bithiazole (BTz), thiazolothiazole (TTz), benzobisthiazole (BBTz), benzotriazole (BTA), s-tetrazine (STTz), naphtho[1,2-c:5,6-c]bis[1,2,5]thiadiazole (NT)) or a carbonyl group (CO): diketopyrrolopyrrole (DPP), [2,3-c]thiophene-4,9-dione (NTDO), isoindigo (II) and thieno[3,4-c]pyrrole-4,6-dione (TPD), fluorenone (Scheme 36). Similarly to donors, acceptors can be classified as weak, medium and strong. In DA copolymers the electron accepting strength affects mainly EA (or LUMO) although in the series of copolymers of a strong donor dithienosilole (DTS) with acceptors of varying strength, both changes in IP (or HOMO) and in EA (or LUMO) contribute to band gap narrowing as clearly seen in Scheme 37.
 Scheme 36  Typical electron accepting units used in the design of donor–acceptor conjugated polymers. 
 Scheme 37  Effect of the electron accepting strength on EA and IP of dithienosilole (DTS) copolymers containing different acceptors. Data taken from ref. 269, 283d and 350. 
In general, the following combinations weak donor–weak acceptor, medium donor–weak acceptor, and weak donor–medium acceptor yield alternating copolymers band gaps of which are too wide for efficient photovoltaic applications. Such applications require the combination of strong donor–strong acceptor or strong donor–medium acceptor or medium donor–strong acceptor. Similar rules apply to ambipolar FET applications, however ambipolarity in DA copolymers is more rare than it should be expected based on the IP and EA values.
Copolymers containing donors such as thienothiophene and cyclopentadithiophene and acceptors such as isoindigo, diketopyrrolopyrrole, thiazolothiazole, and benzothiazole were found to yield p-channel transistors with high hole mobilities. In these polymers strong intermolecular interactions are induced by the large dipole moment between the donor and the acceptor units. Among the most promising polymers, high molecular mass poly(cyclopentadithiophene-co-benzothiadiazole) should be cited, exhibiting the FET mobility of hole carriers of up to 5.5 cm2 V−1 s−1.351 Already mentioned diketopyrrololopyrrole-copolymers with various donors reached an FET mobility of up to 2.2 cm2 V−1 s−1.145,352 Recently, an even higher hole mobility of 8 cm2 V−1 s−1 was reported for a copolymer of dithienyldiketopyrrolopyrrole and dithienylvinylene.353 Most recently studied DA copolymers of isoindigo can also be considered as a promising group of semiconductors for p-channel FETs, for example Lei et al.324b reported a hole mobility of 1.06 cm2 V−1 s−1 for a copolymer of isoindigo and bithiophene.
Recent progress in heterocyclic chemistry makes possible to design and synthesize copolymers with sequences more complicated than DA sequences, for example: (D–A1–D–A2–D) or (A–D1–A–D2–A). This approach, extremely useful for fine tuning the redox, electronic and optoelectronic properties of the resulting polymers, was applied for the construction of periodic copolymers exhibiting ambipolar behavior, which consisted of diketopyrrolopyrrole and benzothiadiazole as acceptors and thiophene or selenophene as a donor. Well balanced hole and electron FET mobilities were measured for these polymers.354
In order to demonstrate the peculiar properties of DA conjugated copolymers we will discuss four series of copolymers. In each series the acceptor unit is common and combined with different donor units of varying electron donating strength. The selected A units are: benzobisthiazole (weak acceptor), thiazolothiazole (medium acceptor), thienopyrroledione (medium acceptor) and benzothiadiazole (strong acceptor).
The first series of copolymers can be obtained either by Stille or Suzuki couplings. In the Stille condensation two routes are possible: (i) the reaction of the dibromo derivative of dithienylbenzobisthiazole with the distannyl derivative of the donor or inversely (ii) the reaction of the distannyl derivative of dithienylbenzobisthiazole with the dibromo derivative of the donor. The application of Suzuki coupling is also possible.260,350b All copolymers of this series (l5.1–l5.6) (see Fig. 24) show in solution one broad absorption band in the 330–650 nm range, assigned to the intramolecular charge transfer band. A significant red-shift in the absorption maximum is observed as the donor comonomer is varied from dithienobenzene (l5.2) to dithienopyrrole (l5.6), which can be interpreted as originating from increasing intramolecular charge transfer strength with increasing donor strength.355 The electron donating unit affects mainly the IP value (or in other words the energy of the HOMO level which is related to IP) which decreases from 5.71 eV (l5.1) to 4.79 (l5.6). In contrast |EA| (∼3.3 eV) remains essentially independent of the nature of the donor unit (Table 4). Since Voc in BHJ PCs is inversely related to IP of the polymer used in combination with PC71BM, test cells have been fabricated to verify whether the expected trend is reflected by the changes in the open circuit voltage Voc. This is indeed the case, Voc decreases from 0.81 V to 0.31 V when going from l5.1 to l5.6. Changes in power conversion efficiencies are more complex, but this parameter is strongly morphology dependent and should be treated with caution.350b
 Fig. 24  Chemical structures of copolymers containing benzobisthiazole acceptor units.

From the point of view of their application in p-channel FETs, l5.1 suffers from ineffective hole injection caused by too high IP whereas in the case of l5.6 low on/off ratios are expected because of its low IP value which leads to weak resistance against oxidative doping (see Section 4). Indeed copolymers with high IP values (l5.1 and l5.6) show inferior FET parameters, the best ones being measured for l5.2 (Table 5).

383b





As already stated thiazolothiazole can be considered as a medium strength electron acceptor. Stille coupling of the distannyl derivative of dialkylthienylthiazolothiazole with a precursor introducing a strong donor unit (dibromo derivative of dithienosilole)356 yields the copolymers of the l5.7–l5.10 series (Fig. 25).350a,357 Alternatively they can also be prepared by Suzuki coupling.357 The available literature data allow for the elucidation of the effect of topology and chemical nature of the solubilizing substituent in the thiophene ring on the redox and optical properties of the resulting polymer. First, a reduction of the optical band gap is observed as a result of the electron-donating properties of dithienosilole.356 A comparison of l5.7A, l5.7C and l5.7D which contain the hexyl, octyl and 2-ethylhexyl substituent, respectively, shows that this band gap narrowing is essentially independent of the topology of the alkyl substituent since optical gaps of all polymers are similar (1.81 eV, 1.79 eV and 1.82 eV). The same is observed for the IP values which are smaller than those of the homopolymer and very close to each other (5.06 eV, 5.10 eV and 5.10 eV). Surprisingly, shifting of the substituent from the position 3 to 4 slightly decreases the optical band gap to 1.76 eV and IP to 5.04 eV, however these changes are minimal and on the borderline of the measurement error. Replacing alkyl substituents by more electron-donating alkoxy ones changes the DA balance which is manifested by further lowering of the optical band gap and the IP values to 1.73 eV and 5.0 eV, respectively.
 Fig. 25  Chemical structures of copolymers containing thiazolothiazole acceptor units. 

l5.7B and l5.7E show reasonable hole carrier mobilities measured in the FET configuration of 0.17 cm2 V−1 s−1 and 0.078 cm2 V−1 s−1, respectively. For other polymers of this group lower hole mobilities are measured. The described copolymers are promising materials for BHJ PCs yielding devices with a PCE of 5.88% in the case of l5.7E. Interestingly, the l5.7B-based devices exhibited the lowest PCE (2.10%), despite the narrowest gap of this copolymer. However, PCE is a delicate parameter, depending on many factors which are not easy to control.

l5.8
i.e. poly[N-9′′-heptadecanyl-2,7-carbazole-alt-2,5-bis(3-hexylthiophene-2-yl)thiazolo[5,4-d]thiazole] (Fig. 25) is a DA copolymer containing the same acceptor as those of the l5.7 group but a weaker donor, namely N-alkyl carbazole. It was synthesized through a Suzuki coupling of diboronato-derivative of carbazole and the dibromo-derivative of dialkylthienylthiazolothiazole.358 As expected both the optical band gap (2.05 eV) and IP (5.31 eV) were higher than in the case of the polymers of the l5.7 group and reflected weaker DA interactions. The copolymer showed field effect in the p-channel configuration, the measured FET hole carrier mobility being 3.8 × 10−3 cm2 V−1 s−1. More promising results were obtained in photovoltaic applications. Test cells fabricated from l5.8 showed PCE values of up to 4.88%. Consistent with its lower lying HOMO (higher IP) the Voc values measured for l5.8-based cells (0.86 V) were higher than those determined for the l5.7 group of polymers.

l5.9A and l5.9B (Fig. 25) contain dialkoxy-substituted benzodithiophene as the D unit. Alkoxy groups are introduced to enhance the electron-donating properties of this segment and to facilitate solubilization of the copolymers. The copolymers were synthesized by Stille coupling copolymerization between 2,5-bis(5-bromo-3-dodecylthiophene-2-yl)thiazolo[5,4-d]thiazole and 2,6-bis(trimethyltin)-4,8-didodecyloxybenzo[1,2-b:4,5-b′]dithiophene359 or 2,6-bis(trimethyltin)-4,8-bis(2-ethylhexyloxy)benzo[1,2-b:4,5-b′]dithiophene,360 respectively. IP and optical gap values of l5.9A (5.30 eV and 1.99 eV) are very close to those reported for l5.8 suggesting a similar extent of DA interactions. l5.9A showed a moderate hole carrier mobility of 2.8 × 10−3 cm2 V−1 s−1 (Table 5), determined in the FET configuration. Both copolymers were used as components of BHJ PCs showing moderate PCE values of 2.72% and 2.6% for l5.9A and l5.9B, respectively.

l5.10
i.e. poly([4-(2′-ethylhexyl)-4-octyl-4H-cyclopenta[2,1-b:3,4-b′]dithiophene-2,6-diyl]-alt-[2,5-di(3′-hexylthiophen-2′-yl)thiazolo[5,4-d]thiazole-5′,5′′-diyl]) (Fig. 25) contains the same acceptor unit as l5.7–l5.9 and asymmetrically alkyl-substituted cyclopentadithiophene as the donor unit. It was synthesized by Suzuki polycondensation.361 Although IP determined electrochemically (5.31 eV) was very close to those measured for l5.8 and l5.9A, both electrochemical and optical band gaps were narrower (1.80 eV). l5.10 showed moderate FET mobility of hole carriers (1.0 × 10−3 cm2 V−1 s−1) (Table 5). Taking into account its low lying HOMO level (high IP) and narrow band gap, higher Voc and PCE values were expected for this polymer as compared to l5.7–l5.9. This was not reflected in the parameters of the best cells which gave Voc = 0.67 V and PCE = 4.08%.
Thieno[3,4-c]pyrrole-4,6-dione emerged as a very promising electron-deficient unit in the design of conjugated low band gap polymers (see Fig. 26). Alternating copolymers containing this unit combine rigidity and good solubility with favorable structural organization in this solid film.274f,362
 Fig. 26  Chemical structures of copolymers containing thieno[3,4-c]pyrrole-4,6-dione acceptor units. 
In l5.11 thienopyrroledione segments alternate with pyrroloindacenodithiophene – a moderate electron donor. The copolymer was synthesized by microwave-assisted Stille coupling of distannylated pyrroloindacenodithiophene and dibrominated thienopyrrolodione.363 The electron-donating effect of pyrroloindacenodithiophene results in a decrease of IP to 5.10 eV and in a significant band gap narrowing whose value was determined as 1.70 eV. Films of l5.11 can be used as active layers in p-channel FETs, however the obtained hole mobilities are moderate (0.01 cm2 V−1 s−1) (Table 5). BHJ PCs fabricated from l5.11/PC71BM show moderate PCE values (up to 1.10%) (Table 4) and low Voc. It seems that its low IP (or high lying HOMO level) limits the performance of devices based on l5.11.
More promising results were obtained for copolymers containing dialkoxy substituted benzodithiophene as a donor unit (the same as in l5.9).274a–d Copolymers of the l5.12 series were prepared by Stille coupling between 2,6-bis(trimethyltin)-4,8-di(alkyl)benzo[1,2-b:4,5-b′]dithiophene and 1,3-dibromo-5-octylthieno[3,4-c]pyrrole-4,6-dione.274a Three different alkyl side chains were used as solubilizing groups in the acceptor unit: linear octyl in l5.12A, dimethyloctyl in l5.12B and 2-ethylhexyl in l5.12C.274b The measured IP values (∼5.50 eV) were little dependent on the alkyl substituent topology and significantly lower than in the case of l5.9 which combined the same donor with a weaker acceptor. The DA interactions were reflected by the band gap narrowing to ca. 1.7 eV (see Table 4). The linear substituent favored however ordered supramolecular structure through more effective side groups interdigitation which led to a closer π-stacking: d = 0.36 nm in l5.12A as compared to d = 0.38 nm in l5.12C. Consistent with their higher IP (lower lying HOMO level) polymers of the l5.12 series showed higher Voc as compared to l5.11. The highest value of PCE (6.80%) was measured for l5.12A but two other polymers of the series also gave promising results (Table 4).

l5.13A was synthesized by Stille coupling of 2,6-di(trimethyltin)-N-(2-ethylhexyl)dithieno[3,2-b:2′,3′-d]pyrrole and 1,3-dibromo-5-(2-ethylhexyl)thieno[3,4-c]pyrrole-4,6-dione.364 Having the same acceptor unit as l5.12 it contains a stronger donor. l5.13B differs from l5.13A by its alkyl substituent (octyl instead of 2-ethylhexyl). A strong electron-donating effect is manifested in these copolymers by their low IP values (∼5.10 eV) and narrow band gaps (1.48 eV and 1.59 eV for l5.13B and l5.13A, respectively). This low IP (or high lying HOMO level) limits the performance of PCs in which l5.13B and l5.13A are used as components of BHJ. For this reason low Voc and PCE values are measured (see Table 4).365l5.13B exhibits a moderate FET hole mobility (3.9 × 10−4 cm2 V−1 s−1) (Table 5).274h
Also, in the case of l5.13B a large discrepancy between the electrochemically and optically determined band gap is observed.274h The former (2.08 eV) is significantly larger than the latter (1.59 eV). Similar discrepancies are sometimes observed in conjugated polymers of various nature.283h,346a,366 It should be therefore underlined that all electrochemical measurements must be treated with caution and the recorded redox processes must be identified as originating from the abstraction of an electron from the HOMO level or the addition of an electron to the LUMO level. For the identification of these processes the knowledge of the frontier orbitals distribution is helpful.83 The same applies to the determination of the optical band gap – the observed transition must be identified as the HOMO to LUMO one. For this reason a popular method of the calculation of the LUMO level by adding the optical gap to the energy of the HOMO level may sometimes be very misleading.
The l5.14 series comprises DA copolymers of thieno[3,4-c]pyrrole-4,6-dione with a cyclopenta[2,1-b:3,4-b′]dithiophene as a donor unit. They were synthesized by Stille coupling reaction.367 The following alkyl side groups were introduced into the conjugated chain: butyl (l5.14A), hexyl (l5.14B), octyl (l5.14C), and 2-ethylhexyl (l5.14D) with the goal to improve solution processability and to adjust the packing of the polymer chains in the solid state.
With this moderate donor the IP values are intermediate between those of the l5.13 and l5.12 series, slightly increasing with an increase of the substituent size to the values ∼5.25 eV (Table 4). All copolymers show narrow band gaps close to 1.70 eV. As revealed by X-ray diffraction investigation, l5.14C shows the shortest π-stacking distance of 0.367 nm.367l5.14B, l5.14C and l5.14D are promising materials for the fabrication of BHJ PCs showing PCEs either exceeding 6% or close to this value (Table 4).367

l5.14E has different alkyl substituents both in the acceptor and donor units (dodecyl and octyl, respectively) It shows redox and optical properties similar to those reported for l5.14C and l5.14D, however its photovoltaic properties are inferior.274g The copolymer shows the field effect in the p-channel configuration and its hole carrier mobility approaches 10−2 cm2 V−1 s−1 (Table 5).
In the l5.15 series thieno[3,4-c]pyrrole-4,6-dione acceptor units alternate with dithieno[3,2-b:2′,3′-d]silole donors. The copolymers can be prepared by Stille coupling between 4,4-bis(alkyl)-2,6-bis(trimethyltin)dithieno[3,2-b:2′,3′-d]silole and 1,3-dibromo-5-alkylthieno[3,4-c]pyrrole-4,6-dione.274i,l The presence of the dithienosilole donor narrows the band gap to ∼1.7 eV but, surprisingly, does not significantly lower the IP value, which remains in the range 5.37 eV to 5.57 eV, contrary to the case of the l5.7 series (vide supra).274i,l Appropriately high IPs (low HOMO levels) combined with narrow gaps of these copolymers are very advantageous in view of their use in photovoltaics. Moreover, they show improved nanoscale morphology as compared to other DA copolymers.72d,274l,368 Their hole carrier FET mobilities are in the range of 10−4–10−3 cm2 V−1 s−1 for high molecular mass samples274i and are strongly molecular mass dependent increasing over two orders of magnitude with a three fold increase of Mn. Although the Voc values are high and constant for all polymers studied(∼0.9 V) their PCE is Mn dependent reaching 7.40% for the highest molecular mass fraction of l5.15A (see Table 4).72d
High IP (or low lying HOMO level) is also advantageous from the point of view of the air operating stability. Chu et al.368 reported an air stable inverted organic solar cell consisting of ZnO nanocrystals film as an electron transporting layer and a bulk heterojunction film composed of l5.15A and PC71BM. A power conversion efficiency of 6.7% was measured for the inverted devices which, in addition, turned out to be highly stable and retained 85% of their original efficiency after being stored in air for 32 days without encapsulation.

l5.16 differs from the l5.15 series by the use of the dithieno[3,2-b:2′,3′-d]germole donor instead of its silole analogue. Like l5.15 it was synthesized by Stille coupling using either Pd(PPh3)4369 or Pd2(dba)3/P(o-Tol)3 as catalysts.370 Since the C–Ge bond (0.327 nm) is longer by 0.017 nm compared with the C–Si bond the bulky substituents should be better separated from the polymer backbone which should result in better π–π interactions. l5.16 shows a slightly narrower optical band gap than l5.15A (1.69 eV vs. 1.73 eV),370 a slightly higher IP of 5.66 and slightly higher |EA| with the electrochemical band gap (1.73 eV) close to the optical one.369 Amb et al. have reported a similar IP of 5.60 eV, but a lower |EA| of 3.50 eV.370 Thus redox and optical properties of l5.16 are similar to those of l5.15A. As a result efficient BHP PCs could be fabricated from this polymer reaching PCE values of 4.1% (Table 4)369 or even 7.3% in inverted device architecture ITO/ZnO/l5.16:PC70BM/MoO3/Ag, (Table 4).370
In the synthesis of DA copolymers specific building blocks are designed in which the electron accepting unit is attached to two β-substituted or unsubstituted thiophene rings. As a result, in the formed copolymer A units are separated from D units by an additional thiophene spacer. The use of this approach has some advantages since it allows for additional tuning of the solution processing properties as well as the DA interactions.371 Moreover, in some cases (tetrazine, diketopyrrolopyrrole and others) it is synthetically more convenient to prepare a dithiophene containing building block as an intermediate product in which the electron accepting center is formed in the last step of the reaction.136,372 This approach was also used in the case of the synthesis of DA copolymers containing thieno[3,4-c]pyrrole-4,6-dione acceptors where 1,3-di(thien-2-yl)thieno[3,4-c]pyrrole-4,6-dione derivatives were used as building blocks.
A series of alternating copolymers of 1,3-di(thien-2-yl)thieno[3,4-c]pyrrole-4,6-dione (Fig. 27) in combination with fluorene l5.17, carbazole l5.18, dithienopyrrole l5.19 and benzo[1,2-b:3,4-b′]dithiophene l5.20 have recently been synthesized. Stille coupling (Pd(PPh3)4 catalyst) was used for the preparation of l5.17A, l5.18A and l5.19.373l5.17B and l5.18B were synthesized by Suzuki coupling whereas l5.20 – by both methods (Suzuki and Stille).374 For all these copolymers a clear correlation between their redox and optical properties and the electron donating capability of the D unit can be established. The IPs and band gap widths decrease in the order l5.17 > l5.18 > l5.19 > l5.20. l5.19 suffers from too high lying HOMO levels (or too low IP) which in BHJ PC applications results in low Voc and a moderate value of PCE. For l5.17 and l5.18 the Voc values are very high, due to their deep HOMO level (or high IP) but the band gap is less adapted for the solar spectrum. Nevertheless l5.18A shows a promising PCE value of 3.41%. For the studied series of copolymers, in view of their applications in photovoltaics, l5.20 seems to be the best compromise combining reasonably high Voc (0.90 V) and PCE (3.11%) (see Table 4).
 Fig. 27  Chemical structures of copolymers containing 1,3-di(thien-2-yl)thieno[3,4-c]pyrrole-4,6-dione acceptor units. 
Strong electron withdrawing properties of benzothiadiazole are also of interest when DA copolymers are being designed (Fig. 28). The four substitution positions on the phenyl ring make the chemistry of this unit diversified and facilitate tuning of the redox, electronic and optical properties of the resulting copolymers. For these reasons a large number of papers on benzothiadiazole-based DA conjugated copolymers were published.341b,e,362a Here we limit ourselves to a few instructive examples.
 Fig. 28  Chemical structures of copolymers containing benzothiadiazole acceptor units. 

l5.21A–l5.21C were synthesized by Stille coupling of 2,6-di(trimethyltin)-N-(1-alkyl)dithieno[3,2-b:2′,3′-d]pyrrole containing different alkyl chains (octylnonyl for l5.21A, hexylheptyl for l5.21B, pentylhexyl for l5.21C) and 4,7-dibromo-2,1,3-benzothiadiazole using Pd2(dba)3 as a catalyst.375 Since these are “strong donor–strong acceptor” polymers they show very narrow band gaps only slightly exceeding 1.4 eV which makes them good absorbers in the NIR part of the spectrum. A 0.30–0.35 eV discrepancy is found between the “electrochemical” and optical band gap, the former being larger. The IP values are very low <5.0 eV clearly showing that the DA interaction raises the HOMO level. Although their optical properties should be beneficial for photovoltaic applications, their redox properties are far from being optimal. Due to high lying HOMO levels the test cells based on these polymers show low Voc values (<0.5 V) and moderate PCEs (see Table 4).
Dialkyl cyclopenta[2,1-b:3,4-b′]dithiophene is a weaker donor and its copolymerization with benzothiadiazole yields polymers of higher IP. Poly[2,6-(4,4-bis(2-ethylhexyl)-cyclopenta[2,1-b:3,4-b′]dithiophene)-alt-4,7-(2,1,3-benzothiadiazole)] (l5.22A) exhibits a higher IP value (5.30 eV) than the polymers of the l5.21 series and a comparable electrochemical band gap (1.73 eV) (Table 4).376 Judging from these parameters l5.22A seems to be more suitable for use in BHJ and indeed in test cells it yields higher Voc and better PCE than l5.21 polymers (Table 4).

l5.22B and l5.22C differ from l5.22A by the alkyl substituents. Both polymers were tested as active components of p-channel FETs.351a,c Their thin films exhibited ordered supramolecular organization with the π-stacking distance of 0.36–0.37 nm. In both polymers the mobility was found to be molecular mass dependent, reaching an exceptionally high value of 3.3 cm2 V−1 s−1 (Table 5) for the highest molecular mass fraction of l5.22B. Such strong dependence of charge carrier mobility on Mn is known for a variety of conjugated polymers.72a–c,274i,297
Poly[(4,4′-bis(2-ethylhexyl)dithieno[3,2-b:2′,3′-d]silole)-2-diyl-alt-(2,1,3-benzothiadiazole)-4,7-diyl] (l5.23) was prepared by Stille coupling.346a Dialkyldithienosilole is a strong donor, thus its presence in the polymer backbone lowers the IP to a value of 5.05 eV which is intermediate between the values measured for l5.21 and l5.22 (Table 4). Again a discrepancy between the electrochemical band gap (1.78 eV) and the optical one (1.45 eV) is observed as in l5.21. l5.23 shows the field effect in the p-channel configuration, however the hole carrier mobility, measured in the FET configuration, is rather low 3 × 10−3 cm2 V−1 s−1.346a Much better results are reported for its application in photovoltaics. Despite its low IP (or high lying HOMO level) and, as a consequence, a rather low Voc, cells in which l5.23 was a component of the BHJ showed PCE values of up to 5.1%.346a

l5.24 was synthesized via microwave assisted Stille condensation of benzo[1,2-b:4,5-b]bis(2-trimethylstannyl)-4-(2-octyl-1-dodecyl)-4H-amino[3,2-b]thiophene and 4,7-dibromobenzo[c][1,2,5]thiadiazole.363 Due to a rather pronounced effect of its donor unit, l5.24 shows a low value of IP (4.90 eV) and a low band gap (1.40 eV), the former is unfavorable for photovoltaic applications, the latter is favorable. However, its photovoltaic performance is not impressive (PCE = 0.80%). This is, at least in part, caused by low Voc. The copolymer was also tested as a semiconductor in p-channel FETs yielding a moderate hole carrier mobility of 0.02 cm2 V−1 s−1. This could be in part caused by its low Mn (7000 Da, Table 5).363
The addition of benzene to the thienosilole moiety in silaindacenodithiophene weakens the electron-donating properties, therefore l5.25 an alternating copolymer of silaindacenodithiophene and benzothiadiazole shows higher IP (5.32 eV) and wider optical (1.70 eV) and electrochemical (2.07 eV) gaps than l5.23. l5.25 was obtained by Stille coupling using Pd(PPh3)4 as a catalyst.377 It was used as a p-type semiconductor in the FET configuration yielding a hole mobility of 2.5 × 10−3 cm2 V−1 s−1 (Table 5). Although in the case of l5.25 matching of the solar spectrum by its absorption spectrum is worse than for l5.23, promising results were obtained in test BHJ PCs. Voc exceeded 0.81 V due to relatively high IP (or low lying HOMO) and the measured PCE approached 3.50% for the best cells.
Polymers of the l5.26 series contain indacenodithiophene as a donor unit. l5.26A, l5.26C, l5.26D and l5.26E were synthesized by Suzuki condensation,378 whereas l5.26B by Stille coupling.379 They show high IP values in the range of 5.30 eV to 5.40 eV and the electrochemical band gaps from 1.70 eV to 1.84 eV (Table 4). The IP values are high enough to achieve air operating stability in FETs but at the same time low enough to assure effective hole injection. Therefore these copolymers were tested as active layers in p-channel test transistors. l5.26A, l5.26C and l5.26D showed the FET hole mobility in the range of 0.14 to 1.2 cm2 V−1 s−1, the highest value being reached for l5.26A (Table 5). l5.26E exhibited much lower hole mobility, which could be attributed to its lower molecular mass because hole mobility in the polymers of this series was found to be strongly Mn dependent, as in other DA copolymers and in homopolymers.72a–c,297,274i,351c
Taking into account their IP values and the reduced band gaps polymers of the l5.26 series are promising candidates for photovoltaic applications. With the exception of l5.26A, all polymers showed good performance when tested as components of BHJ in PCs. The best results were obtained for l5.26B.379 The initially obtained PCE value of 5.4% was further improved to 6.10% by the application of previously investigated special annealing techniques which involved slow drying and solvent vapor annealing of the BHJ layer.380
Poly[N-9′-heptadecanyl-2,7-carbazole-alt-5,5-(4′,7′-di-2-thienyl-2′,1′,3′-benzothiadiazole)] (l5.27) (Fig. 29) was prepared by Suzuki coupling using Pd2(dba)3, (o-tol)3P catalyst.314a Introduction of thienylene spacers between the acceptor unit (benzothiadiazole) and the donor unit (2,7-carbazole) significantly changes the redox and optical properties of the resulting copolymer. This can be evidenced by comparing l5.21 (which has no spacers) with l5.27. First, the IP value measured for l5.27 is much higher, indicating weaker DA interactions. This is of course advantageous for air operating stability and for high Voc values in PCs. Second, both electrochemical (1.90 eV) and optical (1.88 eV) band gaps are larger than in l5.21, meaning less adequate matching of the absorption spectrum with the solar spectrum. This is of course disadvantageous. Evidently, the high IP value (implying a low lying HOMO level) is a more dominant factor in this case since the performance of l5.27-based PCs is much better than those based on l5.21. Initially obtained PCE values (up to 3.6%314a) were later improved to 6.1% by adding an optical spacer (TiOx)381 and then to 6.5% by incorporating a conjugated polyelectrolyte interlayer as recommended in ref. 382.
 Fig. 29  Chemical structures of copolymers containing 4,7-di(thien-2-yl) benzothiadiazole-acceptor units. 

l5.28 (Fig. 29) was obtained by a modified Suzuki coupling reaction of 2,7-bis(4,4,5,5-tetramethyl-1,3,2-dioxaborolan-2-yl)-9,9-dioctylsilafluorene with 4,7-bis(5-bromo-2-thienyl)-2,1,3-benzothiadiazole.383 Its IP value (5.39 eV) was lower than that of l5.27 indicating a slightly more pronounced electron-donating effect of dioctylsilafluorene on the position of the HOMO level. l5.28 acted as a p-type semiconductor in p-channel FETs showing, however a rather low hole mobility of 1 × 10−3 cm2 V−1 s−1 (Table 5).383b However its performance in BHJ PCs was much more promising. A PCE of up to 5.4% was obtained (Table 4) with a high Voc value. This was not surprising, taking into account that its redox and optical properties constituted a good compromise for the applications in photovoltaics.
Poly{[2,7-(9,9-bis-(2-ethylhexyl)fluorene)]-alt-[5,5-(4,7-di-2′-thienyl-2,1,3-benzothiadiazole)]} (l5.29A) and poly{[2,7-(9,9-bis-(3,7-dimethyl-octyl)-fluorene)]-alt-[5,5-(4,7-di-2′-thienyl-2,1,3-benzothiadiazole)]} (l5.29B) (Fig. 29), which have the same polymer backbone but different side substituents were synthesized by Stille coupling using Pd(PPh3)4 as a catalyst.384 They are copolymers of a weak donor and a strong acceptor. For this reason they show a high value of IP (5.5 eV) and a relatively large optical band gap. In photovoltaic applications they give promising results – l5.29B shows a high value of Voc (0.97 V) and good PCE (4.5%) (Table 4).384b
Ester, keto and fluoro derivatives of thieno[3,4-b]thiophene constitute promising acceptor units in DA copolymers for photovoltaic applications.346b,c Especially good results are obtained if they are combined with dialkoxy benzo[1,2-b:4,5-b′]dithiophene donors. These copolymers are depicted in Fig. 30. The l5.30–l5.32 series is an instructive example of successful chain engineering. Replacement of the ester group by a keto one increased the IP value by ∼0.1 eV. Addition of the fluorine substituent further increased IP which reached 5.22 eV for l5.32. Consistent with lowering the HOMO level (or increasing IP) Voc increased as well as PCE (7.4% for l5.32). Even better PCE values were obtained for l5.33 which showed slightly lower IP and Voc but PCE as high as 9.2% (Table 4). Some of these polymers are commercial products offered by 1-Material Chemscitech Inc.
 Fig. 30  Chemical structures of copolymers containing substituted thieno[3,4-b]thiophene acceptor units. 
The trends discussed in the text above require a short discussion. Band gap narrowing, which is beneficial for photons harvesting, results in raising of the HOMO level (decreasing IP) and lowering of the LUMO level (increasing of |EA|). However Voc and short circuit current (Isc) are sacrificed due to a smaller energetic difference between the HOMO level of the copolymer and the acceptor constituent of BHJ and more difficult charge separation at the interface of the polymer/acceptor interface. A compromise between these contradictory phenomena is needed. Evidently l5.32 and l5.33 represent a good compromise reaching high PCE despite a rather moderate Voc.
To summarize this part of the paper, the concept of designing alternate or even more complex periodic copolymers from donor and acceptor building blocks turned out to be very successful. A plethora of new semiconductors were prepared showing interesting photovoltaic properties and/or high hole carrier mobilities in p-channel FETs. However, the number of polymers, which would combine sufficiently high IP values (or sufficiently low lying HOMO level) with the band gap appropriately matching the solar spectrum, is still rather scarce. The same applies to semiconductors exhibiting ambipolarity in their electrical transport properties, prepared using this approach. Their number is also low, for the simple reason that it is easier to adjust the appropriate value of IP than |EA|, the latter determining the n-type conductivity conditions.
6 High-spin molecules and macromolecules
6.1 Generalities
In the past decade many research attempts have been directed to spintronics, which exploits the intrinsic spin of the electron and its associated magnetic moment. The advantages of spintronics materials applications are related to lower power use and faster information transfer than in classical electrical devices. To date, only diluted magnetic semiconductors, for example Cd1−xMnxTe or Ga1−xMnxAs, can fulfill these requirements.385 However, the successful use of purely organic materials in organic and molecular electronics can stimulate the search for new organic compounds also for spintronics. So far, organic semiconductors have been used only in few spintronic devices.386 There exists, therefore, a strong need for the design and preparation of new functional organic materials exhibiting spin alignment.
The most general strategy for building high-spin organic molecules is based on alternation of spin containing units (SC) and ferromagnetic coupling units (FC)387 (see Section 6.1.1.5). SC are chemical structures which have an unpaired electron in the form of a radical (Scheme 38, structure 6.1–6.6) or a radical ion (Scheme 38, structures 6.7–6.9). Carbenes 6.1,388 triphenylmethyl radicals 6.2,389 aminyl radicals 6.3,390,391 phenoxyl radicals 6.4,392 nitroxide radicals 6.5,393 nitronyl nitroxides 6.6394 are typical SC units. Radical cations can be created after partial oxidation† of conjugated segments as in octatetraene 6.7 or oligothiophene.15,16 However, the most studied and most stable are aryl ammonium radical cations 6.8.395
 Scheme 38  Organic chemical moieties bearing spins (spin containing units, SC). 
Very recently radical anions 6.9 fulfilling these requirements have also been reported.396
The early studies of organic high-spin materials were devoted to oligo- and polycarbenes. Iwamura and co-workers improved the synthesis of these compounds and obtained S = 5 for pentacarbene.388c However, oligo- and polycarbenes are very unstable, thus precluding potential applications. Triphenylmethyl radicals 6.2 were later developed as alternatives to carbenes. The stability of these radicals can be increased by introducing substituents inducing steric hindrance for example perchlorosubstituents, which can impede the spin dimerization by blocking the rotation of the phenylene rings.389b Significant progress in the design and the synthesis of this type of radical was made by Rajca and co-workers.389c,397 The highest spin state (up to S ≈ 5000) was obtained for polyradicals derived from macrocyclic polyarylmethyls.397d Aminyl radicals 6.3 are also interesting as building segments of high-spin molecules. Rajca reported that a diradical similar to 6.3 has a high-spin ground state and postulated from EPR data that they have a more stable high-spin state than the corresponding nitroxide diradicals.390
Polymers containing conjugated segments alternating with 1,3-benzenediyl were synthesized by Dougherty and co-workers.15,16 Radical cations (polarons) have been generated in conjugated segments after p-type doping.15 However, the most chemically stable and most promising compounds studied to date are compounds containing triarylamine radical cations.395

6.1.1 Strategies of electron spin coupling in the design of high spin polymers. 
Several strategies were developed for the preparation of high-spin organic and organometallic compounds with the goal to design organic or organometallic magnets (for a comprehensive review see ref. 398). Some of them rely on the design of such compounds in which mutual intermolecular interactions between organic free radicals or between paramagnetic ions in well-defined crystal structures are possible (for recent reviews in the field of molecular magnetism see ref. 399 and 400). This section focuses on the strategies that are best suited for obtaining high-spin polymers. In the designed macromolecules, through-bond interactions between the electronic spins of organic free radical moieties in the same macromolecule have to be induced (for reviews see ref. 389c and 401–403). They are presented in this section for simple examples of diradicals that were successfully designed in order to obtain high-spin S = 1 ground states and a few examples of oligo- and polyradicals leading to high-spin S > 1 ground states.
6.1.1.1 Ferromagnetic (or antiferromagnetic) coupling of electron spins in biradicals – exchange coupling constant (J). 
Let us consider a biradical with two identical free radical moieties, which are covalently bound to a common molecular backbone. The magnetic properties of such a system can be qualitatively described by the active-electron approximation.404 In this approximation only the two single electrons (noted 1 and 2) corresponding to the two free radical moieties (noted A and B) are considered as active and all other electrons are considered as passive. The molecular orbitals φA and φB corresponding to the singly occupied molecular orbitals of the monoradicals are used as the so-called “natural orbitals” basis set and have an energy level noted E0.
If φA and φB spread over different and disjoint parts of the molecule, the interaction between the two electrons is null or very weak and the biradical behaves as two independent S = 1/2 free radicals.
If φA and φB spread over common parts of the molecule, then they are called coextensive402 and the interaction between the two electrons must be taken into account to derive the molecular orbitals and energy levels that account for the magnetic properties of the biradical405 (see Fig. 31).
 Fig. 31  Energy levels due to interactions between single electrons in a biradical in the active-electron approximation. E0 corresponds to the energy of a single electron with no interaction with the second single electron. K corresponds to the electrostatic interaction between the two single electrons and J to the exchange interaction (see text). 
These molecular orbitals are: (i) the product of a symmetric combination of φA and φB (noted ΦS) with an anti-symmetric singlet spin function χ (S = 0) of energy ES and (ii) the product of an anti-symmetric combination of φA and φB (noted ΦA) with a symmetric triplet spin function χ (S = 1) of energy ET. The interaction between the two electrons changes the energy level E0 by adding a purely electrostatic term (K) and a second term (J) called exchange interaction, whose origin is purely quantic and that lifts the degeneracy between the states of different spins S with ΔEST = ES − ET = J (ref. 404).

J can be positive or negative. When J is positive, the system has an S = 1 ground state, so the two electrons are ferromagnetically coupled. When J is negative, the system has an S = 0 ground state, so the two electrons are antiferromagnetically coupled. Using the same level of approximation, one can demonstrate that J is the sum of an antiferromagnetic term (noted J1) and a ferromagnetic term (noted J2) as shown by eqn (6), ref. 404: (6)From these expressions the conditions for observing a strong ferromagnetic coupling (S = 1 ground state) between the spins of two electrons belonging to the same (macro)-molecule can be deduced:
(i) the two singly occupied orbitals associated to these electrons φA and φB should be orthogonal, then the overlap integral ∫φA(r)·φB(r)·d3r and the antiferromagnetic term J1 vanish,
(ii) φA and φB should be coextensive, in order to make the so-called exchange integral in the ferromagnetic term J2 as high as possible. The term “coextensive” means that these orbitals share some common space in the molecule and that the two electrons can interact quite strongly.402,403
6.1.1.2 Molecular structures inducing ferromagnetic/antiferromagnetic coupling of electron spins – concept of a ferromagnetic coupling unit. 
The trimethylenemethane biradical is the simplest organic biradical with an S = 1 ground state that was predicted by Longuet-Higgins406 in 1950 and observed by Dowd407 sixteen years later. It can be considered as two methyl radicals connected to the same carbon atom of ethylene or as the molecule C(CH2)3 with four electrons in its π system. The electronic structure of this biradical obtained by simple Hückel calculations is shown in Fig. 32 (right). The two electrons at the highest energy level are localized in two degenerate and orthogonal orbitals. Moreover, these two orbitals are coextensive, since two carbon atoms have non-zero coefficients in both orbitals. So all conditions for observing an S = 1 ground state are satisfied.
 Fig. 32  Electronic structures of biradicals bonded to 1,2-ethene (left) and 1,1-ethene (right). The schematic view depicts the energy levels and the electronic structures of the π-system of biradicals and the two singly occupied orbitals that are obtained by using Hückel's approximation. 
On the opposite, two methyl radicals connected at both extremities of ethylene have the same electronic structure as butadiene as shown in Fig. 32 (left). All molecular orbitals are occupied by two electrons of opposite spins (due to the Pauli principle), so this molecule has an S = 0 spin state. Thus a simple difference in topology of the molecular structure of this molecule compared to the trimethylenemethane biradical induces an antiferromagnetic coupling instead of a ferromagnetic coupling between the spins of the two methyl radical moieties.
The same observations can be made for the family of benzoquinodimethane diradicals. The meta-benzoquinodimethane diradical has an S = 1 ground state.408–410 This is consistent with its electronic structure showing two singly occupied degenerate, orthogonal and coextensive orbitals (orbitals 4 and 5 in Fig. 33). On the opposite, para-benzoquinodimethane diradicals (shown in Fig. 33) and ortho-benzoquinodimethane diradicals (not shown) have an S = 0 spin state due to their closed shell electronic structures. Again the ferromagnetic or antiferromagnetic coupling of the two electron spins is determined by the topology of the molecule.
 Fig. 33  Electronic structures of meta-benzoquinodimethane (left) and para-benzoquinodimethane (right) biradicals. This schematic view depicts the energy levels and the two singly occupied orbitals that are obtained by using Hückel's approximation. 
Remarkably, if one replaces the methyl free radicals by another type of free radical moiety to build structures with a topology similar to trimethylenemethane biradical or to meta-benzoquinodimethane diradical, an S = 1 ground state is also obtained. In biradicals 6.10411 and 6.11412 (Scheme 39) nitroxide free radicals or ammonium free radicals are connected by a meta-phenylene moiety, leading to an S = 1 ground state as in the meta-benzoquinodimethane diradical. In the biradical 6.12413,414 nitroxide free radicals are connected by a 1,1-ethylene moiety as in the trimethylenemethane biradical. All these diradicals have an S = 1 ground state.
 Scheme 39  Biradicals 10–12 exhibiting triplet ground state, S = 1 and biradical 13 exhibiting S = 0. 
The observation of the same effects in several other diradicals389c,398 led to the conclusion that meta-phenylene and 1,1-ethylene act as “ferromagnetic coupling units” and produce an S = 1 ground spin state when they connect two free radicals. However, the ferromagnetic coupling is obtained only when the free radicals and the coupling unit are (enough) coplanar in order to obtain a significant conjugation between these constituents of the molecule. For instance, in the biradical 6.13,415 the two nitroxide moieties are strongly twisted out of the plane of the phenyl ring due to the steric hindrance of the methoxy groups, which results in an S = 0 ground state.
6.1.1.3 Topological model as a tool for the design of spin coupling units. 
The trimethylenemethane biradical and the meta-benzoquinodimethane diradical are two examples of alternant conjugated hydrocarbons. A conjugated hydrocarbon is alternant if all its carbon atoms can be divided into two classes (usually referred to as “starred” and “unstarred”) in such a way that each carbon of the molecule is linked only to carbon atoms belonging to the other class. Ovchinnikov13 using the Lieb's theorem416 demonstrated that, when the number of starred atoms is not equal to the number of unstarred atoms (see Scheme 40), the ground spin state is expected to be non-zero with the following relationship (eqn (7)): S = ½|N* − N°|(7)
In such cases, N* − N° also corresponds to the number of non-bonding singly-occupied π orbitals in the considered alternant hydrocarbon. This topological model enabled Ovchinnikov to propose several high spin structures for polymers (see Sections 6.2.1 and 6.4).
 Scheme 40  Examples of simple alternant conjugated hydrocarbons with their expected ground spin state. 
In addition, the topological model paved the way for finding new ferromagnetic coupling units. For instance, this model predicts that 1,3,5-phenylene acts as a ferromagnetic coupling unit between three methyl free radicals (see Scheme 40). Consistently, Ovchinnikov proposed the star-branched hydrocarbon 6.14 as the S = 3/2 ground state triradical, in agreement with the earlier experimental observation417 of the S = 3/2 ground state of 6.15 (despite a rapid dimerization reaction of such hydrocarbon-based triradicals). At last, the observation of the S = 3/2 ground state of 6.16 with ammonium cation radicals as spin carrying moieties418,419 demonstrated that the 1,3,5-phenylene unit, 6.17, acts also as an efficient ferromagnetic spin coupler between three ammonium free radical moieties (Scheme 41).
 Scheme 41  Star-branched structures with ferromagnetic coupling by the 1,3,5-phenylene unit. 
Based on the topological model, one can predict that the 3,4′-biphenylene and 4,4′′-meta-terphenylene groups, represented below, should behave as ferromagnetic coupling units, as confirmed experimentally by the S = 1 ground state of the corresponding free radical 6.19, 6.20 studied by Rajca420 and 6.21 by Bushby421 (Scheme 42). The meta-phenylene, 1,3,5-phenylene and 3,4′-biphenylene groups are the most commonly used ferromagnetic coupling units for the design of high-spin polymers as will be illustrated in Sections 6.2, 6.3 and 6.4.
 Scheme 42  3,4′-Biphenyl and 4,4′′-meta-terphenyl groups as ferromagnetic coupling units. 
Among many other conjugated hydrocarbon structures which can be considered as candidates for FC, let us mention those based on fused polycyclic aromatic compounds such as 2,7-naphthalene 6.22, whose efficiency was confirmed by the S = 1 ground state of 6.24422 and 2,4,5,7 anthracene, which is able to couple up to four free radical moieties in an S = 2 state as observed for the radical 6.23 (Scheme 43).423–425
 Scheme 43  2,7-Naphthalene and 2,4,5,7-anthracene as ferromagnetic coupling units. 
6.1.1.4 Strength of the spin coupling (J) for different ferromagnetic coupling units. 
The topological model is very helpful in the design of high-spin polymers since it predicts whether a given group connecting two (or more) free radical moieties can act as a ferromagnetic coupling unit. However, this model does not give information about the strength (J) of this coupling, which has to be investigated experimentally (see Section 6.1.2) or by quantum chemistry calculations. Li et al.426,427 used an effective valence-bond model to calculate the singlet-triplet energy gaps for hydrocarbon biradicals corresponding to the structures commonly used or envisioned as ferromagnetic spin coupling units.
According to these calculations, 1,1-ethylene and meta-phenylene units produce very strong spin coupling (see Scheme 44 and Table 6). Sets 6.25 and 6.26 include ferromagnetic spin coupling units derived from 1,1-ethylene. For these sets, it is clear that making the system larger results in much less efficient coupling. In set 6.27, which includes ferromagnetic spin coupling units derived from meta-phenylene, the loss of coupling is less pronounced, upon addition of consecutive rings, and the 3,4-biphenyl unit still exhibits a rather high exchange coupling constant J of 0.248 eV (J/k = 2880 K, in temperature units). For set 6.28, which includes 2,7-naphthalene and other ferromagnetic spin coupling units expanded by adding fused phenyl rings, the loss of coupling is moderate so for 2,7-naphthalene and 2,7-anthracene units the calculated J values are more than half of the value computed for meta-phenylene (1.146 eV).426
 Scheme 44  Different ferromagnetic coupling units.

6.1.1.5 Alternation of spin containing units and spin coupling units as a strategy for building high-spin molecules. 
To date, the most developed strategy of building high-spin polymers consists in alternating spin containing units with ferromagnetic spin coupling units in one molecule (macromolecule) ⋯–SC–FC–SC–FC–⋯ as proposed for the first time by Fukutome (Fig. 34).14 The first macromolecules of this type were designed using doped conjugated oligomeric fragments as SC units. Each oligomeric fragment contains one electron or one hole and can be considered as a radical ion capable of forming polarons, hence the name “polaronic model” for this strategy.
 Fig. 34  Polaronic model of the ferromagnetic polymer. 
The first polymer, synthesized according to this model, was reported by Dougherty and coworkers15 in the early 1990's. It consisted of meta-phenylene as an FC unit and tetraene which were oxidized to produce radical cations in the form of positive polarons as SC units (Scheme 45). Oxidative doping of 6.29 with AsF5 yielded a rather low concentration of radical cations, but high enough to obtain an S = 2 ground spin state. Since then, many other examples of molecules (macromolecules) consisting of other FC and SC (not only polaronic structures but also free radicals) were reported. They will be discussed in greater detail in Sections 6.2–6.4.
 Scheme 45  The structure of poly(meta-phenyleneoctatetraene). 
6.1.1.6 Functionalization with pendant radicals on a conjugated polymer backbone as a strategy for building high-spin molecules. 
The design of oligomers and polymers consisting of conjugated main chain and pendant groups of free radical nature was first proposed by Ovchinnikov (Fig. 35).428
 Fig. 35  Model of high-spin polymers designed by grafting SC units as pendant chains on a conjugated polymer backbone. 
Polymers and oligomers with pendant phenoxyl free radicals linked to a poly(phenylenevinylene) backbone are instructive examples of these compounds (see Scheme 46).429 The free radical moieties are grafted on positions that are expected to favour the ferromagnetic coupling between electron spins according to the topological model, as can be checked from the chemical formulae of 6.30–6.32. Experimentally, S = 1 ground states were observed for these dimers and an average spin state up to S = 3/2 was observed for the polymer 6.31 (S = 1 for the polymer 6.30), despite a limited oxidative doping efficiency which yielded 0.59 spin per unit.
 Scheme 46  Conjugated polymers and dimers containing pendant phenoxyl radicals. 
The expected advantage of this strategy is that the resulting molecule or macromolecule should be less sensitive to incomplete formation of spins than the molecules in which radicals or radical cations are being formed in the main chain (polaronic model). In the latter case incomplete oxidation disrupts the magnetic interactions between successive electronic spins (see Section 6.2). On the opposite, incomplete oxidation of pendant groups should not totally vanish the interaction between the spins immediately preceding and following the defect, as exemplified by 6.32 (for m = 0 or 1), both of which exhibit an S = 1 ground state, even if the exchange coupling constant J/k decreases from 24 K for m = 0 to only 6 K in the case of m = 1. More examples illustrating this strategy will be discussed in Sections 6.2.2. and 6.4.


6.1.2 Experimental measurements of spin multiplicity (S) and spin coupling constants (J). 
Two main quantities characterize the magnetic properties of a given polymer: the spin multiplicity S of the ground state and the spin coupling constants J.
The spin multiplicity S = n/2 of the ground state corresponds to the number (n) of individual S = 1/2 electronic spins that are actually ferromagnetically coupled. This number reflects how far the ferromagnetic interaction between the electronic spin propagates along the polymer backbone. In most cases reported to date organic high-spin polymers exhibit spin multiplicity between S = 1 (electron spins coupled by two) and S = 5 (electron spins coupled by groups of ten).430 Ideally S should be made as high as possible in order to obtain a ferromagnetic or ferrimagnetic material.397d
Following the definition given in Section 6.1.1.1, the spin coupling constant between two spins (J) measures the stability of the ferromagnetically coupled high-spin ground state compared with the antiferromagnetically coupled low-spin excited state. The measurements of the J coupling constants between coupled spins in a given high-spin polymer is of outmost importance since they allow us to estimate the maximum temperature to which the polymer can exhibit the desired magnetic properties of the high-spin ground state.
Different techniques can be used for measuring J and S values in high-spin polymers.
