The Evan's method based on NMR measurements and the Faraday's scale can be used (see dedicated sections in the ref. 389c) but we will focus on SQUID magnetometry and EPR spectroscopy only, which are currently the most frequently used techniques. Moreover, we describe the pulsed-EPR nutation technique, which is of particular interest in the case of samples containing several spin states with different multiplicities.
6.1.2.1 SQUID magnetometry. 
Superconducting Quantum Interference Device magnetometry (for the principle of this magnetometry see ref. 431) is the most popular technique to study the magnetic properties of high-spin polymers. This technique enables the measurement of the magnetization of a given sample in a wide range of temperatures (typically 2–300 K) and a wide range of magnetic field (typically up to 7 T) with a high sensitivity. Typically high-spin polymers or low molecular mass compounds containing as few as 3 × 10−7 mol of electronic spins can be studied by this technique.432,433
When measuring the magnetization, M, of a high-spin polymer sample, the magnetometer measures the sum of two types of contributions: (i) the paramagnetic contribution due to electronic spins in the high-spin polymer (noted Mpara), (ii) the diamagnetic contribution (noted Mdia) associated with all diamagnetic components of the sample (the sample holder, the polymer itself, solvent molecules…). J and S values are related to Mpara only, so Mdia must be determined as precisely as possible, in order to extract Mpara (Mpara = M − Mdia) from the experimental data with a good precision. The determination of Mdia can be a hard task in the case of dilute samples. An efficient experimental procedure to measure and subtract Mdia with a high precision in the case of high-spin organic molecules was described in detail by Bushby et al.421
The spin multiplicity S of the magnetic ground state can be determined by measuring the magnetization as a function of the magnetic field, M = f(H), at low temperature in such a manner that only the magnetic ground state is populated (generally this study is performed at T ≤ 2 K). If the sample contains a high-spin polymer with only one spin state of a given multiplicity S, the performed measurement can be directly related to S by comparing the experimental curve Mpara = f(H) with the theoretical curves expected for the various S values (namely Brillouin's functions) (eqn (8)): (8)where β stands for Bohr's Magneton, g for Landé's factor, k for Boltzmann's constant and N for the number of spins S in the sample. An example of such magnetometry measurement and analysis applied to a doped polyarylamine (see ref. 432) is shown in Fig. 36. By comparing the experimental normalized magnetization (noted M/Msat, where Msat denotes the maximum value of magnetization in the applied external magnetic field) with the Brillouin's functions, one can notice that the experimental data are very close to S = 2 at low magnetic field and to S = 3/2 at high magnetic field which is diagnostic of a high-spin polymer sample bearing a mix of spin state,16 in this particular case a mix of S = 3/2 spin states and S = 2 spin states432 (the properties of this particular system will be discussed in Section 6.2.1).
 Fig. 36  Example of the analysis of spin multiplicity S in a doped high-spin polyarylamine. M = f(H) is measured and normalized and then compared with Brillouin functions (Bs) corresponding to different values of S. The polymer PN was doped at two equivalents of oxidant per mer. Solid lines represent unpublished calculations (J. M. Mouesca, V. Maurel and I. Kulszewicz-Bajer, 2013) which are compared with the experimental data taken from ref. 432. 
If the spin coupling constant(s) J are small or at least lower than kT, they can be extracted from measurements of the magnetization as a function of the temperature M = f(T) at a given weak magnetic field (typically H ≤ 500 Oe). Then, the χT = f(T) plot, where χ is the magnetic susceptibility of the sample (M = χH) can be compared to the analytical expressions of χT = f(T) derived from Van Vleck's approximation (see for instance ref. 404). These expressions depend on the spin multiplicity S and they include one or several J value(s) as parameter(s), depending on the considered spin coupling pathway. For the simplest case of an S = 1 high-spin ground state corresponding to electrons coupled by two with one coupling constant J, χT = f(T) is modelized by the Bleaney–Bowers434expression (9) (for more complicated cases of coupling of three up to nine S = 1/2 spins see ref. 435 and 436): (9)where β stands for Bohr's Magneton, g for Landé's factor and k for Boltzmann's constant and N for the number of S = 1 spins in the sample.
An example of the J coupling constant measurement in the arylamine dimer421 is shown in Fig. 37: the J/k = 42 K value is obtained by fitting the experimental χT = f(T) plot with the Bleaney–Bowers expression and was the first experimental J value recorded for a diradical designed using a 4-4′′-meta-terphenyl group as a spin coupling unit.
 Fig. 37  Determination of J in oxidatively doped S = 1 arylamine dimer from the plot of χT = f(T). The solid line represents the calculated values with the best fit parameters using the Bleaney–Bowers equation. The obtained J coupling constant is J/k = 42 K with the definition of J adopted here. [reproduced by permission from R. J. Bushby, N. Taylor and R. A. Williams, J. Mater. Chem., 2007, 17, 955–964]. 
6.1.2.2 Continuous-wave Electron Paramagnetic Resonance (CW-EPR). 
Continuous-wave Electron Paramagnetic Resonance is well adapted for the study of organic free radicals and polyradicals437,438 since it selectively detects paramagnetic components of the sample with a good sensitivity. Typically 1010S = 1/2 spins can be detected by this technique. The presence of electron spins with multiplicity S > 1/2 induces two typical features in the CW-EPR spectra: (i) a fine structure originating from the electron–electron interaction, named zero-field splitting interaction which dominates the EPR spectrum, (ii) a much weaker additional signal which appears at half-field due to forbidden ΔMs = ±2 EPR transition(s), nonexistent for S = 1/2 systems.
Thus, by recording the EPR spectrum of a polymer containing unpaired spins, one can easily find if an S > 1/2 high-spin state is present in the sample. However, the determination of the spin multiplicity is more delicate: in most cases quantum chemistry calculations of the zero-field splitting interaction have to be performed to verify whether the observed fine structure is due to S = 1, S = 3/2 or a higher spin state. In some cases, the spectral features due to the zero-field splitting interaction are typical of a given spin multiplicity. For instance, in the case of the star-shape triradical 17, the main EPR signal corresponding to ΔMs = ±1 EPR transition is composed of five equidistant features, consistent with the fine structure of an S = 3/2 system with a quasi-axial symmetry and a small zero-field compared to the electron Zeeman interaction.439
On the other hand, the intensity of the EPR spectra is proportional to the paramagnetic contribution to the sample magnetization, so it is possible to measure J coupling constants by performing a temperature study χT = f(T) of the same kind as described previously for the SQUID magnetometry (see 6.1.3.1) (see for instance ref. 440 and 441). The EPR measurement of J coupling constants has a great advantage over SQUID magnetometry. In this measurement the diamagnetic contribution to the magnetization originating from the sample holder, the solvent and the high-spin molecule/polymer itself is not observed and does not need to be subtracted. However, EPR measurements of J coupling constants can be difficult to perform for samples containing S = 1/2 impurities: the EPR spectrum of the high-spin state under study and the spectrum of the S = 1/2 impurities almost always overlap and the subtraction of the S = 1/2 signal for spectra recorded in a wide temperature range can be difficult due to the very different spin relaxation properties of S = 1/2 and S > 1/2 spin states.
6.1.2.3 Pulsed-EPR nutation spectroscopy. 
Pulsed-EPR nutation spectroscopy442–444 is a powerful tool for the study of high-spin polymers because using this technique it is possible to measure the spin multiplicities S present in a complex system where spins of different multiplicities coexist.
In a pulsed-EPR nutation443,445 experiment, a long and powerful microwave pulse (whose magnetic field is noted as B1) is applied to the sample, which induces a rotation of the electronic spins around B1 called “nutation”. The frequency of this motion of “nutation” depends on several parameters and in particular on the spin multiplicity S with the following relationship for an electron spin S involved in a given (S, mS) ↔ (S, mS + 1) EPR transition (10): (10)
Note: this relationship is not always valid depending on the intensity of B1 and on the fine structure of the considered high-spin state.439,443
In the majority of pulsed-EPR nutation experiments, the nutation frequency is measured as a function of the static magnetic field for a range covering the full CW-EPR spectrum of the sample, providing 2D maps signal = f(B0,νnut) such as the one presented in Fig. 38. This 2D-map was measured for a sample of branched polyarylamine oxidatively doped at the stoichiometry providing the highest proportion of high spin states (as confirmed by the results of SQUID magnetometry presented in ref. 446). From this spectrum it can be seen that four different spin states (S = 1/2, S = 1, S = 3/2 and S = 2) can be detected in the sample. Their identification would be very difficult by SQUID magnetometry, because the corresponding Brillouin functions are not orthogonal and the fitting process of the Mpara = f(H) curve could not be reliable when using a high number of adjustable parameters.
 Fig. 38  Example of 2D field-swept pulsed-EPR nutation spectrum for a sample containing a mix of four different spin states. The pulsed-EPR experiment was performed at T = 7 K with a sample of the polymer PAB1 oxidized to the radical cation state at the oxidant:polymer ratio 3:1 [V. Maurel and I. Kulszewicz-Bajer, 2013, unpublished data]. 
Unfortunately, the relative intensities of the pulsed-EPR nutation signals of the different spins states cannot be used to find the proportions of the different spins states S in the sample, mainly because these signals are affected by the electronic relaxation times of the various spin states. For instance the relaxation times of S = 1/2 spin states are generally longer than those of higher spin states and the relative intensity of S = 1/2 spin states in pulsed-EPR nutation spectra is generally too high compared with the actual proportion of the S = 1/2 spin state in the sample.
Pulsed-EPR nutation spectroscopy is also a very useful tool to optimize the oxidative doping process of a high-spin polymer and find the optimal stoichiometry between the oxidizing agent and the polymer. An example of such a study for a linear polyarylamine grafted with small pending units is shown in Fig. 39, similar results were reported in ref. 432. According to the 2D EPR-nutation maps, the optimal oxidant:polymer ratio is 2:1. At this particular ratio, the signals corresponding to the high spin state S = 2 are dominant compared with other signals originating form the lower spin states. On the opposite, at a 1:1 oxidant:polymer ratio, the dominant signal corresponds to an S = 1/2 state and signals due to higher spin states are very weak.
 Fig. 39  Monitoring of the chemical doping of the polymer PN by 2D pulsed-EPR nutation. The polymer PN was oxidized to the radical cation state with the following molar ratios: Ox/PN = 1 (lower frame), 2 (middle frame), and 3 (upper frame) [V. Maurel and I. Kulszewicz-Bajer, 2013, unpublished data]. 

6.2 High-spin linear oligomers and polymers
Many fundamental studies of ferromagnetic spins interaction in organic molecules have been focused on oligomers which can be considered as model compounds for linear polymers.389c,390,393a,395a,421–425,447,448 This approach allows us to determine several factors characterizing spin interactions for example the planarity of the system,390,423–425 the sign of the exchange interaction,421 the effect of conformational changes.395b,412 These findings can then be extended to linear polymers. Taking into account the location of spin carrying units, SC, one can distinguish two types of linear polymers:
• polymers containing SC units in the main chain,
• polymers containing SC units in the pendant groups.
The polymers of the first class show one main weakness. If in some SC units spins are not created, the ferromagnetic interaction is interrupted and the molecule is divided into several clusters showing lower multiplicity than theoretically predicted. The polymers of the second type are more spin-deficit resistant because they interact magnetically via the conjugated π-system (Fig. 40).
 Fig. 40  Effect of the lack of a spin due to incomplete doping on the coupling between electronic spins in high-spin polymers designed following the polaronic model or by grafting pendant radical on a conjugated backbone. 
However, it should be noticed that polymers of both types show relatively high flexibility, thus local conformation of the polymer chain can be easily changed. The chain conformation can, in turn, modify the character of magnetic spins interactions from ferromagnetic to antiferromagnetic ones. Despite these disadvantages linear polymers still attract significant research attention because they also represent certain advantages. From the practical point of view, they are relatively easily processed and some of them exhibit sufficient chemical stability at room temperature, thus they could be interesting for potential applications.

6.2.1 Polymers containing spin carrying units in the main chain. 
Important attempts to design one-dimensional organic ferromagnets have been undertaken by Iwamura and co-workers.388c,447,448 They prepared linear oligocarbenes (Scheme 47, structure 6.33), which exhibited intramolecular ferromagnetic coupling. The EPR spectrum of tetracarbene showed the features characteristic of a nonet state.447 The temperature dependence of the total signal intensity indicated that S = 4 is the ground-spin state. However, irreversible changes of the spectrum were detected at 64 K, related to twisting of the phenyl rings. The semiempirical calculations demonstrated that tetracarbene can form four conformational isomers, however all of them have nonet as a ground-spin state. Thus, the magnetic properties of this molecule are not sensitive to conformational changes. Similarly, the EPR spectrum of pentacarbene showed the features of fine structure expected for S = 5.388c The magnetic susceptibility measurements performed for tetracarbene confirmed the conclusion derived from the EPR spectra. However, the 1/χ vs. temperature of tetracarbene in a glass matrix of Me-THF indicated that an antiferromagnetic interaction took place at temperatures below 65 K.448 This suggests that tetracarbene molecules are close enough to induce antiferromagnetic interaction between them.
 Scheme 47  Oligocarbenes. 
The lack of chemical stability of carbene compounds directed the research efforts towards more resistant materials like for example organic nitroxides. Trinitroxide 6.34 (Scheme 48) can be considered as a model compound for constructing polymeric organic magnets containing NO˙ groups.393a
 Scheme 48  Polymers containing nitroxide groups. 
To avoid the isomerization to aminoquinone, tert-butyl groups were attached to the phenylene rings for steric protection. The EPR spectrum of 6.34 in glassy toluene showed a fine structure signal, characteristic of randomly oriented molecules in a quartet spin state. Magnetic measurements, using Faraday magnetic balance, confirmed that the intramolecular exchange coupling was strongly ferromagnetic with J/k = 240 ± 20 K.393a Oka and co-workers have synthesized polymers containing nitroxide groups (Scheme 48, structures 6.35, 6.36, 6.37).449,450 It should be pointed out that in these compounds the NO˙ moiety is π-conjugated with adjacent phenylene rings and the spin density is spread over the whole segment. Thus, one can consider the structures of these polymers as nitroxides containing two phenylene rings coupled through m-benzene or as nitroxides NO˙ moieties linked through a meta-terphenyl coupler. 6.35 and 6.37 were very stable in their radical form in solution and in the solid state under ambient conditions, whereas radical forms of 6.36 were stable only in solution. The EPR studies corroborated the formation of high-spin states for all three polymers. The spectra in dichloromethane showed a signal at g = 2.0055 (in the case of 6.37) as well as an additional signal at half-field for ΔMs = ±2 transition. The magnetization data were in accordance with S = 1 for 6.35 and S = 3/2 for 6.36 and 6.37.449,450 The magnetic exchange coupling constant for 6.37 was J/k = 8.6 K.450 It has been demonstrated that effective through-bond spin interaction occurs in the polymers containing nitroxide groups, however, it is difficult to avoid the formation of spin defects.
Rajca and co-workers have postulated from EPR experiments that m-phenylene aminyl radicals should exhibit S = 1 ground state with 4 times greater energy gap between the singlet and the triplet states as compared to the case of polymers containing the nitroxide diradical.390 Thus, they directed their attention toward compounds with aminyl radicals, although these radicals are only short-lived.
The EPR spectrum of the diaminyl radical 6.38 showed six symmetrical signals. The analysis of the 14N hyperfine tensor led to the conclusion that aminyl radicals and the m-phenylene ring are co-planar.390 SQUID measurements revealed the presence of the S = 1 state. Higher homologues of the aminyl diradical have also been synthesized by Rajca et al.391 (see Scheme 49, structure 6.39). These compounds can be considered as oligo(m-anilines) or the derivatives of conjugated azaacenes. Ladder oligomers with up to 13 fused six-membered rings and six aza bridges were obtained.391 The DFT optimized geometries and the crystallographic structures determined by X-ray investigations suggest that these compounds show in-plane bending of the ladder, thus the oligomers can be presented in the form of an arc. High-spin properties of these new compounds have not yet been determined.
 Scheme 49  Model compound with aminyl radicals (6.38) and oligo(meta-aniline) (6.39). 
As previously mentioned, spin containing units (SC) can be composed not only of radicals but also of radical-ions (Section 6.1). Fukutome proposed a design of organic ferromagnets based on the polaronic model.14 In this case the SC unit is constituted of a conjugated segment which forms a radical cation after its partial oxidation (p-type doping). Using this concept Dougherty and co-workers synthesized a series of polymers containing different conjugated segments as SC units and m-benzene as a ferromagnetic coupler (Scheme 50: structures 6.40, 6.41, 6.42).15,16
 Scheme 50  Polymers containing different conjugated segments as SC units. 
Several criteria must be taken into consideration when designing this type of polymer. The conjugated segment should have a minimal length. In this respect a short conjugated segment is favored for ferromagnetic interaction which dramatically decreases with increasing spins separation. Moreover, in short conjugated segments polarons are preferentially formed after partial oxidation whereas in a long conjugated segment spinless bipolarons can dominate. On the other hand, an increase of the polaron delocalization in a long conjugated segment stabilizes radical cations. Additionally, long conjugated segments can be oxidatively doped under milder conditions which helps to avoid the polymer degradation. Thus, the design of this type polymer is a real challenge and “a compromise” between all mentioned above factors have to be found. The magnetization measurements performed for 6.40–6.42 showed that the experimental data can be well fitted using the Brillouin function with S = 2.2 for 6.40, S = 2.1 for 6.41 for samples lightly oxidized with AsF5. However, for some samples of 6.40 and 6.41S ≥ 4 was also detected. In the case of 6.42 the S = 1 state was achieved. A weak antiferromagnetic interaction was observed at low temperatures in all cases, probably due to an interchain interaction. It should be, however, noted that polarons created in these polymers are very stable at room temperature under an inert atmosphere and after 7 days the spin concentration drops down to 70% of its original value. The main inconvenience in the use of macromolecules of this type is related to the doping procedure which causes the samples of the same polymer to exhibit different properties caused by slight changes in their oxidation conditions.
Electronically conducting polymers can constitute an interesting inspiration in the design of organic ferromagnets. Among them polyaniline and its derivatives deserve special attention since upon multi-step oxidation they can form radical cations which are very stable at room temperature. For these reasons several attempts to design ferromagnetic organic material were directed towards arylamine compounds. The simplest approach is to synthesize poly(m-aniline) in which radical cations can be generated after the amine groups oxidation. Tanaka et al. have prepared such polymers, either unsubstituted or substituted with alkyl chains with the goal to increase the solubility of the product (Scheme 51, structure 6.43).451,452
 Scheme 51  Poly(m-aniline)s oxidized to radical cations. 
It has been shown that after oxidation with I2 or NOBF4 the polymers contained 10 times as many spins as the pristine materials corresponding to a spin concentration inferior to 0.1 spin per unit. The EPR spectra consisted of the signals at ΔMs = ±1 as well as at ΔMs = ±2 of a forbidden transition. The magnetization measurements data showed that the S = 1 spin state could be obtained and that small clusters of two electronic spins were obtained despite the modest spin concentration. Yamabe and co-workers have performed ab initio MO calculations for a dimer model compound analogue to poly(m-aniline) and suggested that 1,3,5-triazinediyl is a more effective ferromagnetic coupler for poly(m-aniline) than m-benzenediyl.453 The singlet–triplet energy gap in the case of 1,3,5-triazinediyl was estimated to be 12.5 kcal mol−1 (J/k = 6300 K) whereas for 1,3-benzenediyl it was determined to be only 0.7 kcal mol−1 (J/k = 350 K). However, no stable macromolecules containing 1,3,5-triazinediyl as a magnetic coupler have been synthesized to date.
When the SC unit does not contain radicals but radical cations an additional problem should be taken into account, namely the electrostatic repulsion between the adjacent positive charges (holes). Bushby et al. pointed out that the oxidation of poly(m-aniline) is mainly governed by electrostatics.454 The oxidation of every second amine group to a radical cation in the polymer chain is relatively easy. However, in this oxidation state the spin densities do not overlap, thus there is no ferromagnetic interaction. To obtain overlapped spin densities almost all nitrogen atoms should be oxidized to radical cations. This is, in reality, very difficult because of coulombic repulsion between the charges of the same sign. To decrease the electrostatic repulsion one can use 3,4′-biphenyl or 4,4′′-meta-terphenyl as a ferromagnetic coupler instead of 1,3-phenyl. The use of ferromagnetic couplers longer than m-benzene decreases the coulombic repulsion but, at the same time, also decreases the ferromagnetic exchange interaction.454 Bushby and co-workers have synthesized model compounds containing the above-mentioned couplers (Scheme 52, structures 6.44, 6.45).421,455
 Scheme 52  Oligoarylamines containing 3,4′-biphenyl and meta-terphenyl ferromagnetic couplers. 
The oxidation of these compounds was not coulombically limited, but the strength of ferromagnetic interaction decreased sharply and in the case of 6.45 the measured singlet–triplet was only J/k = 42 K.421
An alternative strategy to decrease the electrostatic repulsion and to increase the chemical stability of the formed radical cations is the use the p-phenylenediamine moiety, PD, as a SC unit. It is well known that tetramethyl-p-phenylenediamine (Würster blue) forms one of the most chemically and thermally stable radical cations. Its stability is caused by the electron delocalization in the semiquinone structure. Ito et al. have synthesized and studied a model compound containing two PD units linked by a central m-benzene ring (Scheme 53, structure 6.47).395a
 Scheme 53  Oligoarylamines containing the m-benzene ferromagnetic coupler. 
The EPR spectrum of 6.46 oxidized to the diradical cationic state showed the signals at ΔMs = ±1 and at ΔMs = ±2. The intensity of the signal at half-field demonstrated a linear relationship to the reciprocal temperature indicating the presence of a triplet ground state.395a Similar results were obtained by Janssen et al. for model compounds containing phenyl substituents instead of methyl ones.395b,412 Thus, the ferromagnetic interaction in these model compounds were well established and stimulated subsequent synthesis of macromolecules with alternating meta–para-aniline moieties. Several poly(m–p-anilines) were synthesized using palladium catalyzed amination reactions (Scheme 53, structure 6.47).456–458 The electrochemical oxidation of 6.47 showed reversible waves attributed to the formation of radical cations in the PD units. The CW-EPR spectrum exhibited the signals at ΔMs = ±1 and at ΔMs = ±2 regions.458,459 However, the pulsed-EPR nutation spectrum demonstrated that most of the spins were uncoupled (S = 1/2) and only a small fraction of them formed the triplet state.459 Ito and co-workers have studied a linear oligomer composed of three PD units linked by two m-phenylene rings (Scheme 53, structure 6.48).460 The pulsed-EPR nutation spectrum of 6.48 oxidized to the triradical cationic state revealed only a small signal attributable to the quartet multiplicity (S = 3/2). The difficulties in achieving a high-spin state in linear compounds of this type can be related to conformational changes around the m-benzene couplers impeding the ferromagnetic ordering.
Polyarylamines containing longer than the PD unit conjugated segments have also been prepared (Scheme 54, structures 6.49, 6.50).432,433 The main chains of these polymers are composed of m–p–p-aniline sequences.
 Scheme 54  Polyamines containing m–p–p-aniline segments in the main chain. 
Radical cations formed in aminophenyl-1,4-phenylenediamine units are very stable at room temperature. The pulsed-EPR spectrum of 6.49 oxidized with 1 equivalent of oxidant per polymer repeat unit showed a pure triplet state. Further oxidation led to the creation of spinless dications in the conjugated amine segments and the destruction of the S = 1 state. The exchange coupling constant was J/k = 18 K. Similarly, the highest multiplicity in 6.50 was observed for its oxidation to two diradicals cations per polymer repeat unit. For this oxidation level a quintet spin state S = 2 was unequivocally identified in the EPR measurements, performed at various microwave power values.432 The most interesting feature of poly(m–p–p-aniline) (6.49) was established by magnetization measurements. The magnetization data of diluted solutions of 6.49 can be approximated with S = 1 whereas the experimental data obtained for concentrated solutions can be well fitted using the Brillouin function with S higher than 1. The χT vs. T plot increases strongly between 10 K and 2 K indicating weak intermolecular ferromagnetic interactions. The Curie–Weiss temperature was determined to be Θ = 0.51 K.433
Summarizing the results obtained for 6.49 and 6.50, the principal question should be asked why the majority of the electronic spins are coupled only to S = 1 or S = 2, respectively, with very few, if any, uncoupled spins, as evidenced by pulsed EPR. The first possible reason can be related to incomplete oxidation of the polymer chain. The number of radical cations measured by SQUID magnetometry corresponds to 65% for 6.49 and 74% for 6.50 which imply that some polymer units remain unoxidized, which results in breaking the spin coupling along the chain. The second reason could originate from unfavorable local conformation in some units which, in turn, would lead to a change in the character of the spin interactions from ferromagnetic to antiferromagnetic ones. Finally, the third possible reason is the actual delocalization of the spin density, especially in conjugated oligoaniline segments. If the electronic spins were delocalized on a part of the conjugated segment only, the spin densities of two neighboring units would not effectively overlap, breaking the ferromagnetic interaction. The above mentioned hypotheses should be further investigated with the goal to design macromolecules with increased ferromagnetic ordering along the polymer chain.
Some of the problems outlined above have been solved by the synthesis of polymers containing the phenothiazine moiety linked through 1,3-phenylene, as for example 6.51 and 6.52 (see Scheme 55).461,462
 Scheme 55  Polymers containing the phenothiazine moiety. 
Phenothiazine forms almost planar three fused six-membered rings, thus the rotation in the radical cationic part of the molecule is impeded but the conformation of two adjacent phenothiazine moieties attached to the m-benzene coupler can differ. It should also be mentioned that phenothiazine forms well-known stable radical cations. The spin density calculations performed for model compounds indicate that spins are delocalized and the spin density in the m-phenylene coupler is sufficient to establish ferromagnetic interaction. In view of its rigid structure 6.51 was not soluble in any organic solvents. The introduction of a long alkyl/alkoxy chain into the 1,3-phenylene ring induced, however, the solubility of 6.52 in dichloromethane and THF. The magnetization data obtained by SQUID could be approximated using Brillouin function with S = 1 − 3/2. Although these results do not give simple answer to the question posed above, it seems that the planar and rigid structure promote the ferromagnetic interaction.


6.2.2 Polymers containing spin carrying units in the pendant groups. 
As already pointed out, the second main strategy to obtain high-spin polymers is to graft spin containing units on a conjugated polymer backbone at well-defined positions in order to favor the ferromagnetic coupling of spins according to the topological model (as explained in Section 6.1.1.6). This approach was first proposed by Ovchinnikov in 1977.13,428 This author suggested that grafting of phenoxy radicals on the polyacetylene backbone (structure 6.53) could yield a polymer showing a spin multiplicity S proportional to the degree of polymerization (Scheme 56).
 Scheme 56  The structure of polyacetylene with grafting phenoxy radicals. 
In 1987 he reported the synthesis of an insoluble polymer obtained by thermal and photochemical treatment of diacetylene derivatives bearing nitroxide radical groups463 and exhibiting some ferromagnetism at room temperature whose magnetization was equivalent to a 0.1% fraction in mass of the polymer sample. The exact chemical formula of this polymer could not be firmly established and two structures were proposed (see structures 6.54 and 6.55, Scheme 57).
 Scheme 57  Conjugated polymer containing pendant nitroxide radicals. 
Following this report, several groups tried to reproduce this result and to prepare similar polymers using either pyrolysis or oxidation of conjugated polymers. All these attempts were reviewed in detail by Miller464,465 in 1992. In the main conclusions he stated that in these materials the magnetic properties corresponded to a small fraction of the samples, the chemical structures of the polymers were not clearly established, the reproducibility of the magnetic properties was poor and extrinsic sources of magnetism such as iron oxide could not be discarded, so he concluded that the ferromagnetism of these polymers was not firmly established.
In the 1990's, thanks to the progress in the synthesis of conjugated polymers, several groups started to synthesize polymers with linear conjugated polymer backbones and pendant radical groups using mild conditions of polymerization such as Heck reaction,466 metathesis467,468 or Gilch reaction.469 Such a strategy was reviewed by Nishide in 1995.466 This section will be devoted to the polymers obtained by such mild synthetic processes, starting with selected systems reported in the 1995 Nishide's review and focusing on the most recent reports of high-spin polymers. The four main classes of high spin polymers containing conjugated polymer backbones will be discussed: polyacetylene, poly(phenylenevinylene), polythiophene and poly(aryleneethynylene).
6.2.2.1 Polyacetylene-based high spin polymers. 
In the early studies some representative examples of well characterized polymers containing spin containing units in pendant chains grafted on the polyacetylene backbone were reported by Nishide and coworkers (polymers 6.56a–d on Scheme 58)466,470–473 as well as by other groups (polymers 6.56e and 6.56f).474–476 The non-radical precursor polymers of these polymers (containing X–H precursors of the radical X° moieties) were obtained by polymerization or by head-to-tail condensation of appropriately functionalized phenylacetylenic compounds. The resulting polymers usually exhibited molecular masses above 104 g mol−1 and good solubility. The non-radical precursors could be converted to the corresponding high-spin polymers by oxidation with oxidants such as PbO2 and Ag2O or by electrochemical oxidation. The radical concentration in the polymers, referred to as “spin-concentration” in many reports, in some cases exceeded 0.5 spin per unit (0.8 in the case of the polymer 6.56b).
 Scheme 58  Polyacetylenes containing different pendant radical moieties. 
In all these polymers (but polynitroxide 6.56f), the free radicals are conjugated with the polyacetylene backbone and 6.56a is very close to 6.53 which was predicted by Ovchinnikov as a high-spin polymer with S proportional to the degree of polymerization (vide supra). However, no high-spin magnetic ground state could be observed in these polymers: the electronic spins behaved as isolated spins and even some antiferromagnetic interactions were observed in polymers 6.56d–f. This effect was attributed to the through-space antiferromagnetic coupling of neighboring free radicals.
The absence of ferromagnetic coupling between pendant free radicals was mainly attributed to a non-planar cis–transoid conformation of the polyacetylene backbone, which would be in conflict with the hypothesis of planarity of the conjugated backbone assumed in original theoretical studies by Ovchinnikov. This non-planarity was confirmed by force-field computational studies of poly(phenylacetylene), which revealed that the polyacetylene chain was twisted (with a 75° dihedral angle) and that the phenyl groups are not coplanar with the backbone (with a 35° dihedral angle), which considerably reduces the delocalization of the spin density over the polyacetylene backbone.477 Let us mention here that several other similar polymers exhibiting S = 1/2 or antiferromagnetic behavior were synthesized. In some studies a particular effort was paid to induce chirality by obtaining a helical conformation of the polyacetylene backbone. These reports have been reviewed in ref. 401 and will not be discussed here.
To the best of our knowledge, only four examples of high-spin polymers based on the polyacetylene backbone were reported to date. In 2005 and 2006, Nishide et al. synthesized three polymers with polyacetylene backbones grafted with arylamine radical precursors.467,468 The synthesis was performed by metathesis polymerization catalyzed by rhodium or tungsten catalysts and the precursor polymers were oxidized with NOPF6 or SbCl5 to obtain the desired high spin polymers 6.57 and 6.58 (Scheme 59). Compound 6.58 showed a rather modest degree of polymerization (DP = 7 and 11, respectively). The spin concentration after the oxidation was only 0.25 spin per unit for 6.58 (H) and 0.52 spin per unit for 6.58 (CH3). Nevertheless, SQUID magnetization studies indicated that 6.58 (H) has the properties of an S = 3/2 spin state and 6.58 (CH3) the properties of an S = 2 spin state between 20 and 100 K. At lower temperatures antiferromagnetic interactions were observed, ascribed to through space radical–radical interactions.467
 Scheme 59  Polyacetylenes containing arylamine pendant groups. 
Polyacetylene grafted with dianisylphenyl ammonium radical cations 6.57 could adopt a helical conformation and be obtained as an achiral or as a chiral magnetic polymer depending on the use of achiral or chiral amine as a solvent during the polymerization.468 Remarkably, the spin concentration of the achiral polymer could be as high as 0.98 spin per unit, with a stability of one month in the powder state. Moreover, as revealed by the temperature-dependent SQUID magnetometry and room temperature Evans NMR shift measurements, up to RT the achiral polymer 6.57 exhibited the behavior of an S = 2 spin state while the chiral polymer 6.57 exhibited the behavior of an S = 1 spin state. From these results it was concluded that the through-bond ferromagnetic spin coupling constant J between ammonium radical cations is very high (J/k > 300 K). The lower spin state of the chiral polymer 6.57 was attributed to the fact that a well-ordered helical conformation apparently favors the through space antiferromagnetic interactions between radical cations.
Another remarkable example of a magnetic polymer was reported by Itoh et al.,478 who described polyacetylene grafted with triplet carbene moieties. These authors succeeded in designing the diazo compound 6.59 that was stable enough to perform the polymerization and obtain the precursor polyacetylene-based polymer 6.60 with a high degree of polymerization (DP = 190) (Scheme 60).
 Scheme 60  Polyacetylene grafted with diazo and carbene moieties. 
The photolysis of 6.60 was performed directly at low temperature (T = 2–5 K) inside a SQUID magnetometer due to the limited chemical stability expected for the carbene moieties. The resulting polycarbene exhibited high spin states, whose M = f(H) behavior was between S = 3 and S = 4 Brillouin functions at T = 5 K.
6.2.2.2 Poly(phenylenevinylene)-based high spin polymers. 
Poly(phenylenevinylene)-based high-spin polymers appeared to be good candidates to solve the problem of non-planarity observed with the polyacetylene backbone because of their extended conjugation and rigid coplanarity.466 The first reports on high-spin poly(phenylenevinylene) grafted with nitroxide (6.61, 6.62) and phenoxyl (6.63, 6.64) pendant radicals were published in 1994–1996 (Scheme 61).429,479–481
 Scheme 61  Poly(phenylenevinylene) grafted with nitroxide and phenoxyl groups. 
These polymers were obtained by palladium catalyzed Heck reaction of bromostyrene precursors bearing the phenoxyl moiety protected by an acetoxy group. Then the free radical moieties were generated by oxidation with PbO2, which resulted in spin concentration of up to 0.68 radical per unit in the case of 6.64. 6.61 was unstable due to reactivity of the nitroxide radical with the vinylene proton, but the polymers 6.62–6.64 were stable enough to be studied by SQUID magnetometry. 6.63 and 6.64 had a half-life time of 3–5 hours in solution and 1.5 days as powders at room temperature. 6.62 and 6.63 exhibited similar magnetic properties with the S = 1 high-spin ground state and J/k = 28 K for 6.62 and J/k in the range 28–46 K for 6.63, while 6.64 exhibited an S = 5/2 spin state with an estimated average J/k coupling constant of 69 K.429,481
In an attempt to further improve the magnetic properties of such polymers, the polymer 6.65 containing two phenoxyl radicals grafted on each phenylenevinylene moieties was synthesized (Scheme 62).482 The strategy was to generate an S = 1 ground state diradical on each polymer unit, so that S = 1 diradicals of different polymer units would interact more strongly than S = 1/2 free radicals in the case of polymers 6.63 and 6.64. The synthetic strategy was similar to that used for 6.63 and 6.64. It resulted in a modest polymerization degree of 4, a spin concentration of 0.5 spin per unit and the magnetic behavior of an S = 1 ground state at low temperature. The J/k coupling constant was estimated at 230 K consistent with the coupling of two phenoxyl radicals grafted on the same phenyl ring.
 Scheme 62  Poly(phenylenevinylene) grafted with two phenoxy radicals. 
In 1999–2000 several papers reported on poly(phenylenevinylene) grafted with arylamine radical cations. In addition to their chemical stabilities, ammonium radical cations were known for the large delocalization of the spin density on aryl groups, which was expected to favor the interaction of spins in the polymer backbone.483 The studies of the dimers 6.66 (n = 0 or 1)484 and 6.67485 (Scheme 63) confirmed the validity of this hypothesis, since 6.66 diradical cations up to 100 K exhibited a pure S = 1 ground state as evidenced by EPR spectroscopy. 6.67 had a pure S = 1 ground state with a very strong coupling constant (J/k >300 K) evidenced by SQUID magnetometry.
 Scheme 63  Phenylenevinylene grafted with aryl amine radical cations. 
The poly(phenylenevinylene) backbones grafted with arylamine radical cations 6.68 with t-Bu and OMe substituents corresponding to dimers 6.67 (with t-Bu or OMe) were prepared by Nishide's group. 6.68-t-Bu described in the first report483 showed a spin concentration of 0.41 spin per unit and the spin multiplicity reached 3/2 ≤ S ≤ 2 up to 150 K. In the following paper4866.68-OMe was described which exhibited similar properties with 0.37 spin per unit S ∼ 2 up to room temperature. However, by dispersing 6.68-OMe in a poly(styrenesulfonate tetrabutylammonium) matrix the magnetic properties could be enhanced to 0.63 spin/unit and S = 5/2 persisting at room temperature. The synthesis of poly(phenylenevinylene) grafted with both arylamine radical cations and long aliphatic chains was also reported (Scheme 64, structure 6.69),469 with the aim to improve the solubility and film forming properties. A S = 3/2 spin multiplicity was measured for samples dispersed in poly(styrenesulfonate tetrabutylammonium).
 Scheme 64  Poly(phenylenevinylene) grafted with aryl amine radical cations. 
As in the case of poly(phenylenevinylene) grafted with phenoxyl radicals, the polymer 6.70 was synthesized with the aim of further enhancing the magnetic properties by grafting two arylamine radical cations per unit (Scheme 65).487 This polymer exhibited a very good spin concentration estimated to be 0.8 spin per unit and an S = 5/2 spin state at temperatures up to 100 K. Remarkably 6.70 in its radical cation state was chemically stable at room temperature with a half-life longer than 10 days.
 Scheme 65  Poly(phenylenevinylene) grafted with two aryl amine groups per the polymer unit. 
6.2.2.3 Polythiophene-based high-spin polymers. 
Regioregular, solution processable polythiophene derivatives have been extensively studied since early 1990's as components of field effect transistors and photovoltaic cells since they showed better structural organization and better electrical transport properties as compared to their non-regioregular counterparts (see Section 4). They were also identified as promising candidates for functionalization with spin containing substituents (see ref. 488 and references therein). High-spin regioregular 3-substituted polythiophenes containing phenoxyl and galvinoxyl free radicals were reported by Nishide and coworkers in the 2000–2001 years (Scheme 66).488–490
The polymer 6.71, bearing phenoxyl radicals488 was prepared by oxidative polymerization of the thiophene monomer functionalized with a protected phenolic group followed by its deprotection and subsequent oxidation. It exhibited an S = 1 spin state at low temperatures with a J/k = 104 K spin coupling constant, which is larger than that measured for the corresponding polymer with the poly(phenylenevinylene) backbone. This limited spin multiplicity was attributed to the low efficiency of the oxidation of phenolic moieties to phenoxyl radicals, since the spin concentration was only 0.3 spin per unit. In their second report489 the same group described the preparation of the copolymer 6.72 synthesized by feeding the polymerization reaction with an equimolar ratio of 3-hexyl-thiophene and 3-substituted thiophene with a phenoxyl radical precursor. It also exhibited an S = 1 spin multiplicity at low temperatures with a limited spin concentration of 0.25 spin per unit. Remarkably, the spin coupling constant dropped to J = 18 cm−1. This lower coupling constant was attributed to the longer distance between neighboring phenoxyl radicals in 6.72 compared with 6.71.
 Scheme 66  Polythiophenes grafted with phenoxyl radicals. 
Following these studies, the galvinoxyl radical moiety was chosen as a spin carrying unit with the aim of improving the spin concentration in the polymer.490 In 6.73 the spin concentration of 0.93 spin per unit was obtained. However, no high spin state could be detected: this polymer exhibited the properties of a pure S = 1/2 state as evidenced by EPR and SQUID magnetometer studies with only a weak ferromagnetic interaction at low temperatures. The absence of any high-spin state despite the quantitative generation of free radicals on the pendant chains was attributed to their possible complete localization on the pendant groups without extending it to the polythiophene backbone.
Very recently an elegant strategy for grafting radical anions on regioregular poly(3-hexylthiophene) was reported.396 This polymer was modified in the post-polymerization functionalization process involving the following steps: (i) bromination of poly(3-hexylthiophene) in position 4; (ii) coupling between brominated poly(3-hexylthiophene) and an electron rich alkyne; (iii) cycloaddition of the strong acceptor TCNE (tetracyanoethylene) to the alkyne functions followed by a cycloreversion to obtain 6.77, a polymer containing n-dopable TCBD (1,1,4,4-tetracyanobuta-1,3-diene) moieties (Scheme 67). In the next step 6.77 was either chemically or electrochemically reduced (n-doped) to generate radical anions. The spin concentration in the radical anion of 6.77 was estimated to be 0.17 spin per unit only. Its half-life time was about 26 h and the SQUID magnetometry measurements corresponded to an S = 1 spin multiplicity at low temperature up to T = 50 K.
 Scheme 67  Preparation of polythiophene grafted with 1,1,4,4-tetracyanobuta-1,3-diene. 
6.2.2.4 Poly(aryleneethynylene)-based high spin polymers. 
The expected rigidity and well-defined geometries of poly(phenylene–ethynylene) made oligomers and polymers, based on the phenylene–ethynylene backbone grafted with free radical moieties, good candidates for obtaining high-spin polymers. However, in the majority of cases these polymers and oligomers, when grafted with nitroxides and nitronylnitroxides did not show a high-spin state, but rather antiferromagnetic coupling of the spins (see ref. 401 and 440 and references therein).
Some ferromagnetic coupling was observed in these few examples, for which free radicals were grafted on the 2-positions of the oligo- or poly(1,4-phenyleneethynylene) main chain with the head to tail coupling as for the dimers 6.78491 and 6.79 (n = 0 or 1)484 and the polymer 6.80 (Scheme 68).491 In the case of 6.79 an S = 1 ground spin state at T = 4 K was found by EPR spectroscopy. The dimer 6.78, which was obtained with a 0.69 spin per unit concentration, has also an S = 1 ground state at low temperature with a J/k = 26 K measured by SQUID magnetometry. The polymer 6.80 had only a limited spin concentration of 0.37 spin per unit, which resulted in an S = 3/2 spin multiplicity and J/k = 17 K, compatible with the coupling constant of J/k = 26 K observed for the dimer.
 Scheme 68  1,4-Phenyleneethynylene main chain grafted with phenoxyl radicals and aryl amine radical cations. 
In an elegant improvement of this strategy the poly(1,4-phenyleneethynylene) backbone was replaced by the poly(8,9-anthryleneethynylene) one,392,401,492 for which grafting of two free radicals per unit was possible (Scheme 69). As a result the electronic spins could couple by several pathways. A comprehensive description of these systems is provided in the review by Aoki et al.401 The polymer 6.81 which had the same backbone with one phenoxyl free radical per anthryleneethynylene unit was reported first in ref. 492 showing an S = 1 high-spin ground state, 0.36 spin per unit and J1/k = 56 K. The measured coupling constant was much higher than the J/k = 26 K coupling constant determined for the analogous polymer with the poly(1,4-phenyleneethynylene) backbone. This seems to indicate that the use of the poly(8,9-anthryleneethynylene) backbone probably reduces the steric hindrance between the grafted radicals and the main chain. The best magnetic properties were obtained for the polymer 6.82 which has two phenoxyl radicals per anthryleneethynylene unit, grafted at β-positions. SQUID magnetometry revealed an S = 5/2 ground spin state at low temperatures with a spin concentration of 0.37 spin per phenoxyl unit.392 In the same report the dimer 6.83 was described, showing the coupling constant of two phenoxyl radicals grafted at β-positions on the same anthranyl moieties J2/k = 149 K. So both spin coupling constants J1 and J2 involved in the polymer 6.82 were measured experimentally.
 Scheme 69  Poly(8,9-anthryleneethynylene) and anthranyl grafted with phenoxyl radicals. 

6.3 High-spin macrocyclic compounds
Difficulties encountered in the design and preparation of high-spin linear polymers have been at least partially overcome by employing cyclic structures. There are several advantages of cyclic molecules compared to the linear ones:
(i) a significant reduction of the spin defects influence on the total multiplicity of the system,
(ii) the existence of numerous spin coupling paths which can increase the ferromagnetic interactions,
(iii) the rigid structure of macrocyclic molecules decreasing the number of possible conformations
The creation of unpaired electrons in each spin containing unit, SC, can be difficult and in several cases it is less effective than theoretically predicted. The SC site which does not contain spin can be defined as “a spin defect”. The appearance of this spin defect can interrupt magnetic coupling. Scheme 70 shows the spin defects in branched/linear structure 6.84 and cyclic structures (6.85 and 6.86). The appearance of a spin defect in the branched/linear structure causes a break in the spin–spin connectivity, thus the molecule is divided into several segments bearing lower S values than that theoretical one, predicted for the whole molecule. However, spin defects generated in cyclic molecules have lower influence on the S value than in the previous case. The appearance of a spin defect on peripheral sites (structure 6.85) has a minor effect. Moreover, the existence of a spin defect in one of the spin-coupling paths of the cycle (6.86) does not prevent spin interactions on the remaining coupling paths. High multiplicity of the system is therefore preserved. Thus, the presence of numerous spin-coupling paths in the molecules is very important and enhances the ferromagnetic coupling in cyclic molecules compared to their linear analogues as evidenced by theoretical calculations.426,427,493
 Scheme 70  Spin defects in linear/branched and cyclic structures. 
Iwamura et al. have studied cyclic hexacarbene (6.87, Scheme 71),494 which was obtained from the hexadiazo derivative photolyzed at cryogenic temperature to yield the desired product. The CW-EPR spectrum of 6.87 showed a strong signal centered at g = 2, exhibiting fine structural characteristics of a high-spin state. The magnetization data were well fitted using the Brillouin function with S = 6. Cyclocarbene 6.87 has a rigid structure in which the carbene centers were fixed in non-reactive positions.
 Scheme 71  Hexacarbene. 
Rajca and co-workers have studied macrocyclic polyradicals of triarylmethyl calixarenes (Scheme 72). They prepared the corresponding cyclic polyether precursors which were treated with an excess of Na/K alloy to give carboanion solutions. Carboanions were then oxidized with I2 at 180 K to the corresponding triradical (6.88),495 tetraradical (6.89)495 and its analogues containing a calix[4]arene-type cycle with branched substituents like octaradical (6.90),495 hexadecaradical,496 tetracosaradical497 and 2-strand tetradecaradical (6.91).498 It has been shown that calix[3]arene based triradicals undergo the dimerization, which can be related to an insufficient steric hindrance in its planar system. In the EPR spectrum of 6.88 a signal of relatively low intensity was observed, indicating only a minor amount of a quartet state. The EPR data were qualitatively consistent with a mixture of triplet diradicals. However, in the case of the tetraradical 6.89 and the octaradical 6.90 the EPR spectra showed very intensive signals in both ΔMs = ±1 and ΔMs = ±2 regions, although the EPR spectrum of the octaradical was poorly resolved. Rajca postulated that the main reason for this observation can be attributed to higher defects content and/or many more transitions in the octaradical than in the tetraradical.495 The magnetization data obtained for the tetraradical 6.89 can be well fitted with the Brillouin function with S in the range 1.86–1.93. The experimental data presented as MT vs. T indicated that small intermolecular antiferromagnetic interactions took place at low temperature. However, the S = 2 ground-state was unequivocally established. Similarly, the magnetization data obtained for the octaradical 6.90 can be fitted using the Brillouin function with S = 3.8, which corresponds to 95% yield in the radical formation. Intermolecular antiferromagnetic interactions were negligible for dilute solution of 6.90. Similar results have been obtained for other compounds based on the calix[4]arene cycle. The increase of the number of spin sites by the substitution of branched oligoarylmethyl arms led to an augmentation of the multiplicity of the system. The impressive S = 10 value was detected for tetracosaradical.497
 Scheme 72  Macrocyclic polyradicals of triarylmethyl calixarenes. 
As proved, calix[4]arene based oligoradicals have distorted structure with steric protection which preserves their kinetic stability. Therefore they are interesting as building blocks for high-spin polyradicals, relatively insusceptible to spin defects. Following this strategy Rajca and co-workers have prepared 2-strand tetradecaradical 6.91 (Scheme 72). The arylmethyl bridges help to maintain the ferromagnetic coupling between the spin sites, even in the presence of spin defects in this structure. The magnetization measurements data can be approximated using the Brillouin function with S = 6.2, lower than the theoretically predicted S = 7. This can be explained by the presence of spin defects. The MT vs. T plot showed a drop down at high temperature which suggests the presence of low-lying low-spin excited states. Rajca postulated that the strain in the center calix[4]arene ring may increase basicity which in turn facilitates the formation of defects. Moreover, twisting of arylmethyl bonds in the center calix[4]arene ring could weaken the spin coupling. The magnetic properties were then tentatively rationalized by considering three coupled spins, namely S = 3 (left calix[4]arene ring), S = 1/2 (central part with only one single electron due to a defect) and S = 3 (right calix[4]arene ring). Thus, the formation of a symmetric trimer of S = 3–S = 1/2–S = 3 can be postulated. Cyclic structures of triarylmethyl polyradicals are undoubtedly very interesting and can give a high-spin state, however, using the described preparation procedure it is rather difficult to avoid the creation of spin defects in the process of the oxidation of the formed polyanions to the corresponding polyradicals. In addition they are not chemically stable. The exposure of polyradicals solution to room temperature causes a dramatic destruction of the radical sites.497,498
The defined, rigid structure of calix[4]arene is promising as a building block for other spin containing units like, for example nitroxides. It should be stressed that nitroxides are chemically stable and that the best (and almost unique) example of organic material showing ferromagnetic ordering (at T < 1.48 K in the crystalline phase) is the N,N′-dioxy-l,3,5,7-tetramethyl-2,6-diazaadamantane diradical designed by Rassat.499 There were several attempts to combine calix[4]arene structure with nitroxides. Scheme 73 shows some of them like biradical 6.92,500 tetraradicals 6.93, 6.94501 and 6.95.502
 Scheme 73  Calix[4]arene structure with nitroxides. 
In biradical 6.92 nitroxide groups are attached to the upper calix[4]arene rim. The EPR spectrum of this compound at room temperature shows two sets of overlapping signals at g = 2.0067.500 The character of the spectrum indicates strong spin–spin exchange interaction, supported by the transition for ΔMs = ±2 at half-field. Based on these findings, it was concluded that calix[4]arene maintain partial cone conformation with two N–O˙ groups pointing into the cavity. The two SC units are located in close vicinity, thus resulting in strong spin–spin exchange interaction. In this case spin exchange interaction takes place through space. However, an exchange coupling can also be mediated through bonds and this type of interaction is generally more effective, especially when realized through a cross-conjugated π-system. Rajca et al. have synthesized two kinds of tetraradicals (6.93, 6.94) with nitroxide groups located on the upper rim.501 The EPR spectrum of 6.93 at room temperature shows a well-resolved nonet, whereas the EPR spectrum at 135 K comprises a single peak at ΔMs = ±1 and only a slightly visible peak at the ΔMs = ±2 region. This can be explained by the specific conformation of the cycle in which two nitroxide groups are arranged in parallel on both sides of the molecule. The magnetization measurements showed that the χT vs. T remained constant in the 40–150 K temperature range and fell down at lower temperatures. Rajca concluded that through-bond exchange interaction between adjacent nitroxides was controlled by the conformation of the nitroxide–m-Ph–CH2–m-Ph–nitroxide coupling path. The exchange interaction was antiferromagnetic with J/k ≈ −1 K.501 However, in the case of tetraradical 6.94 with nitroxide groups located on the same side of the calix[4]arene rim, the exchange interaction was ferromagnetic with J/k ≈ +1 K and mostly intramolecular in nature.
The multiplicity of the tetraradical 6.95 has been studied using pulsed-EPR spectroscopy.502 The 2D nutation spectrum demonstrated signals at 2.νS=1/2 and √6.νS=1/2 corresponding to a quintet high-spin state (see Section 6.1.2.3). Additionally, more than three open-shell species contributed to the experimentally measured spectrum, which could be related to a small amount of nitroxide deficient radicals. However, the quintet state was reported as the ground-state or a nearly degenerate state with the singlet-ground state.
Molecules composed of two (or more) aromatic rings linked by aliphatic bridges are called cyclophanes. The replacement of methylene bridges in the structure 6.95 by aza-bridges leads to the formation of tetraazacyclophane, 6.96 (Scheme 74); which shows interesting high-spin properties.
 Scheme 74  Azacyclophanes. 
Spectacular progress in the chemistry of new aza compounds through palladium-catalyzed amination reaction503 has led to the preparation of aza-cyclophanes with reasonable yields. Ito et al. have synthesized tetraazacyclophane 6.96 with the isolated yield of 12.4%.504 By improving the preparation procedure they have synthesized a series of azacyclophanes containing from 3 to 8 aza-bridges.505 Similarly, Rajca and co-workers have obtained N-benzyl substituted aza-meta-cyclophanes composed of 4, 6, 8 and 10 aza-bridges.506 However, the electrochemical studies showed that all these compounds underwent an irreversible oxidation. Radical cations were not stable and participated in an immediate benzidine formation reaction before the polycationic state was established. This irreversible oxidation did not allow for the observation of any high-spin states in all aza-meta-cyclophanes, although they were classified as co-extensive non-Kekulé molecules. Ito et al. suggested that the introduction of suitable substituents into the ortho-position to the amino groups can facilitate the creation of the high-spin state.505 Indeed, the new cyclophane 6.97 (Scheme 74) containing phenylenediamine units, PD, with anisyl substituents exhibited sufficient chemical stability in the oxidized state.507 The radical cation created in phenylenediamine unit was delocalized within N–Ph–N conjugated segments, thus electrostatic repulsion was not so strong as in the case of meta-cyclophanes. Additionally, radical cations were stabilized by the presence of electro-donating anisyl groups. In effect, all oxidation processes of the azacyclophane 6.97 were chemically reversible. The pulsed-EPR studies showed that 6.97 oxidized to tetraradical cations showed the quintet state (S = 2) and was stable under anaerobic conditions at room temperature for several days.507
The usefulness of the PD unit as a spin containing unit was pointed out previously and proved in the case of the structure 6.97. However, there were several attempts to incorporate the phenylenediamine moiety in the main cycle of cyclophanes. Following this idea tetraazacyclophanes 6.98 (Scheme 75) containing two p-phenylenediamine units have been synthesized.508–510
 Scheme 75  Tetraazacyclophanes (6.98a and 6.99), hexaazacyclophane (6.100a) and octaazacyclophanes (6.98b and 6.100b). 
Hartwig and co-workers have improved the reaction conditions and in a two-step procedure have obtained several tetraazacyclophanes with remarkable yields exceeding 70%.508 The voltammograms of the compounds 6.98a exhibited four reversible waves corresponding to the oxidation of four nitrogens present in the molecules.508,509 The CW-EPR spectra of tetraazacyclophanes oxidized to the diradical cationic state showed the signals at g = 1.98 and g = 4.02 for ΔMs = ±1 and ΔMs = ±2, respectively.508 The intensity of the line for ΔMs = ±2 demonstrated a linear dependence with 1/T indicating a triplet-ground state.509a The presence of a triplet state was also confirmed by pulsed-EPR studies which unequivocally proved the existence of S = 1.510 Diradical cations of tetraazacyclophanes were stable for several weeks in solution at −78 °C, but underwent degradation at higher temperature.509a Recently, Sakamaki et al. have prepared a larger macrocycle than tetraazacyclophane, namely octaazacyclophane 6.98b, containing four PD units linked by four 1,3-benzenediyl couplers. Molecules of 6.98b can be reversibly oxidized up to their octacations. However, the pulsed-EPR spectrum of 6.98b oxidized to tetraradical cationic form showed the signals which could be attributed to four different spin states (S = 1/2, 1, 3/2 and 2).509b The different multiplicity observed for this compound can be related to the conformational changes. An increase of the macrocyle size makes it more flexible and facilitates these changes.
Hartwig and co-workers have also prepared polycyclic arylamines such as ladders consisting of two or three cyclophane units.511 Unfortunately, the EPR spectra of oxidized ladder cyclophanes did not exhibit a forbidden transition at half-field, suggesting that the compounds of this type have a singlet-ground state.
Nelsen et al. have synthesized cyclophanes containing two PD units linked by trimethylene bridges512 (6.99, Scheme 75). Surprisingly, the EPR spectrum of the compound containing iso-propyl substituents showed the signals at g = 2 as well as at the half-field transition for ΔMs = ±2 characteristic of the triplet state. Temperature dependent NMR studies revealed that these diradicals have a singlet ground state with a thermally accessible triplet. The authors postulated that the oxidation of two PD units to radical cations caused changes in the molecule conformation and the PD units took up a nearly parallel position. Thus, it could be concluded that in this case the ferromagnetic interaction was realized through-space, similarly to the interaction observed for calix[4]arene with nitroxide groups, as mentioned previously.
To increase the stability of ammonium radical cations, hexaazacyclophane containing prolonged conjugated segment, i.e. two N–Ph–N–Ph–N units, was synthesized (6.100a, Scheme 75).510 Indeed, this compound when oxidized to diradical cations turned out to be very stable even at room temperature. The pulsed-EPR nutation measurements performed for hexaazacyclophane oxidized to its diradical cationic state indicated that spins interacted ferromagnetically and created a pure triplet state.510 The magnetization measurements allow us to determine the exchange coupling constant of 6.100a equal to J/k = 57 K, i.e. 1.7 times bigger than in the case of the corresponding linear dimer (J/k = 33 K).433 Taking into account the chemical stability of these radical cations this entity seems to be very promising as a building block for magnetic materials. Sakamaki and co-workers synthesized octaazacyclophane 6.100b with conjugated amine parts elongated to four nitrogen atoms linked with three phenylene rings.509b This compound can also be reversibly oxidized. The pulsed-EPR studies showed that almost pure triplet state was obtained for 6.100b oxidized to diradical cations, however, further oxidation led to the formation of a spinless, diamagnetic state.
Tetraazacyclophanes have also been used to design more complex structures. Ito and co-workers have prepared compound 6.101 containing three cyclophane moieties bonded by the 1,3,5-benzenetriyl center513 as well as compound 6.102 composed of two cyclophane moieties linked by the p–m–p-oligoaniline bridge (Scheme 76).395e
 Scheme 76  Multicyclophane structures. 
The pulsed-EPR nutation studies of compound 6.101 oxidized to the triradical cationic state clearly indicated the dominance of the spin quartet. However, further oxidation to the tetraradical cationic state caused antiferromagnetic coupling of the newly generated spin to the already existing three spins.513 In the case of compound 6.102 the pulsed-EPR spectra allowed us to detect the septet spin state (S = 3) for the oxidation to hexaradical cations. Furthermore, triplet and quintet states were also present, which suggested that the high-spin state is nearly degenerate with thermally accessible intermediate spin states.395e
Azacyclophanes have also been exploited to construct sophisticated molecular structures. Very recently Ito et al. have synthesized double- and triple-decker molecules which can be regarded as a 1,3,5-benzenetriamine multiple-decker linked by three meta-phenylenes or two locked tetraaza-meta-cyclophanes514 or two locked hexaazacyclophanes containing m–p–p-bonds alternation.441 However, tetraazacyclophanes used as building blocks for a belt-shaped polymacrocyclic oligoarylamine showed the most interesting properties.515 The latter compound is composed of six p-phenylenediamine units forming two cyclophane rings locked by two PD units (6.103, Scheme 77). Electrochemically it could be reversibly oxidized up to dodecacations. By chemical oxidation, using Magic Blue as an oxidant, only the tetraradical cationic state could be reached. The pulsed-EPR spectrum demonstrated the presence of a spin quintet state. Moreover, the excited triplet state was available as well. The study by EPR spectroscopy in liquid solution at different temperatures revealed that the compound containing one radical cation could undergo thermal interconversion between two side slipped structures.
 Scheme 77  Belt-shaped polymacrocyclic oligoarylamine (tetraazacyclophane moiety – in red, PD bridge – in blue). 
In summary, it should be stressed that cyclic structures with different spin bearing units are very promising as building blocks for high-spin or magnetic polymers. One should however be aware of the fact that the properties of these new compounds cannot be simply predicted and sometimes unusual behavior may appear, as recently demonstrated by Ito.441,514,515
6.4 High-spin branched and two-dimensional polymers
In 1968 Mataga proposed a two dimensional (2D) model of polyradicals in which spins can be ferromagnetically aligned (6.104, Scheme 78).516 In this polymeric network each spin of triarylmethyl radicals is ferromagnetically coupled with all spins of six neighboring radicals through 1,3,5-benzenetriyl units.
 Scheme 78  The model of two dimensional polyradicals. 
This two dimensional network allows us to overcome the main difficulties caused by the presence of spin defects. As mentioned before (Section 6.2) in linear polymers spin defects have a severe influence on the multiplicity of the system, breaking magnetic spin–spin interaction (see also Fig. 41). In the case of cyclic structures the spin defects do not affect significantly the multiplicity because spins can interact on two coupling paths (Section 6.3). The Mataga's network contains multiple ferromagnetic coupling paths, thus this model is the most insusceptible to the spin defects (Fig. 41). Moreover, the two-dimensional rigid structure does not allow changing the local conformations and would help to maintain ferromagnetic ordering. However, considering the presence of multiple coupling paths it should be underlined that the spin concentration in the polymer network has to exceed a certain limit value to preserve the ferromagnetic ordering, i.e. the spin concentration should exceed the percolation threshold (ca. 70%). Although the Mataga's polymer has not yet been synthesized to date, there were several attempts directed to design this type of network.
 Fig. 41  Spin defects in linear and 2-D structures. 
Iwamura and co-workers have prepared partially dendritic hexacarbenes (Scheme 79, structures 6.105, 6.106) as well as cyclic structure 6.87 described previously (Chapter 6.3, Scheme 71).494
 Scheme 79  Dendritic hexacarbenes (6.105 and 6.106) and 2D network (6.107). 
These building blocks can be used to construct the polymer network 6.107, similar to the polyradical structure proposed by Mataga.516 The photolysis of hexadiazo precursors led to the creation of the corresponding hexacarbenes. The EPR spectra of both dendritic compounds were similar in the line shape. The signals centered at g = 2 contained characteristic fine structures. The temperature dependence of the spectra followed the Curie–Weiss law in 10–50 K temperature range. The magnetization data could be well fitted using the Brillouin function with S = 6. Thus, the photolysis of precursor compounds proceeded without the generation of low spin impurities. Moreover, it can be concluded that these types of compounds can retain their molecular shape and symmetry.
Rajca and co-workers have studied dendritic triarylmethyl oligoradicals. “Star-branched” topology allows the design and synthesis of hepta- and decaradicals (Scheme 80, structures 6.108, 6.109).517 The EPR spectra showed the presence of S = 7/2 for heptaradicals and S = 5 for decaradicals. The absence of other paramagnetic species indicated that both oligoradicals have high-spin ground states. Following this route different dendritic oligoradicals up to 31-radicals were also prepared.518,519
 Scheme 80  Star-branched dendritic triarylmethyl oligoradicals. 
The EPR spectra of these oligoradicals exhibited a broad single line. The magnetization measurements data indicated the presence of a mixture of spins with different multiplicity and weak antiferromagnetic interaction at low temperatures. The experimental data cannot be approximated using one Brillouin function which is related to the formation of spin defects breaking the whole molecule into several segments possessing different S values.
However, the most impressive results have been obtained for pseudo-2D polymers with triarylmethyl radicals (Scheme 81, structure 6.110).397a,d
 Scheme 81  Triarylmethyl polymers exhibiting S ≈ 5000. 
In this case the polymer chain consists of macrocyclic modulus of S = 2 cross-linked with two bridges of S = 1/2 each. The structure is relatively rigid and “a priori” spins of the bridges should be ferromagnetically coupled to spins of macrocycles, but the twisting of phenyl rings in biphenyl moieties can change the nature of magnetic interaction from ferromagnetic to antiferromagnetic ones. Rajca demonstrated that magnetic properties depend strongly on the molecular mass of the polymer. The magnetization measurements data indicated that for the polymer with Mn = 4 × 104 Da the average S ≥ 17 was obtained, for the polymer with Mn ≥ 105 Da S ≥ 40 was achieved.397a Rajca optimized the preparation procedure and obtained polymers in which the average S values varied from 600 (for a short polymerization time) to 7000.397d The dependence of magnetization showed unusually abrupt increase at low magnetic field at 1.8 K, but the complete saturation was not achieved even at 50 kOe.397d The average S value of ca. 5000 was estimated. The detailed studies revealed that zero field-cooled and the field-cooled magnetizations diverged below 10 K, indicating the slow relaxation (blocking) of the magnetization. The χT vs. T dependence exhibited rapid increase below 10 K. Using these data an effective magnetic moment μeff ≈ 8000 μB was determined. It should be underlined that these magnetic parameters obtained for 6.110 were not exceeded to date.
Considering the stability of the spin sources much attention was paid to polymers containing arylamine groups. Bushby et al. have prepared a series of three dimensional network polymers containing arylamines linked through 3,4′-biphenyl (Scheme 82, structure 6.111) or meta-terphenyl (6.112 and 6.113) couplers.520
 Scheme 82  Polyarylamines containing 3,4′-biphenyl and meta-terphenyl ferromagnetic couplers. 
The electrochemical studies indicated that the polymers can be oxidized to radical cations without the formation of spinless dications. The magnetization measurements showed that the experimental data can be approximated using the Brillouin functions with S = 3/2 for 6.113 and S = 5/2 for 6.112. However, in the case of 6.111 only S = 1/2 was detected. The main problem for all of these polymers was very low oxidation level, insufficient to exceed the percolation threshold. Bushby pointed out two main reasons causing the low radical cation concentration. The first one is the electrostatic repulsion between adjacent positive charges of radical cations, as pointed out in Section 6.2.1. However, in the case of rather large couplers like 3,4′-biphenyl or meta-terphenyl the repulsion cannot limit very much the oxidation process. The second reason is the steric effect of the rigid polymer matrix which is not able to incorporate large counter-ions and this effect seems to limit the efficiency of the doping reaction.
To build up a two-dimensional polymer network it is profitable to use multifunctional ferromagnetic couplers, like for example 1,3,5-benzenetriyl. In this view several attempts have been performed. Star-shaped oligoarylamines containing the 1,3,5-benzenetriyl coupler in the center of a molecule and different aromatic substituents like anisyl,439 4-N,N-dimethylaminophenyl,395c 4-N,N-diphenylaminophenyl,395b 4-N,N-dianisylaminophenyl395d,521 have been synthesized by several groups (typical structure represents 6.114, Scheme 83).
 Scheme 83  Model compounds containing trifunctional ferromagnetic couplers. 
These oligoanilines can be considered as model compounds for suitable two-dimensional polymers. It has been proved that these star-shaped oligomers can be reversibly oxidized to the triradical cationic state. The stability of polycationic species increased with the increase of conjugation in aromatic amine parts. Thus, the oligomer containing 4-N,N-dianisylaminophenyl substituents showed enhanced stability after the oxidation in comparison with the oligomer with 4-N,N-dimethylaminophenyl substituents.395d Moreover, the pulsed-EPR spectra indicated the features characteristic of the quartet spin state. The magnetization measurements revealed that χT values increased gradually with decreasing temperature and reached its maximum at 40 K, then decreased, indicating the intermolecular antiferromagnetic interaction.395d The other trifunctional couplers like s-triazine (structure 6.115)522 or 2,6,10-substituted triphenylene (structure 6.116)523 have also been studied. The AM1-CI calculation performed for s-triazine by Zhang and Baumgarten524 suggested that the singlet–triplet splitting is ca. 23 kcal mol−1 indicating its preference to form a quartet spin state. The EPR spectrum of 6.115 at 100 K showed the features typical of S = 3/2. However, the variable temperature EPR analysis and NMR shift susceptibility measurements showed that 6.115 forms a mixture of quartet and doublet states at room temperature.
Triphenylene was also studied as a ferromagnetic coupler (structure 6.116).523 It forms planar π-conjugated structure. The size of such a coupler reduces the steric hindrance between substituted arylamine groups and, on the other hand, diminishes the electrostatic repulsion between adjacent radical cations. This compound can be reversibly oxidized to triradical cations. The half-life of oxidized 6.116 was 1.2 months at room temperature. The EPR spectrum exhibited the formation of the quartet spin state. The magnetization data could well be fitted using the Brillouin function with S = 3/2 and the spin concentration per amine group 0.9. This molecule is very promising as a building block for 2D polymers.
Taking into account the effectiveness of 1,3,5-benzenetriyl in coupling spins in a ferromagnetic fashion several 2D/3D polyarylamines containing this FC unit have been synthesized. Kulszewicz-Bajer et al. have prepared branched 2D polymers with different lengths of conjugated amine segments, namely the p-phenylenediamine unit (Scheme 84, structure 6.117) and the N,N-diaminophenylamine unit (structure 6.118).458
 Scheme 84  2D/3D polyarylamines with conjugated segments of different length. 
The pulsed-EPR spectra of both polymers oxidized to triradical cations showed the features characteristic of high-spin states.446 The detailed analysis of the spectra revealed the presence of S = 1/2, S = 1, S = 3/2 and S = 2. However, spins with different multiplicity have different relaxation times, so it was not possible to determine the relative concentration of various spins using this method.
