The magnetization measurements data should also be composed of Brillouin functions with S = 1/2, S = 1 and S = 3/2 weighted by the concentration of particular magnetic centers, nevertheless the majority of them have S = 3/2 multiplicity. The χT vs. T exhibited upward deviation with decreasing temperature indicating ferromagnetic coupling with some antiferromagnetic interaction at T ≤ 20 K. Magnetization measurements confirmed the ferromagnetic ordering of spins with an exchange constant J = 0.75 meV estimated for 6.117.446 One can ask a question why in the case of the discussed polymers different high-spins were observed while for star-shaped oligomers (like for example 6.114) no other than quartet spin state was detected. It seems that the presence of spins with different multiplicity in 6.117 and 6.118 can be explained by the influence of local conformation of the chains which change the nature of magnetic interaction.
Michinobu et al. have synthesized networked aromatic polyamines containing 1,3,5-benzenetriyl as well as 1,3-benzenediyl couplers.525,526 The 2D polymer 6.119 (Scheme 85) was prepared by a palladium-catalysed amination reaction of bi- and trifunctionalized monomers.
 Scheme 85  3D polyarylamines containing 1,3-benzenediyl and 1,3,5-benzenetriyl couplers. 
The soluble part of 6.119a had the molecular mass Mw = 76.8 kDa, Mn = 16 kDa whereas 6.119b exhibited the molecular mass Mw = 10 kDa and Mn = 6.5 kDa. The electrochemical studies showed reversible oxidation to polyradical cations without any side reactions. 6.119 can also be oxidized chemically to the polyradical cationic state using NOPF6. The half-life of the polyradical of 6.119a reached 1 week at 20 °C in CH2Cl2/CF3COOH (95/5 vol%) and under a nitrogen atmosphere.525,526 The EPR spectra of 6.119 at room temperature exhibited a broad single line at g = 2.003, but at low temperature the signal at g = 4 at half-field was clearly visible. This last signal showed a fine structure indicating multiple states at least higher than S = 1. The plot of the intensity of the signal vs. temperature showed the upward deviation from a linear Curie dependence indicating the intramolecular ferromagnetic interaction between adjacent radical cations. The magnetization measurements data could be approximated using the Brillouin function with S = 10/2 at low magnetic fields and S = 7/2 at high fields. The spin concentration was estimated to be 0.66 spins per unit for 6.119a and 0.75 spins per unit for 6.119b. The χT values were higher than the theoretical value of 0.375 for S = 1/2 below 180 K. The χT values increased significantly below 50 K for 6.119a and below 100 K for 6.119b, respectively, indicating ferromagnetic coupling between eight or nine spins. Thus, polyradical cations can be generated effectively in 6.119 which assemble spins to form local clusters. The observed multiplicity was the highest, measured to date in networked polytriarylamines.
High-spin hyperbranched polymers containing the poly(1,2-phenylenevinylene) skeleton and the triarylamine (structure 6.120) or tert-butylphenoxyl moiety (structure 6.121) as spin sources have been synthesized by Nishide et al.430,527 (Scheme 86).
 Scheme 86  Poly(1,2-phenylenevinylene) containing arylamine groups or phenoxyl moieties. 
In the case of 6.120 the determined molecular mass of 5.2 kDa corresponds to the degree of polymerization DP = 12.527 The molecular mass of 6.121 was estimated to be 32 kDa.430 The electrochemical studies of 6.120 revealed that the oxidation of amine moieties inside the hyperbranched polymer was kinetically shielded by the outer layer, but the oxidation potential of these amine groups was lower than that of terminal ones. The EPR spectrum of 6.120 oxidized to radical cations showed a strong unimodal signal at g = 2.0028. The half-life of the polyradicals was estimated to be ca. 3 weeks at room temperature. The spin concentration determined by iodometric titration was 0.65 spin per unit. The magnetization measurements indicated a χT value of ca. 0.8 suggesting a high-spin state or a strong spin-exchange interaction. Magnetization was also measured by the NMR shift method using the Evans equation. The multiplicity determined by this method corresponded to S = 7/2, even at 70 °C.527
In the case of 6.121, the precursor polymer was converted to polyphenoxyl radicals and isolated as a brownish green powder. The high-spin state was persistent at room temperature even in air with the half-life equal to ca. 1.2 days.430 The normalized magnetization of 6.121 can be approximated using the Brillouin function with S = 10/2 and the spin concentration 0.42 spin per phenol residue. The latter studies show unequivocally that the extended network can act as an effective π-conjugated coupler for different spin sources.
7 Conclusions
Extremely quick progress has been observed in the synthesis of semiconducting and high spin polymers in the past decade. Through the design of new monomers, elaboration of new polymerization methods, modification of already known ones as well as development of post-polymerization functionalization, it was possible to prepare a large number of solution processable electronic and high spin macromolecular compounds, showing highly regular chain microstructure and high molecular mass of reduced polydispersity. In parallel, significantly more attention has been paid to the elaboration of solution processing techniques, which resulted in the fabrication of supramolecularly ordered, oriented thin films. Better elucidation of the peculiar properties of semiconducting and high-spin polymers has been made possible through application of complementary and, in some cases, hyphenated characterization methods. Taking into account a large number of semiconducting polymers, it must be however stated that only a small fraction of them attained technological maturity, combining operating stability with good parameters of the fabricated devices. High spin polymers are much less developed and are still in the emerging state, comparable to that of semiconducting polymers twenty years ago. Further synthetic efforts are required to obtain stable radical or radical ion forms of these polymers which would exhibit strong spin interactions. Finally, further standardization of the principal characterization techniques is needed. Electrochemical determination of IP and EA is an instructive example of the problem where different calculation assumptions may lead to different values of these parameters for identical electrochemical data. Thus, without appropriate standardization, in all comparative studies the original, “raw” data have to be consulted.

Table Content:

Piotr Bujak
	Piotr Bujak got his MSc in chemistry from Silesian University of Technology in 2004. In 2008, at the University of Silesia he defended his PhD thesis devoted to the NMR investigation of copolymers. Then he started working at the Department of Inorganic and Coordination Chemistry (University of Silesia), where he developed an effective method for the synthesis of new isoxazolines. In 2012 he accepted the position of a research associate at Warsaw University of Technology, joining the group of Professor Adam Pron, where he presently works on the preparation of electroactive hybrids consisting of inorganic semiconductor nanocrystals functionalized with organic semiconductors.

Irena Kulszewicz-Bajer
	Irena Kulszewicz-Bajer obtained her PhD in chemistry from Warsaw University of Technology in 1984. She spent one year in the Laboratory of Inorganic Chemistry at the University of Nancy I in France at the position of “maitre de conference” in 1985. Then she returned to Warsaw University of Technology, at which she has been working till now. In 1991 she worked in Uniax Corporation in Santa Barbara (USA), collaborating with Alan J. Heeger and Paul Smith. She became full professor in 2009. Her research interests involve the synthesis and spectroscopic characterization of π-conjugated materials and high-spin organic compounds.

Malgorzata Zagorska
	Malgorzata Zagorska, a University of Warsaw graduate, obtained her PhD in chemistry from Warsaw University of Technology in 1987. Then she moved to Royal Institute of Technology in Stockholm (Sweden) where she worked as postdoctoral fellow under the supervision of Goran Ahlgren and Bernd Krische. In 1992–1994 she worked in UNIAX Corporation in Santa Barbara (USA), closely collaborating with Alan J. Heeger. In 1994 she returned to Warsaw University of Technology where she became full professor in 2005. Her research interests involve the synthesis, electrochemistry and spectroelectrochemistry of organic semiconductors as well as their application in organic electronics.

Vincent Maurel
	Vincent Maurel obtained his PhD in 2004 from Joseph Fourier University in Grenoble (France) working under the supervision of Serge Gambarelli and Jacques Gaillard. Then he moved to Canada working as a postdoctoral fellow in the team of Professor Tito Scaiano at the University of Ottawa. In 2006 he joined the University of Strasbourg where worked under the supervision of Philippe Turek. Since 2007, he has been working as a researcher at Atomic Energy Commission (CEA) in Grenoble. His research interests involve advanced EPR spectroscopy, molecular magnetism and photocatalysis.

Ireneusz Wielgus
	Ireneusz Wielgus was born in 1969 in Warsaw (Poland). In 1993 he obtained his MSc degree in chemistry and chemical engineering from Warsaw University of Technology. In 2000 he defended his PhD thesis at the same university, devoted to conducting polymers and carried out under the supervision of Professor Malgorzata Zagorska. In 2003 he obtained the position of an assistant professor. His research interests involve the synthesis and characterization of specialty polymers.

Adam Pron
	Adam Pron was born (1951) and educated in Poland. After obtaining his MSc in chemistry he moved to the USA where in 1980 he completed his PhD at the University of Pennsylvania (under the supervision of Alan G. MacDiarmid). Then he joined the Warsaw University of Technology where he became full professor in 1993. In 1998 he moved to Atomic Energy Commission (CEA) in Grenoble (France). In 2012 he retired from CEA and accepted full-time professorship at Warsaw University of Technology. His research interests involve preparation and characterization of organic electroactive materials and hybrid organic–inorganic nanomaterials.

 	Fig. 37  Determination of J in oxidatively doped S = 1 arylamine dimer from the plot of χT = f(T). [reproduced by permission from R. J. Bushby, N. Taylor and R. A. Williams, J. Mater. Chem., 2007, 17, 955–964].

Kulszewicz-Bajer, 2013, unpublished data].

Footnote
† This partial oxidation reaction is frequently termed “doping” by the community of solid state physicists since it induces profound changes in the physical properties of electronic or magnetic organic compounds, phenomenologically similar to the effect of doping in inorganic semiconductors.
